The Particle 'Hatta': Ghaya and 'Atf in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing the particle 'hatta' as ghaya and 'atf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two primary meanings of 'hatta' (ghaya and 'atf) and apply contextual clues to determine which meaning is intended in a given legal text.

Core Idea
Hatta either limits a ruling (ghaya) or expands it ('atf) – context decides.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi’s Kanz al-Wusul examines the Arabic particle ‘hatta’, a key tool in istinbat (derivation). Understanding its dual nature is critical for correctly interpreting legal texts, especially in oaths and conditionals. We will learn to distinguish between its two meanings using contextual examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle
Locate 'hatta' in the text under analysis. Note its position and the words it connects.
2
Determine context
Analyze the surrounding words and overall sentence structure to decide whether 'hatta' indicates an endpoint (ghaya) or inclusion ('atf). Look for clues like a preceding verb of duration or a list of items.
If the sentence implies a time limit or goal, ghaya is likely. If it adds another element to a previous category, 'atf is likely.
3
Apply the meaning
Translate the ruling accordingly: ghaya sets a limit beyond which the ruling ends; 'atf includes the following item in the same ruling.
In legal texts, this affects obligations, prohibitions, and conditions.
4
Check for ambiguity
Some contexts may allow both meanings. Use qara'in (additional evidence) such as customary usage, other texts, or scholarly consensus to resolve.
Hanafi usul gives priority to ghaya unless strong evidence supports 'atf.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion on the Arabic particle 'hatta' (حتى) and its two primary legal-linguistic meanings: ghaya (end, limit) and 'atf (conjunction, inclusion). The text examines the conditions under which 'hatta' conveys each meaning, with examples from Arabic speech and potential legal implications. For instance, in the phrase 'I will not leave you until he judges' (لا أفارقك حتى يقضي), 'hatta' indicates the endpoint (ghaya) of the action of leaving. In other contexts, 'hatta' can mean 'also' or 'even' (عطف), adding something. The author may be quoting from Hanafi usul works that discuss how these meanings affect interpretation of legal texts, such as in oaths or conditions. The discussion likely stems from the chapter on 'umum and khusus (general and specific) or on huruf al-ma'ani (particles of meaning). Although the OCR is corrupt, the topic is recognizable: 'hatta' is a key particle in usul al-fiqh because it can delimit a ruling (ghaya) or expand it ('atf). Hanafi usul scholars meticulously analyze such particles to ensure correct derivation of rulings.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): A particle that primarily indicates ghaya (end, limit) or 'atf (conjunction, inclusion) depending on context.
الغاية (al-ghaya): The end or limit of an action or time; in legal language, it specifies the termination point of a ruling.
العطف (al-'atf): Grammatical conjunction; in this context, it means 'including' or 'even,' adding another element to a previous ruling.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'hatta' in Arabic legal texts can indicate either a limit (ghaya) or inclusion ('atf), and correct interpretation depends on careful analysis of context and qira'in.

The Particle Hatta: Ghaya vs Atf
Linguistic analysis of the particle 'hatta'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the ghaya (limit) and atf (conjunction) meanings of 'hatta' and apply the correct interpretation in a legal context.

Core Idea
The particle 'hatta' can mean 'until' (ghaya) or 'including' (atf) – a distinction that changes legal rulings.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh examines the Arabic particle 'hatta'. Its dual meaning—as a limit (ghaya) or conjunction (atf)—has direct implications for legal interpretation. Mastering this distinction is crucial for accurate istinbat (derivation of rulings) because the same word can produce different rulings depending on context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its context
Locate the word 'hatta' in the legal text and note the words before and after it. Determine whether the sentence describes a sequence, a condition, or an inclusive listing.
Context is key – the same particle can have different functions.
2
Determine ghaya vs atf
Decide whether 'hatta' indicates the end point of an action (ghaya) or whether it joins something to the previous statement (atf). For example, 'I ate the fish, even its head' (atf) vs 'I fasted until sunset' (ghaya).
In the example 'hatta ra'saha', both readings are plausible.
3
Apply the Hanafi default principle
The Hanafi usul holds that the literal meaning (haqiqah) is presumed. If both meanings are possible, the default is ghaya unless context indicates atf. Seek corroborating evidence (qarinah) from the text or speaker's intent.
This follows the maxim: al-asl fi al-kalam al-haqiqah.
4
Derive the ruling
Based on the chosen meaning, derive the legal consequence. If ghaya, the ruling applies only up to the limit; if atf, the ruling includes the joined item.
Example: 'I will not speak to you until you leave' – if ghaya, silence ends when you leave; if atf, silence includes the leaving action.

And the future over the past so its limit fell and time does not bear it and or the goal is true or conjunction is obligatory. Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul is a textual analysis of the Arabic particle 'hatta' (حتى). The author discusses its two primary meanings: (1) ghaya (غاية) meaning 'up to' or 'until' indicating a limit or endpoint, and (2) atf (عطف) meaning 'even' or 'including' functioning as a conjunction. The text gives examples from Arabic speech, such as 'I ate the fish, even its head' (اكلت السمكة حتى رأسها), where 'hatta' can be interpreted either as 'including' (atf) or 'up to' (ghaya). This distinction matters in legal contexts: for instance, if someone says 'I will not speak to you until you leave' (hatta takhruj), the ruling depends on whether 'hatta' indicates a condition (ghaya) or mere conjunction. The author notes that the correct application requires understanding the speaker's intent. The passage also references the Qur'anic usage (though garbled) and the principle that when the meaning is ambiguous, the default is the literal meaning (haqiqah). Due to severe OCR corruption, the exact arguments are obscured, but the core subject is the linguistic analysis of 'hatta' and its legal implications.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): A particle that can indicate ghaya (goal/limit) or atf (conjunction).
غاية (ghaya): The endpoint or limit; 'until' or 'up to'.
عطف (atf): Conjunction; 'even' or 'including'.

Deriving a Ruling for a Muhrim's Necessity Using Qiyas Khafi
Derivation of a ruling for a muhrim's necessity concession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of qiyas khafi to derive a ruling when a general prohibition is overridden by necessity, and construct the full istinbat chain.

Core Idea
A muhrim may eat prohibited food under necessity, but must still pay the penalty — the general prohibition is lifted but liability remains.

This passage illustrates how Hanafi usul reconciles a general prohibition (al-amm) with a specific necessity (darurah). The case of a muhrim in the Haram forced to consume a prohibited item is used to demonstrate qiyas khafi and istihsan. We will dissect the derivation chain from the general rule to the final concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule (amm)
Determine the base prohibition: a muhrim is generally prohibited from certain actions (makhzur al-ihram). This is the asl in the qiyas.
2
Recognize the exceptional case (far')
The specific scenario: a muhrim forced by necessity (darurah) to consume such a prohibited item within the Haram.
3
Determine the illa (effective cause)
Necessity (darurah) is the illa that justifies the concession. In Hanafi usul, darurah can override a general prohibition.
The illa is extracted from the context of the Haram and extreme need.
4
Apply the ruling via qiyas khafi
Through hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), the ruling is that the act is permitted but the original penalty (jaza') remains. This is not strict qiyas (which would forbid absolutely) but istihsan.
Qiyas khafi is used because the analogy is not obvious; it relies on subtle reasoning.
5
Confirm the role of istihsan
Istihsan (juristic preference) allows the concession despite the general prohibition. The penalty is retained to maintain the sanctity of the ihram rules.
This shows how Hanafi usul balances textual commands with practical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امر في قة وجب العمل فيه باثبات الرجفة والعمل به وجب با صبار یا رہنما اصله عزيمته و هذا لكن اصابة تحفتر حلاله ناول طعام غير معمته يا اباحة مطلقة حتى اذا تركنا در في كان شهيد ا ختلاف العام نفسه وان است نامه من الكون معقول في نفر و ذلك مثل تناول مخطور الإحرام من ضرورة بال بالحرم انه يرخص له ويضم الجزأ فكذلك همنا والله اعلم بالصواب في نحت عام
A matter in which it is obligatory to act by establishing the [؟] and acting upon it became obligatory. [؟] its origin is determination and this [؟] but [؟] his permission [؟] he took food not [؟] as absolute permission until if we left [؟] there was a martyr? Difference of the general itself and if [؟] it from the universe is reasonable in [؟] and that is like the taking of a prohibited thing by a muhrim out of necessity in the Haram, that he is permitted to it and he pays the penalty. So similarly our [؟], and Allah knows best what is correct in the carving of the general.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage discusses a legal ruling derived by analogy (qiyas). The author presents a case where a person in the state of ihram (muhrim) is forced by necessity to consume something that is normally prohibited (makhzur) within the Haram. The ruling is that he is permitted to eat it due to the necessity (rukhsah), but he must also pay the penalty (jazā') for violating the prohibition. This is similar to the principle that general prohibitions (al-amm) can be subject to exceptions due to necessity. The author uses this example to illustrate how a general rule ('amm) may be qualified by a specific necessity, and how the derived ruling aligns with qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) based on istihsan. The text ends with the author seeking Allah's guidance in understanding the correct application of general principles.

Key Terms
مخطور الإحرام (makhzur al-ihram): Prohibited actions/items during the state of ihram.
الجزأ (al-jaza'): Compensation or penalty for violating ihram prohibitions

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is permissible only if he performs a sacrifice.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a muhrim to violate an ihram prohibition under necessity but maintains the penalty (jaza), illustrating the interplay of amm, darurah, and istihsan.

Definition of Khass (Specific) in Usul al-Fiqh
Defining the term 'khass' linguistically and technically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'khass' in Hanafi usul and explain its linguistic roots and technical application.

Core Idea
Khass is a word for a single meaning exclusively — it cuts off all sharing.

This lesson introduces the term 'khass' (specific), a foundational concept in usul al-fiqh, especially for understanding how general texts are specified (takhsis). The passage defines it both linguistically and technically, tracing its Arabic roots to the idea of isolation from wealth and partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the sentence that defines khass technically: 'a word placed for one meaning in isolation and cutting off sharing'. Note the two key elements: singularity and exclusivity.
The definition is both positive (one meaning) and negative (no sharing).
2
Trace the linguistic origin
Find the root word 'khasasah' and its connection to poverty and isolation. Understand how the term evolved to mean 'specific'.
The author uses lexical derivation to ground the technical term.
3
Examine the example given
Consider the example 'insan' (human) when used to refer to the human species exclusively. Understand how this illustrates khass within a genus.
This example shows that khass can be a generic term, not only a proper noun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الخاص : فكل لفظ وضع لمعنى واحدٍ على الانفراد، وانقطاع المشاركة، وكلّ اسم وضع لمسمى معلوم على الانفراد. وهو مأخوذ من قولهم : اختص فلان بكذا: أي انفرد به وفلانٌ خاص فلان أي منفرد به. والخَصَاصَةُ : اسم للحاجة الموجبة للانفراد عن نيل المال، ولذلك سُمِّي الفقر : خصاصة. المال، وعن أسباب فصار الخصوص عبارةً عما يوجب الانفراد، ويقطع الشركة. فإذا أُريد به خصوص الجنس : قيل : إنسان؛ لأنه خاص من بين سائر
As for the specific (khass): it is every word placed for one meaning in isolation and cutting off sharing, and every name placed for a known named entity in isolation. It is derived from their saying: 'So-and-so was singled out for such-and-such,' meaning he was alone in it, and 'so-and-so is the specific of so-and-so' meaning he is unique to him. And 'khasasah' is a name for the need that necessitates isolation from obtaining wealth, and therefore poverty is called 'khasasah'. ... So 'khussus' became an expression for what necessitates isolation and cuts off partnership. So if one intends the specific of a genus, one says: 'insan' (human), because it is specific among the rest...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of 'khass' (specific) in usul al-fiqh. Technically, it is a term that signifies a single meaning exclusively, without sharing; for example, a proper name or a word for a specific genus. The author traces the linguistic origin: the root means isolation or uniqueness. 'Khasasah' refers to the need that forces one to be alone, hence poverty is called 'khasasah' because it isolates one from wealth. The term 'khussus' thus denotes what requires isolation and cuts off partnership. The example given is 'insan' (human) when one intends the specific genus of mankind, as it is specific among other genera. This definition is foundational for understanding the categories of 'amm' (general) and 'khass' in Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): A word that signifies one specific meaning in isolation, without sharing.
الانفراد (al-infiraad): Isolation or uniqueness; the characteristic of khass.
المشاركة (al-musharakah): Sharing; the opposite of khass.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It comes from 'khasam' meaning dispute; it relates to cutting off argument.
B) It comes from 'khasasah' meaning poverty; it relates to isolation from sharing.
C) It comes from 'khassah' meaning special; it relates to privilege.
D) It comes from 'khata' meaning error; it relates to avoiding mistakes.

Key Takeaway
The definition of khass as a word for a single meaning in isolation is fundamental for understanding how Islamic legal texts are precisely interpreted.

Defining Khas and Aam in Hanafi Usul
Defining specific and general terms with linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khas and aam according to Hanafi usul, distinguish aam from mushtarak, and explain why al-Jassas's definition is problematic.

Core Idea
A general word encompasses multiple individuals uniformly; a homonym has multiple unrelated meanings—they are not the same.

In this passage, the author lays the foundational definitions of two key terms in Hanafi usul: khas (specific) and aam (general). You will learn not only their linguistic roots but also the precise technical boundaries that separate a general term from a homonym (mushtarak). The critique of al-Jassas's definition sharpens this distinction, an essential skill for correctly interpreting legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic root of khas
Identify the derivation from 'ikhtisas' (exclusivity) and the three levels: genus, type, individual. This shows how specificity operates at different layers.
The three levels are 'insan' (genus), 'rajul' (type), 'Zayd' (individual).
2
Define aam and its two modes
Aam encompasses a collection of nouns either in wording (e.g., plurals) or in meaning (e.g., 'man', 'ma'). Understand that 'nouns' here refers to named entities.
Example of wording: 'Zaydoon'; example of meaning: the particle 'man' which includes all persons.
3
Contrast aam with mushtarak
Mushtarak has multiple distinct meanings taken separately; it has no generality. Aam applies uniformly to all individuals or instances of a single meaning.
The word 'ayn is mushtarak (eye, spring, spy); the word 'al-muslimun' is aam for all Muslims.
4
Critique al-Jassas's definition
Al-Jassas said aam encompasses 'names or meanings'. The author argues 'meanings' cannot be multiple under one word; if they are distinct, the word is mushtarak. A possible metaphorical interpretation is that a single meaning with multiple loci is called 'meanings' metaphorically.
This correction maintains conceptual clarity—a general term cannot simultaneously have multiple unrelated meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الخاص : فكل لفظ وضع لمعنى واحدٍ على الانفراد، وانقطاع المشاركة، وكلّ اسم وضع لمسمى معلوم على الانفراد. وهو مأخوذ من قولهم : اختص فلان بكذا: أي انفرد به وفلانٌ خاص فلان أي منفرد به. والخَصَاصَةُ : اسم للحاجة الموجبة للانفراد عن نيل المال، ولذلك سُمِّي الفقر : خصاصة. فصار الخصوص عبارةً عما يوجب الانفراد، ويقطع الشركة. فإذا أُريد به خصوص الجنس : قيل : إنسان؛ لأنه خاص من بين سائر الأجناس، وإذا أريد به خصوص النوع : قيل: رجل؛ لأنه خاص من بين سائر الأنواع. وإذا أريد به خصوص العين قيل : زيد وعمرو، فهذا بيان اللغة والمعنى.
ثم العام بعده، وهو: كلُّ لفظ ينتظم جمعاً من الأسماء، لفظاً أو معنى. ومعنى قولنا : الأسماء : يعني من المسميات هنا. ومعنى قولنا : لفظاً أو معنى : هو تفسير الانتظام، يعني أن ذلك اللفظ إنما ينتظم الأسماء مرةً لفظاً، مثل قولنا : زيدون، ونحوه، ومرة معنى، مثل قولنا : من، وما، ونحوهما.
والعموم في اللغة: هو الشمول، يقال: مطر عام: أي شمل الأمكنة، وخِصْبٌ عام: أي عم الأعيان، ووسع البلاد، ونخلة عميمة: أي طويلة، والقرابة إذا توسعت انتهت إلى صفة العمومة.
وهو كالشيء : اسم عام يتناول كل موجود عندنا، ولا يتناول المعدوم، خلافاً للمعتزلة، وإن كان كل موجود ينفرد باسمه الخاص. وقد ذكر الجصاص أن العام : ما ينتظم جمعاً من الأسماء أو المعاني. وقوله : أو المعاني : هذا سهو منه، أو مؤول؛ لأن المعاني لا تتعدد إلا عند اختلافها وتغايرها، وعند اختلافها وتغايرها لا ينتظمها لفظ واحد، بل يحتمل كل واحد منها على الانفراد، وهذا يسمى : مشتركاً. وقد ذَكَرَ بعد هذا أن المشترك لا عموم له، فثبت أنه سهو أو مؤوَّلٌ. وتأويله : أن المعنى الواحد لما تعدد محله سمي معاني؛ مجازاً؛
As for the specific (al-khas): every word placed for a single meaning in isolation, with cessation of participation, and every name placed for a known named entity in isolation. It is derived from their saying: 'so-and-so was specified with such-and-such', meaning he was alone with it; and 'so-and-so is the specific of so-and-so', meaning exclusive to him. And 'al-khasasah' is a name for the need that necessitates isolation from obtaining wealth, and for that reason poverty is called 'khasasah'. Thus specificity (al-khusus) became an expression for that which necessitates isolation and cuts off partnership. If specificity of genus is intended, one says 'insan' (human), because it is specific among all genera. If specificity of type is intended, one says 'rajul' (man), because it is specific among all types. If specificity of individual is intended, one says 'Zayd' and 'Amr'. This is the explanation of language and meaning. Then general (al-amm) after it, and it is: every word that encompasses a collection of nouns, either in wording or in meaning. And the meaning of our saying 'nouns' (al-asma) is that it refers to the named entities here. And the meaning of our saying 'in wording or in meaning' is the interpretation of 'encompassing': that the word encompasses nouns sometimes in wording, like our saying 'Zaydoon' and the like, and sometimes in meaning, like our saying 'man' and 'ma' and the like. And generality (al-umum) in language is comprehensiveness. It is said: 'general rain' meaning it covered places, and 'general prosperity' meaning it encompassed individuals and spread across lands, and 'a tall palm tree' (nakhlah amimah) meaning tall, and kinship when expanded reaches the quality of generality (umumah). And it (the general) is like 'al-shay' (thing): a general name that includes every existent according to us, and does not include the non-existent, contrary to the Mu'tazila, although every existent is distinguished by its specific name. And al-Jassas mentioned that the general is what encompasses a collection of nouns or meanings. And his statement 'or meanings' is an oversight from him, or it is interpretable; because meanings do not become multiple except when they differ and are distinct, and when they differ and are distinct, a single word does not encompass them; rather each one of them is taken in isolation, and this is called 'mushtarak' (homonym). And he mentioned after this that the homonym has no generality, so it is established that it is an oversight or interpretable. And its interpretation: that the single meaning, when its locus becomes multiple, is called 'meanings' metaphorically.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage provides detailed definitions of two fundamental concepts in Hanafi usul: 'khas' (specific) and 'aam' (general). The author begins with 'khas', tracing its linguistic roots to isolation and exclusivity. He distinguishes three levels of specificity: genus (e.g., insan), type (e.g., rajul), and individual (e.g., Zayd). This linguistic analysis forms the basis for legal hermeneutics. For 'aam', the author gives the technical definition: a word that encompasses a collection of nouns either in wording (like plurals) or in meaning (like particles 'man' and 'ma'). He also discusses the linguistic usage of 'umum' and the example of 'shay' (thing). A critical part of this passage is the author's refutation of al-Jassas's definition that includes 'meanings' within the scope of 'aam'. The author argues that if meanings are different, a single word cannot encompass them; rather, such a word would be mushtarak (homonym). This correction is crucial because it maintains the distinction between general terms (which apply uniformly to multiple individuals) and ambiguous terms (which have multiple distinct meanings). The author suggests that al-Jassas's inclusion of meanings may be metaphorical or an oversight. This discussion highlights the precision required in usul terminology within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
خاص (khāṣṣ): A word placed for a single meaning in isolation, with cessation of partnership; it can be specific to genus, type, or individual.
عام (ʿāmm): Every word that encompasses a collection of nouns (individuals) either in wording (like plurals) or in meaning (like 'man', 'ma'). Not encompassing multiple distinct meanings.
مشترك (mushtarak): A word that has multiple distinct meanings, each taken in isolation; it has no generality (la umum lahu).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject al-Jassas's inclusion of 'meanings' in the definition of aam?
A) Because 'meanings' cannot be multiple
B) Because the word 'aam' is only for names, not meanings
C) Because if meanings are distinct, a single word cannot encompass them; that would be mushtarak
D) Because it contradicts the Arabic language

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a general term (aam) applies uniformly to multiple individuals of a single meaning, while a homonym (mushtarak) has multiple distinct meanings and no generality.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Definitions of General, Homonym, Ambiguous, and Interpreted
Defines 'am, mushak, mujmal, mu'awwal with linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-'am, al-mushtarak, al-mujmal, and al-mu'awwal by correctly applying their definitions to new examples.

Core Idea
General words encompass all members; homonyms require preponderance; ambiguous needs clarification; interpreted has been resolved.

In this foundational passage from Bazdawi's Usul, you will be introduced to four key categories that classify the clarity of scriptural texts. Understanding these terms is essential for any student of Hanafi usul, as they directly impact how legal rulings are derived from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four terms
Locate and list the four Arabic terms defined: العا, المشترك, المجمل, المؤول.
2
Analyze the definition of al-'am
Examine the definition: 'every word that encompasses a collection of nouns, either in wording or in meaning.' Understand the linguistic meaning of 'umum as comprehensiveness, and note the critique of al-Jassas's inclusion of 'meanings'.
The word 'shay'' (thing) is an example of a general word covering all existents.
3
Analyze the definition of al-mushtarak
A homonym is a word with multiple possible meanings, where only one is intended. Examples: 'ayn, mawla, qur'. Note that it has no generality.
Mushtarak is derived from 'sharing'—the word shares multiple meanings.
4
Distinguish mujmal from mushtarak
The key difference: mushtarak can be understood by linguistic reflection and preponderance (tarjih), while mujmal cannot due to a legal addition or closed door of preponderance. Mujmal requires external clarification (bayan).
5
Understand al-mu'awwal
Al-mu'awwal is a mushtarak that has been resolved by predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y). It is the outcome of ta'wil (interpretation).
The Quranic verse about 'ta'wil' in 7:53 is cited to illustrate the meaning of 'outcome'.

و الأجناس، وإذا أريد به خصوص النوع : قيل: رجل؛ لأنه خاص من بين سائر الأنواع. وإذا أريد به خصوص العين قيل : زيد وعمرو، فهذا بيان اللغة والمعنى. ثم العام بعده، وهو: كلُّ لفظ ينتظم جمعاً الأسماء، لفظاً له، فثبت أنه سهو أو مؤوَّلٌ. وتأويله : أن المعنى الواحد لما تعدد محله سمي معاني؛ مجازاً؛ لتعدد محاله، لكن كان ينبغي أن يقول : والمعاني، والصحيح أنه سهو. وأما المشترك فكل لفظ احتمل معنى من المعاني المختلفة، أو الأسماء على اختلاف المعاني، على وجه لا يثبت إلا واحد من الجملة مراد به. مثل : العين : اسم لعين الناظر، وعين الشمس، وعين الميزان، وعين الركبة، وعين الماء، وغير ذلك. ومثل : المولى، والقُرء من الأسماء المشتركة عند أهل اللغة. وهو مأخوذ من الاشتراك، ولا عموم لهذا اللفظ، وهو مثل: الصريم: هو اسم لليل والصبح جميعاً على الاحتمال، لا على العموم. وهذا يُفارق المُجمَل ؛ لأن المشترك يحتمل الإدراك بالتأمل في معنى لغةً، برجحان بعض الوجوه على البعض، فقبل ظهور الرجحان الكلام سمي : مشتركاً. فأما المُجمل : فما لا يُدرك لغةً؛ لمعنى زائد ثبت شرعاً، أو لانسداد باب الترجيح لغةً، فوجب الرجوع فيه إلى بيان المجمل، على ما نبين في موضعه إن شاء الله تعالى. وأما المؤول: فما ترجّح من المشترك بعض وجوهه بغالب الرأي، مأخوذ من : آل، يؤول : إذا رجع ، وأوَّلته: إذا رجعته وصرفته؛ لأنك لما تأملت في موضع اللفظ ، وصرفت اللفظ إلى بعض المعاني خاصة : فقد أولته إليه وصار ذلك عاقبة الاحتمال بواسطة الرأي. قال الله تعالى: ﴿هَلْ يَنظُرُونَ إِلَّا تَأْوِيلَهُ الأعراف/٥٣ : أي عاقبته. وليس هذا كالمُجمَل إذا عُرفت بعض وجوهه ببيان المجمل، فإنه
And the genera, and if what is intended is the specificity of the type, it is said: 'man' (رجل) because it is specific among the rest of the types. And if what is intended is the specificity of the individual, it is said: Zayd and Amr. This is the linguistic explanation and the meaning. Then the general (العام) comes after it, and it is: every word that encompasses a collection of nouns, in wording or in meaning. And the meaning of our saying 'the nouns' means 'the named entities' here. And the meaning of our saying 'in wording or in meaning' is the interpretation of 'encompassing', meaning that that word encompasses the nouns sometimes in wording, like our saying 'Zaidun' (زيدون) and the like, and sometimes in meaning, like our saying 'who' (من) and 'what' (ما) and the like. And generality (العموم) in language is comprehensiveness. It is said: general rain, meaning it covered the places; general prosperity, meaning it encompassed the entities and expanded the lands; a tall palm tree (نخلة عميمة) meaning tall; kinship when expanded reaches the attribute of unclehood (العمومة). And it is like 'thing' (الشيء): a general word that encompasses every existent according to us, and does not encompass the non-existent, contrary to the Mu'tazila, although every existent has its own specific name. And al-Jassas mentioned that the general is: what encompasses a collection of nouns or meanings. And his saying 'or meanings': this is an oversight on his part, or interpreted; because meanings do not multiply except when they differ and are distinct, and when they differ and are distinct, one word cannot encompass them, rather each one of them must be taken individually, and this is called: a homonym (مشترك). And he mentioned after this that the homonym has no generality, so it is established that it is an oversight or interpreted. Its interpretation: that when the same meaning has multiple loci, it is called 'meanings' metaphorically, due to the multiplicity of its loci, but it would have been better to say 'and the meanings' (والمعاني), and the correct view is that it is an oversight. As for the homonym (المشترك), it is every word that can bear a meaning among different meanings, or nouns with different meanings, in such a way that only one of them is intended from the whole. Like 'ayn: a name for the eye of the beholder, the eye of the sun, the eye of the balance, the eye of the knee, the eye of water, and others. And like mawla (مولى) and qur' (قُرء) are among the homonymous nouns according to the linguists. It is derived from 'sharing' (الاشتراك), and there is no generality for this word, and it is like as-sarim: it is a name for both night and dawn together, probabilistically, not as a general. This differs from the ambiguous (المجمل); because the homonym can be comprehended by reflecting on its meaning linguistically, by giving preponderance to some aspects over others, so before the appearance of preponderance, the speech is called: homonym. As for the ambiguous (المجمل): it is what cannot be comprehended linguistically due to an additional meaning established by law, or due to the closure of the door of preferring one meaning linguistically, so it is necessary to refer to the clarification of the ambiguous, as we will explain in its place, God willing. As for the interpreted (المؤول): it is what from among the homonyms has some of its aspects given preponderance by predominant opinion, derived from 'al, yauulu' meaning 'he returned', and 'awwaltuhu' meaning 'I caused it to return and turned it'; because when you reflect on the context of the word, and you turn the word to some meanings specifically, you have interpreted it to that meaning, and that becomes the outcome of the probability by means of opinion. Allah the Exalted said: 'Do they await anything else than its interpretation?' (Quran 7:53) meaning its outcome. And this is not like the ambiguous when some of its aspects are known by the clarification of the ambiguous, for then it becomes...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage from Bazdawi's Usul is a definitional section on four key usul terms: al-'am (the general), al-mushtarak (the homonym), al-mujmal (the ambiguous), and al-mu'awwal (the interpreted). The author begins by distinguishing levels of specificity: generic (jins), specific type (naw'), and individual ('ayn) with examples. He then defines al-'am as a word encompassing a collection of entities either in wording (like plural Zaidun) or in meaning (like the particles 'man' and 'ma'). He clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'umum as comprehensiveness, and uses the example of the word 'thing' (shay') as a general term covering all existents, noting that the Mu'tazila erroneously included non-existents. He then critiques al-Jassas's definition that 'am encompasses both nouns and meanings, arguing that it should be only nouns, because meanings that are distinct cannot be covered by a single word; if they were, it would be a homonym, not a general. He further clarifies that if the same meaning has multiple instances, it is metaphorically called 'meanings' but that is not proper. Next, he defines mushtarak as a word with multiple possible meanings, where only one is intended in context (e.g., 'ayn, mawla, qur'). He distinguishes it from mujmal: a mushtarak can be understood by linguistic reflection and preponderance (tarjih), while a mujmal cannot be understood linguistically due to a legal addition or closure of the path of preponderance, requiring external clarification (bayan). Finally, he defines mu'awwal as a mushtarak where one meaning has been given preponderance by predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y), citing the Quranic verse on ta'wil (7:53) to illustrate the concept of 'outcome'. The passage is foundational for understanding how Hanafi usul classifies texts based on clarity and ambiguity.

Key Terms
العام (al-‘āmm): Every word that encompasses a collection of nouns, either in wording or in meaning.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word with multiple possible meanings, where only one is intended in context.
المجمل (al-mujmal): That which cannot be comprehended linguistically due to an additional legal meaning or closure of the door of preponderance.
المؤول (al-mu’awwal): That from among the homonyms which has some of its aspects given preponderance by predominant opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Mushtarak has no generality, while mujmal has generality.
B) Mushtarak can be understood through linguistic preponderance, while mujmal cannot.
C) Mushtarak requires clarification from the Lawgiver, while mujmal does not.
D) Mushtarak is specific to nouns, while mujmal applies only to verbs.

Key Takeaway
The classification of a text as 'am, mushtarak, mujmal, or mu'awwal determines how it is understood and applied in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Specific Term (al-Khas) and Its Definitive Nature
Defines the specific term and applies it to two Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a specific term (khas) in a Quranic text and apply the Hanafi rule that khas is qat'i (definitive) in its meaning, using the example of 'three quru'.'

Core Idea
A specific term is definitive; its number cannot be lessened, so 'three quru'' must mean three complete menstrual cycles.

In Hanafi usul, the classification of a term as specific (khas) carries immense weight: its meaning is considered certain and not open to reinterpretation. This lesson examines the definition of the specific term and how the Hanafis apply it to two Quranic verses—one on the waiting period ('iddah) and one on bowing in prayer. You will see how the specificity of the number 'three' forces a particular interpretation of the ambiguous word 'quru'.'

Guided Path
1
Define the Specific Term
Understand the theoretical definition: a term that covers its specific referent with certainty and cannot be altered by explanation (bayan).
Key Arabic: 'اللفظ الخاص يتناول المخصوص قطعاً ويقيناً'
2
Analyze the First Quranic Example (2:228)
Examine the phrase 'three quru'.' Note that 'three' is a specific number. The term 'quru'' is ambiguous between menstruation and purity.
Verse: ﴿ وَالْمُطَلَّقَاتُ يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ ثَلَاثَةَ قُرُوءٍ ﴾
3
Apply the Hanafi Argument
If 'quru'' meant purity periods, three would not yield a whole number; only the menstruation interpretation gives a complete integer count, confirming the specificity of 'three'.
The Hanafi position: quru' = hayd (menstruation).
4
Analyze the Second Quranic Example (2:43)
Examine the term 'ruku'' as a specific action. The passage argues that equating standing (i'tidal) to ruku' based on a solitary hadith would be invalid because ruku' is clear and specific.
Verse: ﴿ وَارْكَعُوا مَعَ الرَّاكِعِينَ ﴾
5
Conclude the Principle
A specific term (khas) is definitive in its meaning and cannot be overridden by analogy or weak evidence without proper authority.
This reinforces the Hanafi hierarchy: khas is qat'i al-dalalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان معرفة أحكام الخصوص. اللفظ الخاص: يتناولُ المخصوص قطعاً ويقيناً بلا شبهة لما أريد من الحكم، ولا يخلو الخاص عن هذا في أصل الوضع وإن احتمل التغير أصل وضعه، ولكن لا يحتمل التصرف فيه بطريق البيان؛ لكونه بينا عن لما وضع له. ذلك من : أن الله تعالى قال: ﴿ وَالْمُطَلَّقَاتُ يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ ثَلَاثَةَ قُرُوءٍ ﴾ [البقرة / 228]: قلنا نحن المراد بها الحيض؛ لأنا إذا حملنا على الأطهار انتقص العدد عن الثلاثة، فصارت العدة قُرعَيْن وبعض الثالث، : وإذا حملنا على الحيض : كانت ثلاثة كاملةً والثلاثة اسم خاص لعدد مخصوص معلوم لا يحتمل غيره كالفرد لا يحتمل العدد، والواحد لا يحتمل المثنى، فكان هذا بمعنى الرد والإبطال. ومن ذلك : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَارْكَعُوا مَعَ الراكعينَ ﴾ [البقرة / 43]: والركوع اسم خاص لفعل معلوم ، وهو الميلان عن الاستواء بما يقطع اسم الاستواء، فلا يكون إلحاق التعديل به على سبيل الفرض حتى تفسد الصلاة بتركه بياناً صحيحاً، لأنه بَيِّن بنفسه، بل يكون رفعاً لحكم الكتاب بخبر الواحد). (۱) باب بيان معرفة أحكام الخصوص: قوله: بخبر الواحد :
Chapter on explaining the rulings of the specific. The specific term: it covers its specific referent absolutely and certainly without doubt regarding the intended ruling, and the specific is not devoid of this in the original usage, although its original usage may admit of change, but it cannot be altered by way of explanation (bayan) because it is already clear for what it was coined. Among that: Allah Most High said: 'And divorced women shall wait concerning themselves for three quru'' [al-Baqarah/228]. We (Hanafis) say: what is meant by it is menstruation, because if we take it to mean purity periods, the number falls short of three, so the waiting period becomes two quru' and part of the third. And if we take it as menstruation, then it is three complete ones. And 'three' is a specific term for a known specific number that cannot admit of another, just as 'odd' does not admit of even, and 'one' does not admit of dual. So this was in the sense of rejection and invalidation. And among that: His saying Exalted: 'And bow with those who bow' [al-Baqarah/43]. And bowing (ruku') is a specific term for a known action, which is inclining from straightness to the extent that it cuts off the name of straightness. Thus, equating [standing] to it by way of obligation that renders prayer invalid if omitted is not a valid explanation, because it is clear in itself; rather, it would be raising the ruling of the Book by a solitary hadith. (1) Chapter on explaining the rulings of the specific: His statement: 'by a solitary hadith':
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the rulings of the specific term (al-khusus). The author defines a specific term as one that indicates its meaning with certainty and without doubt. It is clear by its original coinage and cannot be reinterpreted through a bayan (clarifying statement) because it is already clear. Two Quranic examples are given to illustrate this point. First, the verse on 'iddah: 'three quru''. The word 'quru' is ambiguous, having both meanings of menstruation and purity. The Hanafi position, attributed to the author saying 'we say,' is that it means menstruation. The reasoning is that the number three is a specific term that cannot be divided; if we interpret quru' as purity periods, the 'iddah would not be a whole number, contradicting the specificity of 'three'. Therefore, the alternative interpretation (that quru' means purity) is rejected. Second, the verse 'and bow with those who bow.' The word 'ruku' is a specific term for a known action of bending. To equate standing (i'tidal after ruku) with ruku' by making it obligatory in the same way would be a false explanation because ruku' is clear in its meaning. Such an equation would, if based on a solitary hadith, amount to abrogating the Quranic ruling by a solitary hadith, which is invalid. Thus, the specificity of the term must be respected.

Key Terms
اللفظ الخاص (al-lafz al-khas): A term that covers its specific referent with certainty and is clear in its original coinage, not open to reinterpretation by bayan.
القرء (al-qur'): The term for a woman's menstrual cycle or purity period; in this context, the Hanafi interpretation restricts it to menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in this passage, why must 'quru' in Quran 2:228 mean menstruation?
A) Because a solitary hadith states so.
B) Because 'three' is a specific term for a complete integer, and only the menstruation interpretation gives three complete periods.
C) Because the majority of scholars say so.
D) Because the verse itself mentions 'quru' in the context of divorce.

Key Takeaway
A specific term (khas) in the Quran is definitive in its scope and cannot be reinterpreted to yield an incomplete or fractional meaning.

The Specific Term and Its Certainty
Defining the specific term and its certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the specific term (al-khass) according to Hanafi usul and apply its certainty principle to resolve the interpretation of 'quru'' in the iddah verse.

Core Idea
A specific term is certain in its meaning; the number three demands completeness, so quru' must mean menstruation.

In this passage, Al-Bazdawi establishes a foundational principle: the specific term (al-lafz al-khass) denotes its meaning with absolute certainty and cannot be altered through explanation. To demonstrate, he pits the Hanafi interpretation of 'three quru'' against the Shafi'i view. The analytical move is deceptively simple: a specific number must be complete, and only the menstruation reading satisfies that condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-khass
Read the definition: the specific term encompasses its meaning with certainty and no doubt. Note that it does not admit alteration through explanation (bayan), only potentially through abrogation (naskh).
Contrast this with the general term (amm) which is open to specification (takhsis).
2
Analyze the iddah verse as an example
The verse commands three quru'. The Hanafi position: quru' means menstruations. Follow the logic: if it meant purity periods, the count would be two and a fraction, violating the specificity of 'three'.
3
Apply the certainty of the specific term
The specific term 'three' cannot be compromised. The opposing interpretation is refuted because it would undermine the integer's completeness.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a specific term (khass) is qat'i in its indication.
4
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi iddah is three menstrual cycles; Shafi'i iddah is three clean periods. The difference stems from the initial interpretation.

باب بيان معرفة أحكام الخصوص

اللفظ الخاص: يتناول المخصوص قطعاً ويقيناً بلا شبهة لما أريد من الحكم، ولا يخلو الخاص عن هذا في أصل الوضع وإن احتمل التغير أصل وضعه، ولكن لا يحتمل التصرف فيه بطريق البيان؛ لكونه بينا لما وضع له.

وذلك من : أن الله تعالى قال: ﴿ وَالْمُطَلَّقَاتُ يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ ثَلَاثَةَ قُرُوءٍ ﴾ [البقرة / 228] قلنا نحن المراد بها الحيض؛ لأنا إذا حملنا على الأطهار انتقص العدد عن الثلاثة، فصارت العدة قرعين وبعض الثالث، وإذا حملنا على الحيض كانت ثلاثة كاملةً والثلاثة اسم خاص لعدد مخصوص معلوم لا يحتمل غيره كالفرد لا يحتمل العدد، والواحد لا يحتمل المثنى، فكان هذا بمعنى الرد والإبطال.

ومن ذلك : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَارْكَعُوا مَعَ الراكعينَ ﴾ [البقرة / 43] والركوع اسم خاص لفعل معلوم ، وهو الميلان عن الاستواء بما يقطع اسم الاستواء، فلا يكون إلحاق التعديل به على سبيل الفرض حتى تفسد الصلاة بتركه بياناً صحيحاً، لأنه بين بنفسه، بل يكون رفعاً لحكم الكتاب بخبر الواحد، لكنه يلحق به إلحاق الفرع بالأصل، ليصير واجباً ملحقاً بالفرض، كما هو منزلة خبر الواحد من الكتاب.

ومن ذلك : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلْيَطَوَفُوا بِالْبَيْتِ الْعَتِيقِ ﴾ [الحج / 29] وهذا فعل خاص وضع لمعنى خاص، وهو الدوران حول البيت، فلا يكون وقفه على الطهارة عن الحدث حتى لا ينعقد إلا بها عملاً بالكتاب، ولا بياناً له، بل نسخاً محضاً، فلا يصح بخبر الواحد، لكنه

عن أبي هريرة أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم دخل المسجد، فدخل رجل فصلى، ثم جاء فسلم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: ارجع فصل فإنك لم تصل، فرجع فصلى كما صلى، ثم جاء فسلم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقال: ارجع فصل فإنك لم تصل، ثلاثاً، فقال: والذي بعثك بالحق، ما أحسن غيره، فعلمني، فقال : إذا قمت إلى الصلاة فكبر، ثم اقرأ ما تيسر معك من القرآن، ثم اركع حتى تطمئن راكعاً، ثم ارفع حتى تعتدل قائماً، اسجد حتى تطمئن ساجداً، ثم ارفع حتى تطمئن جالساً، ثم اسجد حتى تطمئن ساجداً، ثم افعل ذلك في صلاتك كلها. متفق عليه.

وفي رواية لمسلم : إذا قمت إلى الصلاة فأسبغ الوضوء، ثم استقبل القبلة فكبر. الحديث، وأخرجه الترمذي، وفيه بعد قوله: «تطمئن جالساً، ثم قم، فإذا فعلت ذلك : فقد تمت صلاتك، وإن انتقصت من ذلك شيئاً : فقد انتقصت صلاتك»، قال: وكان هذا أهون عليهم من الأول أنه من انتقص من ذلك شيئاً: انتقص من صلاته، ولم تذهب كلها.

وفي رواية لأبي داود: «فإذا صنعت ذلك : فقد قضيت صلاتك، وما انتقصت ذلك : فإنما تنتقصه من صلاتك».
Chapter: Explanation of the Rulings of Specificity

The specific term: It encompasses the specified meaning absolutely and certainly, without any doubt, for the intended ruling. The specific term is not devoid of this in its original usage, even if its original usage may potentially change. However, it does not admit of being altered through explanation, because it is clear in what it was placed for.

And from that is that Allah Almighty said: {And divorced women shall wait by themselves for three periods [qurū']} [al-Baqarah 2:228]. We said: The intended meaning is menstruation; because if we interpret it as purity periods, the number would fall short of three, so the waiting period would become two periods and part of a third. But if we interpret it as menstruation, it becomes a complete three. And three is a specific term for a particular, known number that does not admit its other, just as an individual does not admit a number, and the singular does not admit the dual. So this is in the sense of refutation and invalidation.

And from that is His statement: {And bow with those who bow} [al-Baqarah 2:43]. Bowing is a specific term for a known action, which is inclining from erectness so as to cut off the name of erectness. So attaching adjustment to it as an obligation such that the prayer becomes invalid by its omission cannot be a valid explanation, because it is clear in itself. Rather, it would be an abrogation of the ruling of the Book by a khabar al-wahid. But it is attached to it by attaching the branch to the root, so that it becomes a duty annexed to the obligation, as is the status of khabar al-wahid in relation to the Book.

And from that is His statement: {And let them perform tawaf around the Ancient House} [al-Hajj 22:29]. This is a specific action placed for a specific meaning, which is circumambulating around the House. So requiring it to be conditioned on purity from minor impurity such that it is not valid except with it cannot be in accordance with the Book, nor an explanation of it, but a pure abrogation, so it is not valid by khabar al-wahid. However...

On the authority of Abu Huraira that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) entered the mosque, and a man entered and prayed, then came and greeted the Prophet (peace be upon him). He said: "Go back and pray, for you have not prayed." So he went back and prayed as he had prayed, then came and greeted the Prophet (peace be upon him). He said: "Go back and pray, for you have not prayed" - three times. The man said: "By Him who sent you with the truth, I cannot do better than this; teach me." He said: "When you stand for the prayer, say the takbir, then recite whatever is easy for you of the Qur'an, then bow until you are at ease in bowing, then rise until you stand straight, then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration, then rise until you are at ease sitting, then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration, then do that throughout your entire prayer." Agreed upon.

In a narration of Muslim: "When you stand for the prayer, perform ablution properly, then face the qibla and say the takbir." The hadith. And al-Tirmidhi reported it, and in it after his saying: "until you are at ease sitting, then stand. If you do that, your prayer is complete. And if you diminish anything from that, you have diminished from your prayer." He said: This was easier for them than the first: that whoever diminishes anything from that diminishes from his prayer, and it does not all go.

In a narration of Abu Dawud: "If you do that, you have fulfilled your prayer. And whatever you diminish from that, you have diminished from your prayer."
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is from Al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh, a foundational Hanafi usul text. The author opens a chapter explaining the rulings of specificity (al-khusus). He first defines the specific term (al-lafz al-khass): it denotes its specified meaning with certainty and without doubt. Though the original usage may theoretically be subject to change (e.g., via abrogation), the specific term itself does not admit alteration through explanation (bayan) because it is inherently clear.

To illustrate, he gives three Quranic examples. First, the verse on the waiting period (iddah) of divorced women: "three qurū'" (Q. 2:228). The Hanafi position ("we said") is that qurū' means menstruations (hayd), not purity periods (at-har). The reasoning is that three is a specific integer that must be complete; if qurū' meant pure periods, the count would be two complete periods and part of a third, which contradicts the specific number three. Thus the Hanafi interpretation is correct, and the opposing view is refuted.

Second, the command to "bow with those who bow" (Q. 2:43). Bowing (ruku') is a specific act defined as inclining from erectness so as to remove the name of erectness. The author discusses the role of khabar al-wahid (a report from a single narrator) in relation to this clear Qur'anic command. Adding an additional requirement (like adjusting bowing) as an obligation whose omission invalidates prayer cannot be a valid explanation of the verse, since the verse is clear. It would effectively abrogate the Qur'anic ruling, which a khabar al-wahid cannot do. However, such a report can attach a subsidiary obligation to the original, making it a wajib annexed to the original fard, which is the proper role of khabar al-wahid.

Third, the command to "perform tawaf around the Ancient House" (Q. 22:29). Tawaf is a specific action with a specific meaning: circumambulating the Kaaba. Imposing a condition of ritual purity (wudu) as a validity requirement would not be an explanation (bayan) of the verse but a pure abrogation (naskh), which again cannot be done by khabar al-wahid.

The passage then transitions into a hadith. The author likely uses this hadith (the famous hadith of the man who prayed incorrectly) to demonstrate how the Prophet (peace be upon him) explained the specific acts of prayer, which are obligatory yet detailed through his statements and actions. The hadith is cited with multiple narrations, each emphasizing that the prescribed acts are integral parts of prayer and that omitting any of them reduces the prayer's completeness. This supports the usul point that specific commands from the Prophet (whether from mutawatir or ahad reports) define obligations, but they cannot abrogate a clear Qur'anic ruling unless they meet certain criteria.

Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi distinction between what can be established by khabar al-wahid (addition of a subsidiary ruling) versus what requires a stronger source (abrogation or change of a clear Qur'anic ruling).

Key Terms
خاص (khāṣṣ): A term that denotes its specified meaning with certainty and without doubt; it cannot be altered through explanation.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid): A report transmitted by a single narrator at any generation, not reaching the level of tawatur.
قروء (qurūʾ): The waiting period term in divorce; interpreted by Hanafis as menstruations (hayd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the interpretation of 'three quru'' as three purity periods?
A) Because the word 'قرء' never means purity periods in Arabic.
B) Because if quru' meant purity, the number three would not be a complete integer, contradicting the specificity of the number.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said it means menstruation.
D) Because the specific term cannot be explained at all.

Key Takeaway
A specific term is certain and must be taken as a complete unit; any interpretation that undermines its completeness is invalid.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Khabar al-Wahid and the Clear Specific Text
Explaining the limits of khabar al-wahid in relation to specific Quranic commands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid explanation (bayan) by khabar al-wahid and an invalid abrogation (naskh), using the examples of ruku' and tawaf from the passage.

Core Idea
A khabar al-wahid cannot abrogate a clear Quranic command but can add a subsidiary obligation.

After establishing the certainty of specific terms, Al-Bazdawi turns to the interface between these clear texts and hadith. The examples of bowing (ruku') and circumambulation (tawaf) illustrate a critical usul principle: a single-narrator report (khabar al-wahid) cannot alter or abrogate a clear, specific command from the Quran. However, it can attach additional duties (wajibat) to the original obligation. The hadith of the man who prayed incorrectly then demonstrates how the Prophet (peace be upon him) explained the specific acts of prayer, which became binding yet without abrogating the general command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic commands
Two clear, specific commands: 'bow with those who bow' (ruku') and 'perform tawaf around the Ancient House'. Both are specific acts with defined meanings.
The specificity comes from the terms 'bow' and 'tawaf' being khass for particular actions.
2
Understand the role of khabar al-wahid
Khabar al-wahid can explain ambiguities or add details, but it cannot change the clear meaning of the Quranic command. If it tries to change the meaning (e.g., requiring a specific manner that wasn't part of the original), that would be abrogation, which it cannot do.
In the ruku' example, adding an extra requirement like 'adjustment' as an obligation whose omission invalidates prayer would be abrogation, not explanation.
3
Distinguish between annexation and abrogation
The author says that khabar al-wahid can attach a subsidiary obligation (wajib) to the original fard, like attaching a branch to the root. This is not abrogation but an addition that does not change the Quranic command.
This is the Hanafi concept of 'ilhaq al-far' bi al-asl'.
4
Analyze the hadith as a practical example
The hadith details the specific acts of prayer (takbir, recitation, bowing, etc.) and states that omitting any diminishes the prayer. This hadith does not change the command to pray; it specifies the wajib components. It is a valid explanation (bayan) by a khabar al-wahid.
The multiple narrations show that these acts are obligatory but not that the prayer is invalid without them; rather, the prayer is incomplete.

Source Text

The specific term is not devoid of this in its original usage, even if its original usage may potentially change. Agreed upon.

In a narration of Muslim: "When you stand for the prayer, perform ablution properly, then face the qibla and say the takbir." The hadith. And al-Tirmidhi reported it, and in it after his saying: "until you are at ease sitting, then stand. If you do that, your prayer is complete. And if you diminish anything from that, you have diminished from your prayer." He said: This was easier for them than the first: that whoever diminishes anything from that diminishes from his prayer, and it does not all go.

In a narration of Abu Dawud: "If you do that, you have fulfilled your prayer. And whatever you diminish from that, you have diminished from your prayer."
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is from Al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh, a foundational Hanafi usul text. The author opens a chapter explaining the rulings of specificity (al-khusus). He first defines the specific term (al-lafz al-khass): it denotes its specified meaning with certainty and without doubt. Though the original usage may theoretically be subject to change (e.g., via abrogation), the specific term itself does not admit alteration through explanation (bayan) because it is inherently clear.

To illustrate, he gives three Quranic examples. First, the verse on the waiting period (iddah) of divorced women: "three qurū'" (Q. 2:228). The Hanafi position ("we said") is that qurū' means menstruations (hayd), not purity periods (at-har). The reasoning is that three is a specific integer that must be complete; if qurū' meant pure periods, the count would be two complete periods and part of a third, which contradicts the specific number three. Thus the Hanafi interpretation is correct, and the opposing view is refuted.

Second, the command to "bow with those who bow" (Q. 2:43). Bowing (ruku') is a specific act defined as inclining from erectness so as to remove the name of erectness. The author discusses the role of khabar al-wahid (a report from a single narrator) in relation to this clear Qur'anic command. Adding an additional requirement (like adjusting bowing) as an obligation whose omission invalidates prayer cannot be a valid explanation of the verse, since the verse is clear. It would effectively abrogate the Qur'anic ruling, which a khabar al-wahid cannot do. However, such a report can attach a subsidiary obligation to the original, making it a wajib annexed to the original fard, which is the proper role of khabar al-wahid.

Third, the command to "perform tawaf around the Ancient House" (Q. 22:29). Tawaf is a specific action with a specific meaning: circumambulating the Kaaba. Imposing a condition of ritual purity (wudu) as a validity requirement would not be an explanation (bayan) of the verse but a pure abrogation (naskh), which again cannot be done by khabar al-wahid.

The passage then transitions into a hadith. The author likely uses this hadith (the famous hadith of the man who prayed incorrectly) to demonstrate how the Prophet (peace be upon him) explained the specific acts of prayer, which are obligatory yet detailed through his statements and actions. The hadith is cited with multiple narrations, each emphasizing that the prescribed acts are integral parts of prayer and that omitting any of them reduces the prayer's completeness. This supports the usul point that specific commands from the Prophet (whether from mutawatir or ahad reports) define obligations, but they cannot abrogate a clear Qur'anic ruling unless they meet certain criteria.

Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi distinction between what can be established by khabar al-wahid (addition of a subsidiary ruling) versus what requires a stronger source (abrogation or change of a clear Qur'anic ruling).

Key Terms
خاص (khāṣṣ): A term that denotes its specified meaning with certainty and without doubt; it cannot be altered through explanation.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid): A report transmitted by a single narrator at any generation, not reaching the level of tawatur.
قروء (qurūʾ): The waiting period term in divorce; interpreted by Hanafis as menstruations (hayd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school say that requiring ritual purity (wudu) for tawaf based on a khabar al-wahid is not valid?
A) Because khabar al-wahid cannot add conditions to any act of worship.
B) Because the Quranic command to perform tawaf is clear and specific; adding purity as a condition would be an abrogation, which khabar al-wahid cannot do.
C) Because ritual purity is never required for tawaf according to the Quran.
D) Because khabar al-wahid cannot contradict another hadith.

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid can explain and add subsidiary obligations to clear Quranic commands, but it cannot abrogate or change their fundamental meaning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Khabar al-Wahid Cannot Override a Clear Nass
Argument that khabar al-wahid cannot impose conditions on a clear Quranic text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that khabar al-wahid cannot impose conditions on a clear nass and apply it to evaluate an inter-madhhab dispute regarding intention (niyyah) in wudu.

Core Idea
A clear nass stands on its own; khabar al-wahid cannot add conditions it does not contain.

One of the most consequential debates between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools is whether a solitary hadith can add a condition to a clear Quranic command. This lesson examines the Hanafi argument that the Quran's own clarity prevents that. We will work through the reasoning using the wudu verse as a case study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the clear nass (khas)
Locate the Quranic text that is the subject of the dispute: Surah al-Ma'idah 5:6 (فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ). Why is it considered khas? Because it uses specific terms (ghusl, mash) with clear meanings.
A khas text is one that indicates a particular meaning with clarity, not open to multiple interpretations.
2
Understand the opposing claim
The opponent (Shafi'i) argues that intention (niyyah) is required for wudu based on the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and other reports. They claim these reports explain (bayan) the verse.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
The Hanafi rule: Khabar al-wahid can only explain ambiguous (mujmal) texts, not add conditions to clear (khas) texts. Since the verse is clear, the condition of niyyah cannot be imposed by a solitary report.
4
Evaluate the two errors
According to the text, the opposing view commits two errors: (1) lowering the status of a specific Quranic text, and (2) raising the ruling of khabar al-wahid above its proper station.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: For Hanafis, wudu is valid without niyyah (niyyah is sunnah). For Shafi'is, intention is obligatory (fard).
This shows how a single usul principle affects actual practice.

و فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ ﴾ المائدة /٦ : فإنما الوضوء غَسل ومسح، وهما لفظان خاصان لمعنى معلوم في أصل الوضع فلا يكون شرط النية في ذلك لا بياناً له ، وهو بَيِّن بنفسه لما وضع له، بل يجب أن يُلحق به عملا على الوصف الذي ذكرنا.

وصار مذهب المخالف في هذا الأصل غلطاً من وجهين : أحدهما : أنه حطّ منزلة الخاص من الكتاب عن رتبته.
والثاني : أنه رَفَعَ حُكْمَ خبر الواحد (۱) فوق منزلته .
(۱) قوله : رَفَعَ حُكْمَ خبر الواحد أراد ما عن عمر بن الخطاب قال: سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : إنما الأعمال بالنية، وإنما لكل امرىء ما نوى الحديث رواه الجماعة.

وما في الصحيحين عن عبد الله بن زيد أنه حكى وضوء رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم متوالياً، وعن ابن عباس مثله عند البخاري، وما رواه الدارقطني من طريق =
It is added to it as a wajib annexed to the fard, just as the status of khabar al-wahid is to the Book, so that the ruling is established according to its evidence. And from that is His statement Exalted: 'O you who have believed, when you rise to prayer...' Agreed upon. And I have not found in it the word: 'innovator.' Have you menstruated? I said: Yes. He said: This is a thing that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, has decreed upon the daughters of Adam. Rather, it must be annexed to it in practice according to the description we mentioned. And the condition of continuity (walā') and order (tartīb), and also the naming (tasmiyah) are nullified; for what we mentioned. And the madhhab of the opponent in this principle is erroneous in two respects: one: that it lowers the status of the specific text (khas) of the Book from its rank. And the second: that it raises the ruling of khabar al-wahid above its station. (1) His statement: 'raises the ruling of khabar al-wahid' means what is from Umar ibn al-Khattab said: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'Indeed actions are by intentions, and indeed for every person is what he intended' - the hadith narrated by the group. And what Abu Dawud narrated from Baqiyya from Burayr ibn Sa'd from Khalid from Ibn Ma'dan from some of the Companions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, that the Prophet, peace be upon him, saw a man praying and on his foot a spot that water had not reached, so he ordered him to repeat the wudu and the prayer. Shaykh Taqi al-Din said in 'al-Imam': Baqiyya is a mudallis, except that al-Hakim narrated it in 'al-Mustadrak' and said in it: narrated to us Burayr ibn Sa'd, so the accusation is removed. But Muslim narrated it from the hadith of Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, with the wording: 'Go back and improve your wudu, then pray.' And Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah narrated it from the hadith of Anas with the wording: 'Go back and improve' and its chain is trustworthy. And al-Tabarani and al-Daraqutni narrated it with the wording: 'Go and complete your wudu,' and its chain is weak. And what is in the two Sahihs from Abdullah ibn Zayd that he described the wudu of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, as continuous, and from Ibn Abbas similar in al-Bukhari, and what al-Daraqutni narrated from the path of ...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational Hanafi usul argument: khabar al-wahid (a solitary report) cannot impose a condition or additional requirement upon a clear and specific (khas) text from the Quran. The author begins by stating a principle: when a khabar al-wahid adds an obligation (wajib) that is annexed to a fard (obligatory act), it is like the position of khabar al-wahid relative to the Quran—the ruling is established according to the strength of its evidence. Then, the verse of wudu (5:6) is cited: 'O you who have believed, when you rise to prayer, wash your faces...' The author argues that the words 'washing' (ghusl) and 'wiping' (mash) are specific terms (khas) with known meanings in their original linguistic usage. Therefore, the condition of intention (niyyah) is not an explanation (bayan) of these terms; the verse is clear by itself for what it was placed. So, any requirement of niyyah from khabar al-wahid would not be a proper explanation. Rather, if a khabar al-wahid reports an additional act (like saying 'Bismillah'), it should be annexed in practice according to the description. This also invalidates conditions like continuity (walā'), order (tartīb), and saying 'Bismillah' because the verse does not mention them. The author then declares the opposing madhhab (Shafi'i) to be erroneous in two ways: (1) they have lowered the status of a specific text (khas) from the Quran, and (2) they have raised the ruling of khabar al-wahid above its proper station. The footnote explains that the 'khabar al-wahid' referred to is the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and other hadith about wudu. It then discusses the chains of these hadiths, noting weaknesses and support. The overall lesson is a classic Hanafi usul position that a solitary report cannot override the clear apparent meaning of the Quran in imposing conditions, even if the report is authentic—it can only explain (bayan) ambiguous (mujmal) texts, not clear ones. The passage also interjects several hadith about tawaf and hajj, which seem to be additional examples of the principle, possibly from a commentary.

Key Terms
خاص (khas): A specific text that indicates a particular meaning with clarity, not open to multiple interpretations.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A solitary report that does not reach the level of tawatur; it is zanni in proof.
نية (niyyah): Intention, which is not required for wudu according to Hanafis based on this passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this Hanafi usul text, why can khabar al-wahid not impose the condition of niyyah (intention) in wudu?
A) Because the hadith about niyyah is weak in its chain.
B) Because the verse of wudu (5:6) is a clear (khas) text that does not require explanation.
C) Because niyyah is not mentioned in any hadith.
D) Because the Quran abrogates the hadith.

Key Takeaway
A clear nass from the Quran cannot be conditioned or restricted by a solitary report, as that would invert the hierarchy of evidence.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Refuting Conditions of Niyya, Mawalat, Tartib, and Tasmiya in Wudu
Analyzes Quranic text to reject additional wudu conditions from ahad hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a khass (specific) Quranic text cannot be specified by a khabar al-wahid, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A clear Quranic text is self-sufficient; ahad hadith cannot add conditions to it.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a core Hanafi principle: the Quranic command to wash and wipe in wudu is specific (khass) and requires no addition. The opponent (Shafi'i) uses solitary hadiths to introduce conditions like intention, continuity, order, and basmala. The Hanafi response is twofold: it preserves the rank of the clear Quranic text and prevents elevating ahad hadith above their proper level. Understanding this argument is key to mastering the Hanafi hierarchy of proofs.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic Text
Identify the verse (5:6) and the specific words 'فاغسلوا' (wash) and 'وامسحوا' (wipe). The author argues these are the sole components of wudu.
The verse is treated as the primary dalil.
2
Classify the Text
Determine that these words are khass (specific) because they refer to known physical actions with clear limits. In Hanafi usul, khass is qat'i in dalala (definitive in meaning).
Contrast with 'amm (general) which is zanni in dalala.
3
Apply the Hanafi Principle
State the rule: a khass text cannot be overridden or specified by a khabar al-wahid (solitary report). Any hadith that adds conditions is either weak or irrelevant.
This is based on the hierarchy: Quran > Sunnah mutawatira > khabar al-wahid.
4
Identify the Opponent's Argument
Recognize that the Shafi'i school classifies the same verse as 'amm and allows specification by ahad hadith, citing the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' and others.
The Hanafi view is that this misclassifies the text.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi position is preferred: it maintains the proper status of the Quran and does not elevate ahad hadith. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that wudu without intention is valid in the Hanafi school but invalid in the Shafi'i.
Both schools have internal consistency; the dispute is methodological.

Source Text
