فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ ﴾ المائدة /٦ : فإنما الوضوء غَسل ومسح، وهما لفظان خاصان لمعنى معلوم في أصل الوضع فلا يكون شرط النية في ذلك لا بياناً له ، وهو بَيِّن بنفسه لما وضع له، بل يجب أن يُلحق به عملا على الوصف الذي ذكرنا.
وبطل شرطُ الولاء والترتيب، والتسمية أيضاً؛ لما ذكرنا. وصار مذهب المخالف في هذا الأصل غلطاً من وجهين : أحدهما : أنه حطّ منزلة الخاص من الكتاب عن رتبته.
والثاني : أنه رَفَعَ حُكْمَ خبر الواحد (۱) فوق منزلته .
(۱) قوله : رَفَعَ حُكْمَ خبر الواحد أراد ما عن عمر بن الخطاب قال: سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : إنما الأعمال بالنية، وإنما لكل امرىء ما نوى الحديث رواه الجماعة.
وما أخرج أبو داود عن بقية عن بحير بن سعد خالد عن بن معدان عن بعض أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رجلاً يصلي وفي قَدَمِه لُمْعَةٌ لم يُصبها الماء، فأمره أن يعيد الوضوء والصلاة». قال الشيخ تقي الدين في «الإمام»: وبقية: مدلس، إلا أن الحاكم رواه في «المستدرك»، فقال فيه : حدثنا بحير بن سعد فزالت التهمة، لكن أخرجه مسلم من حديث عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه بلفظ : «ارجع فأحسن وضوءك، فرجع، ثم صلى»، وأخرجه أبو داود وابن ماجه من حديث أنس بلفظ : «ارجع فأحسن وسنده ثقات، وأخرجه الطبراني والدارقطني بلفظ : اذهب فأتم وضوءك»، وسنده ضعيف.
وما في الصحيحين عن عبد الله بن زيد أنه حكى وضوء رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم متوالياً، وعن ابن عباس مثله عند البخاري، وما رواه الدارقطني من طريق المسيب بن الواضح عن عبد الله بن عمر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم توضأ مرةً مرة، وقال: هذا وضوء مَن لا يَقبلُ الله منه الصلاة إلا به». الحديث. قال عبد الحق: هذا من أحسن طرق هذا الحديث، وقال ابن أبي حاتم: المسيب: صدوق، لكنه يخطئ كثيراً ، وقال البيهقي : رُوي من أوجه كلها ضعيفة. - وجه الاستدلال به أنه لا يخلو أن يكون والى في هذا، أو لا، لا جائز أنه لم يوال، وإلا لزم عدم صحته متوالياً، فثبت أنه والى، ويلزم أن لا يصح إلا متوالياً؛ لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال : لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا به».
وما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «لا يقبل الله صلاة امرئ حتى يضع الطهور مواضعه فيغسل وجهه، ثم يديه الحديث، و: «ثم»: للعطف والترتيب. وهذا الحديث قال مخرجو أحاديث الرافعي: لم نجده في شيء مما رأينا من كتب الحديث، وإنما جاء في رواية لأبي داود عن رفاعة بن رافع أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «لا تتم صلاةٌ لأحد حتى يُسبغ الوضوء كما أمره الله تعالى، فيغسل وجهه ويديه إلى المرفقين، ويمسح برأسه، ويغسل رجليه إلى الكعبين، ثم يكبر». الحديث.
و ما روى النسائي والدارقطني أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال عند السعي: «إن الصفا والمروة من شعائر الله ، فابدؤوا بما بدأ الله به».
والعبرة لعموم اللفظ وما تقدم من حكاية فعله صلى الله عليه وسلم الذي كان يواظب عليه ، وقوله : هذا وضوء لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا به، وتقدم وجه الاستدلال.
وما أخرجه ابن ماجه من طريق كثير بن زيد عن ربيح بن عبد الرحمن بن أبي سعيد عن أبيه عن أبي سعيد الخدري أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه».
وأخرج عن رباح بن عبد الرحمن بن أبي سفيان أنه جدته زيد تَذكر أنها سمعت أباها سعيد بن زيد يقول : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: لا صلاة لمن لا وضوء له، ولا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه».
So wash your faces (5:6): For wudu is washing and wiping, and these are two specific terms for a known meaning in the original usage, so the condition of intention in that is not a clarification of it, and it is clear by itself for what it was placed, rather it must be attached operationally in the manner we mentioned. And the condition of continuity and order and also the mention of God's name are invalidated for what we mentioned. And the opinion of the opponent in this principle became erroneous in two ways: First, that it lowers the rank of the specific from the Book from its status. And second, that it raises the ruling of a solitary report above its status. (1) His statement: 'raised the ruling of a solitary report' he meant by that of Umar ibn al-Khattab said: I heard the Messenger of God say: 'Actions are but by intentions, and every man has only that which he intended' — the hadith narrated by the group. And what Abu Dawud narrated from Baqiyya from Bahr ibn Sa'd Khalid from Ibn Ma'dan from some of the Prophet's companions that the Prophet saw a man praying and on his foot a spot not reached by water, so he ordered him to repeat wudu and prayer. Shaykh Taqi al-Din said in al-Imam: Baqiyya is a mudallis, except that al-Hakim narrated it in al-Mustadrak saying: narrated to us Bahr ibn Sa'd, so the accusation is removed. But Muslim narrated it from Umar ibn al-Khattab with the wording: 'Go back and improve your wudu, so he returned, then prayed.' And Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah narrated it from Anas with wording: 'Go back and improve — and its chain is trustworthy.' And al-Tabarani and al-Daraqutni narrated it with wording: 'Go and complete your wudu,' and its chain is weak. And what is in the two Sahihs from Abdullah ibn Zayd that he described the wudu of the Messenger of God as consecutive, and from Ibn Abbas likewise in al-Bukhari, and what al-Daraqutni narrated via al-Musayyib ibn al-Wadih from Abdullah ibn Umar that the Prophet performed wudu once each, and said: 'This is the wudu of one whose prayer God will not accept except with it.' The hadith. Abd al-Haqq said: This is among the best chains of this hadith. Ibn Abi Hatim said: al-Musayyib is truthful but errs much. Al-Bayhaqi said: It is narrated through ways all weak. The point of inference from it is that he either maintained continuity in this or not; it is impossible that he did not maintain continuity, otherwise its validity would have been lacking consecutively, so it is proven that he maintained continuity, and it necessarily follows that it is only valid with continuity, because he said: 'God will not accept prayer except with it.' And what is narrated that the Prophet said: 'God will not accept the prayer of a man until he puts ablution in its places, then washes his face, then his hands' — the hadith, and 'thumma' is for conjunction and order. This hadith — the compilers of al-Rafi'i's hadiths said: We did not find it in any of the hadith books we have seen; rather it came in a narration by Abu Dawud from Rifa'a ibn Rafi' that the Prophet said: 'The prayer of anyone is not complete until he performs wudu thoroughly as God has commanded him, then washes his face and hands to the elbows, wipes his head, and washes his feet to the ankles, then says 'Allahu Akbar.'' — the hadith. And what al-Nasa'i and al-Daraqutni narrated that the Prophet said during Sa'i: 'Indeed Safa and Marwa are among the symbols of God, so begin with what God began with.' And the consideration is for the generality of the wording and what preceded of the narration of his action which he constantly adhered to, and his saying: 'This is a wudu without which God will not accept prayer' — and the point of inference preceded. And what Ibn Majah narrated via the path of Kathir ibn Zayd from Rabih ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Sa'id from his father from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Prophet said: 'There is no wudu for one who does not mention God's name upon it.' And narrated from Rabah ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Sufyan that his grandmother Zayd mentioned that she heard her father Sa'id ibn Zayd say: The Messenger of God said: 'No prayer for one who has no wudu, and no wudu for one who does not mention God's name upon it.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the Hanafi commentary on al-Bazdawi's Usul, refuting the addition of conditions to wudu beyond what the Quran explicitly states. The type is textual analysis and dispute. The commentator begins with the Quranic verse (5:6) 'So wash your faces' and argues that wudu consists only of washing and wiping (ghusl and mash). These are specific terms (khass) with known meanings, so requiring intention (niyya) would not be an explanation (bayan) of the verse; the verse is self-evident. Instead, the ruling must be applied practically according to the described manner. Consequently, conditions of continuity (mawalat), order (tartib), and reciting basmala are invalid. The opponent (likely the Shafi'i school) is erroneous in two ways: first, they lower the status of a specific Quranic text (khass min al-kitab) by allowing it to be qualified by ahad hadith; second, they raise the status of a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) above its proper level. The commentator then addresses the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' and other narrations about wudu, analyzing their chains and implications. He shows that the hadiths used to support conditions like continuity and order are either weak, not found in major collections, or mistakenly interpreted. The principle (al-ibra li-umum al-lafz) is that general wording governs, not individual practices. The Hanafi view is that the Quranic text is specific and clear, so no additional conditions are needed.

Key Terms
خاص (khass): A specific term in the Quran that has a clear meaning, not subject to addition by ahad hadith.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A solitary report, not reaching the level of tawatur or mashhur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the main reason the Hanafi school rejects the condition of intention (niyya) in wudu?
A) The hadith on intention is not authentic.
B) The Quranic command to wash is a specific term (khass) that needs no addition.
C) Intention is only required in prayer, not wudu.
D) The practice of the Prophet shows he did not make intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the specific (khass) wording of the Quranic wudu command prevents any additional conditions from being imposed by solitary hadith.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
The Principle of Specific Wording (Lafz Khaass) and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on the Thief Verse
Argument using Quranic examples to prove that specific wording must be strictly followed, then refuting Shafi'i on the thief verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify specific Quranic words (lafz khaass) and apply the Hanafi principle that such wording must be strictly followed, and evaluate the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection regarding the thief's hand-cutting verse.

Core Idea
Specific Quranic wording binds the ruling precisely; deviation nullifies it.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's usul work demonstrates the Hanafi insistence that specific words in the Quran carry precise legal meanings that cannot be altered. The author first provides three clear examples (the letter fa' in divorce, the word ibtigha' in marriage, and the word fard in mahr) to establish the principle, then directly addresses the Shafi'i argument on the thief verse, where al-Shafi'i claimed that 'cutting' necessarily nullifies ownership. The Hanafi response hinges on the word jaza', which shows the punishment is purely Allah's right, thus only inviolability (ismah) transfers, not ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of lafz khaass
Understand that a specific word (lafz khaass) is placed for a specific meaning and must be acted upon exactly. The author gives examples where deviation leads to nullifying the ruling.
Lafz khaass is the opposite of 'amm (general).
2
Analyze the three Quranic examples
Examine the words fa' (indicating immediate sequence), ibtigha' (seeking via contract), and fard (determination by Allah). For each, note the precise legal implication they carry and why any deviation would invalidate the ruling.
These examples establish the method before the main dispute.
3
Understand the Shafi'i claim on the thief verse
Al-Shafi'i argues that 'cutting' (fa-iqta'u) is a specific word that must nullify the inviolability of property, implying ownership is lost. Recognize why this seems plausible.
Both schools agree the word is specific, but differ on its effect.
4
Follow the Hanafi response using 'jaza''
The Hanafis argue that the word 'jaza'an' (as recompense) shows the punishment is from Allah for a crime against His right. Therefore, only the inviolability (ismah) of the property transfers to Allah, not the ownership itself.
The response derives from a connected text (قرين).
5
Distinguish between inviolability and ownership
Understand the Hanafi concept that 'ismah' (inviolability) is an attribute of property separable from ownership (milk). The locus of the crime is the inviolability, not the ownership, so cutting removes the protection but not the ownership.
The owner's determination is procedural (for legal standing), not substantive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افْتَدَتْ بِهِ ﴾ البقرة / ۲۲۹ ، فإنما بدأ بفعل الزوج ، وهو الطلاق، ثم زاد فعل وهو الافتداء، وتحت الأفراد تخصيص المرأة به، وتقرير فعل المرأة والزوج على ما سبق فإثبات فعل الفسخ من الزوج بطريق الخلع : لا يكون عملاً به، بل يكون رفعاً. ذلك : قوله تعالى بعد :هذا ﴿ فَإِن طَلَّقَهَا فَلَا تَحِلُّ لَهُ مِنْ بَعْدُ لله ومن ﴾ البقرة/ ٢٣٠ : فالفاء حرف خاص وضع لمعنى مخصوص، وهو الوصل والتعقيب، وإنما وَصَلَ الطلاق بالافتداء بالمال، فأوجب صحته بعد الخلع، فمن وصله بالرجعي وأبطل وقوعه بعد الخلع : لم يكن عملاً به، ولا بياناً. ومن ذلك قوله تعالى : أَن تَبْتَغُوا بِأَمْوَالِكُمْ تُحْصِنِينَ النساء / ٢٤ : فإنما أُحِلَّ الابتغاء بالمال، والابتغاء لفظ خاص وضع لمعنى مخصوص، وهو الطلب، والطلب بالعقد يقع، فمن جوز تراخي البدل عن الطلب الصحيح إلى المطلوب، وهو فعل الوطء: كان ذلك منه إبطالاً، فبطل به مذهب الخصم في مسألة المُفوّضة. ومثله قوله تعالى: ﴿قَدْ عَلِمْنَا مَا فَرَضْنَا عَلَيْهِمْ فِي أَزْوَاجِهِمْ ﴾ الأحزاب / ٥٠ : فالفرض لفظ خاص وضع لمعنى مخصوص، وهو التقدير، فمن لم يجعل المهر مقدراً شرعاً : كان مُبطلاً . وكذلك الكناية في قوله تعالى: مَا فَرَضْنَا : لفظ خاص یراد به نفس ﴿ المتكلّم ، فدل ذلك على أن صاحب الشرع هو المتولي للإيجاب والتقدير، وأن تقدير العبد امتثال به فمَن جعل إلى العبد اختيار الإيجاب والترك في المهر، والتقدير فيه : كان إبطالاً لموجب هذا اللفظ الخاص، لا عملاً به، ولا بياناً له؛ لأنه بين. ومن ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَارِقُ وَالسَارِقَةُ فَأَقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا جَزَاء بِمَا كَسَبَا ﴾ المائدة / ۳۸: فَأَنَّى قال الشافعي رحمه الله : القطع لفظ خاص لمعنى مخصوص يكون إبطال عصمة المال عملاً به، فقد وقعتم في الذي أبيتم. والجواب: أن ذلك ثبت بنص مقرون به عندنا، وهو قوله تعالى: جَزَاء بِمَا كَسَبَا ؛ لأن الجزاء المطلق اسم لما يجب الله تعالى، على مقابلة فعل العبد وأن ما يجب الله تعالى يدل على خلوص الجناية الداعية إلى الجزاء واقعةً على حقه، ومن ضرورته : تحويل العصمة إليه. ولأن الجزاء يدل على كمال المشروع لما شرع له، مأخوذ من قوله : جزى أي قضى وجزاء بالهمزة: أي كفى، وكماله كمالَ يستدعي الجناية، ولا كمالَ مع قيام حق العبد في العصمة ؛ لأنه يكون حراماً لمعنى في غيره. ولا يلزم أن الملك لا يبطل ؛ لأن محل الجناية العصمة، الحفظ، ولا عصمة إلا بكونه مملوكاً. وهي فأما تعين المالك : فشَرْطٌ ؛ ليصير خصمه متعيناً، لا لعينه، حتى إذا وُجد الخصم بلا ملك كان كافياً كالمكاتب، ومتولي الوقف ونحوهما، فلذلك تحولت العصمة دون الملك. ألا يُرى أن الجناية تقع على المال والعصمة صفة للمال، مثل كونه
[In] His saying (2:229) 'fa-ftadat bihi' – He began with the husband's action, which is divorce, then added an action which is fidya (ransom), and among individuals is specification of the woman with it, and confirmation of the action of the woman and the husband according to what preceded. So proving the action of annulment from the husband through khul' is not acting upon it, but rather cancellation. That: His saying after this: 'fa-in tallaqaha fala tahillu lahu min ba'du lillahi wa min' (2:230) – so the letter fa' is a specific word placed for a specific meaning, which is connection and sequence, and He connected divorce with ransom by property, so He obligated its validity after khul'. So whoever connects it with revocable divorce and nullifies its occurrence after khul' does not act upon it nor clarify it. And from that is His saying (4:24): 'an tabtaghu bi-amwalikum muhsinin' – so seeking with property is permitted, and 'al-ibtigha'' is a specific word placed for a specific meaning, which is seeking, and seeking occurs through contract. So whoever permits deferral of the consideration from the valid seeking to the object sought, which is the act of intercourse, that is from him an annulment, so the opponent's view in the case of mufawwada is nullified. And likewise His saying (33:50): 'qad 'alimna ma faradna 'alayhim fi azwajihim' – so 'al-fard' is a specific word placed for a specific meaning, which is determination. So whoever does not make the mahr legally determined is nullifying. And likewise the kinayah in His saying 'ma faradna' is a specific word intended for the Speaker Himself, so that indicates that the Legislator is the one in charge of obligation and determination, and that the determination of the slave is compliance with it. So whoever gives the slave the choice of obligation and abandonment in the mahr, and determination therein, is nullifying the implication of this specific word, not acting upon it nor clarifying it, because it is clear. And from that is His saying (5:38): 'wa al-sariqu wa al-sariqatu fa-iqta'u aidiyahuma jaza'an bima kasaba' – so then al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Cutting is a specific word for a specific meaning, and nullifying the inviolability of property is acting upon it, so you have fallen into what you refused.' And the answer: That is established by a text connected to it in our view, which is His saying 'jaza'an bima kasaba'; because 'al-jaza'' in absolute terms is a noun for what Allah obligates in exchange for the slave's action, and that what Allah obligates indicates the purity of the crime leading to recompense occurring against His right, and from its necessity is transfer of inviolability to Him. And because 'al-jaza'' indicates the perfection of the legislation for what it was legislated for, derived from 'jaza' meaning he fulfilled, and 'jaza'an' with hamzah means sufficed, and its perfection is perfection that demands the crime, and no perfection with the existence of the slave's right in inviolability; because it would be forbidden due to a meaning in other than it. And it does not follow that ownership is not nullified; because the locus of the crime is inviolability, protection, and no inviolability except by it being possessed. As for the determination of the owner, it is a condition so that his opponent is determined, not for its own sake, so that if an opponent is found without ownership it is sufficient, like the mukatab, trustee of endowment, and the like. Therefore inviolability transfers without ownership. Do you not see that the crime occurs against property, and inviolability is an attribute of property, such as its being...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul argument from al-Bazdawi's work, refuting the Shafi'i school on the necessity of strict adherence to specific wording (alfaz khaassah) in the Quran. The author first provides multiple examples from the Quran where specific words dictate precise legal rulings: the letter fa' in divorce (2:230) indicating sequence, the word 'ibtigha'' in marriage (4:24) indicating seeking via contract, and 'fard' in mahr (33:50) indicating determination by the Lawgiver. These examples show that acting on these specific words requires following their precise meanings; deviating (e.g., allowing deferred consideration in mufawwada or making mahr optional) invalidates the ruling. The author then addresses the Shafi'i objection regarding the thief's hand-cutting verse (5:38). Al-Shafi'i argued that cutting is a specific word implying nullification of the inviolability ('ismah) of property, and that Hanafis contradict themselves by saying cutting is a hudud penalty but not nullifying ownership. The Hanafi response: the phrase 'jaza'an bima kasaba' (as recompense for what they earned) indicates that the punishment is a recompense from Allah, which requires that the crime be purely against Allah's right, not mixing with a human right. For the inviolability of property to be fully removed, the thief must have no ownership claim; hence the inviolability transfers to Allah without transferring ownership itself. The owner determination is procedural (for legal standing) not substantive. The passage concludes by distinguishing between crime against property (mal) and inviolability as an attribute.

Key Terms
لفظ خاص (lafz khaass): A specific word placed for a specific meaning that must be acted upon precisely; deviation nullifies the ruling.
جزاء (jaza'): Absolute recompense from Allah in exchange for the slave's action, indicating the punishment is for Allah's right.
عصمة (ismah): Inviolability that protects property; in theft case it transfers to Allah without nullifying ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the passage, what does the word 'jaza'an' in 5:38 indicate?
A) The punishment is a compensation to the victim.
B) The punishment is a recompense from Allah for the crime.
C) The thief must repay the value of the stolen property.
D) The crime is against the state.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that specific Quranic wording must be strictly followed; in the thief verse, the word 'jaza'' shows the punishment is solely Allah's right, so only inviolability transfers, not ownership.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Transfer of Inviolability vs. Nullification of Ownership: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi rebuts Shafi'i's claim that the specific command nullifies property inviolability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of takhsis using the attached phrase 'jaza'an' to distinguish between nullifying inviolability and nullifying ownership in Qur'anic commands.

Core Idea
A command's attached phrase qualifies its legal effect: the thief's hand is cut by transferring inviolability to God, not by nullifying ownership.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: does the command to cut the thief's hand nullify the inviolability of the stolen property or also its ownership? The Hanafi school refutes the Shafi'i claim by analyzing the attached phrase 'jaza'an' (as a recompense). Through linguistic and rational arguments, they show that inviolability is a quality of property that can be transferred, while ownership remains.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i objection
Identify the Shafi'i claim that the specific command 'cut off their hands' must nullify the inviolability of the property entirely.
The Shafi'i view takes the command as unconditional.
2
Locate the Hanafi response and its textual basis
The response relies on the attached phrase 'jaza'an bima kasaba' to qualify the command. Note how this phrase is used to infer a condition.
This is a takhsis by a connected textual evidence.
3
Analyze the linguistic derivation of 'jaza'
Examine the roots of 'jaza': from 'qada' (decreed) and 'kafa' (sufficed), indicating that the penalty is a complete recompense for a pure transgression against God's right.
This derivation is specific to Hanafi usul linguistics.
4
Trace the conclusion: inviolability transfers, ownership does not
Understand why inviolability can be transferred without affecting ownership, using the analogy of wine and the example of the mukatab.
The locus of transgression is inviolability, not ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَارِقُ وَالسَارِقَةُ فَأَقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا جَزَاء بِمَا كَسَبَا ﴾ المائدة / 38: فإن [؟] قال الشافعي رحمه الله : القطع لفظ خاص لمعنى مخصوص يكون إبطال عصمة المال عملاً به، فقد وقعتم في الذي أبيتم. والجواب: أن ذلك ثبت بنص مقرون به عندنا، وهو قوله تعالى: جَزَاء بِمَا كَسَبَا ؛ لأن الجزاء المطلق اسم لما يجب الله تعالى، على مقابلة فعل العبد وأن ما يجب الله تعالى يدل على خلوص الجناية الداعية إلى الجزاء واقعةً على حقه، ومن ضرورته : تحويل العصمة إليه. ولأن الجزاء يدل على كمال المشروع لما شرع له، مأخوذ من قوله : جزى أي قضى وجزاء بالهمزة: أي كفى، وكماله كمالَ يستدعي الجناية، ولا كمالَ مع قيام حق العبد في العصمة ؛ لأنه يكون حراماً لمعنى في غيره. ولا يلزم أن الملك لا يبطل ؛ لأن محل الجناية العصمة، الحفظ، ولا عصمة إلا بكونه مملوكاً. فأما تعين المالك : فشَرْطٌ ؛ ليصير خصمه متعيناً، لا لعينه، حتى إذا وُجد الخصم بلا ملك كان كافياً كالمكاتب، ومتولي الوقف ونحوهما، فلذلك تحولت العصمة دون الملك. ألا يُرى أن الجناية تقع على المال والعصمة صفة للمال، مثل كونه مملوكاً، فأما الملك الذي هو صفةُ المالك كيف يكون محلا للجناية لينتقل ؟ وكيف ينتقل الملك وهو غير مشروع؟ فأما نقل العصمة : فمشروع، كما في الخمر، والله أعلم بالصواب.
And from that is the saying of Allah Most High: 'And the male thief and the female thief: cut off their hands as a recompense for what they earned' (5:38). Then if al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) says: 'The cutting is a specific word for a specific meaning; it nullifies the inviolability of property by acting upon it, so you have fallen into that which you avoided.' The answer: That is established by a text connected with it according to us, which is His saying 'jaza'an bima kasaba' (as a recompense for what they earned). Because 'al-jaza' al-mutlaq' (the absolute recompense) is a name for what Allah has made obligatory in exchange for the servant's action, and that what Allah has made obligatory indicates the purity of the transgression that calls for the recompense, occurring against His right; and from its necessity is the transfer of inviolability to Him. And because 'al-jaza' indicates the perfection of the prescribed act for that which it was prescribed, derived from His saying 'jaza' meaning 'qada' (he decreed) and 'jaza'an' with hamzah meaning 'kafa' (sufficed), and its perfection is a perfection that necessitates the transgression, and there is no perfection with the existence of the servant's right in inviolability, because it would then be forbidden due to a meaning in other than it. And it does not follow that ownership is not nullified, because the locus of the transgression is inviolability, preservation, and there is no inviolability except by virtue of being owned. As for specifying the owner: it is a condition to make his opponent specified, not for its own essence, such that if the opponent exists without ownership it would suffice, like the mukatab, the administrator of a waqf, and the like. Therefore inviolability is transferred without ownership. Do you not see that the transgression occurs upon the property, and inviolability is a quality of the property, like its being owned? As for ownership which is a quality of the owner, how can it be a locus of transgression to be transferred? And how can ownership be transferred when it is not legislated? As for the transfer of inviolability: it is legislated, as in the case of wine. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi textual analysis and refutation of a Shafi'i argument regarding the Qur'anic verse on cutting the hand of the thief. The Shafi'i objection is that the command 'cut off their hands' is a specific word for a specific meaning, and acting upon it would necessarily nullify the inviolability of the stolen property. The Hanafis avoid this consequence by not taking the command as absolute but by considering the attached phrase 'jaza'an bima kasaba' (as a recompense for what they earned). The Hanafi response argues that this attached text establishes that the penalty is a recompense from Allah in exchange for the servant's action. The word 'jaza' (recompense) is interpreted from two roots: 'jaza' meaning 'qada' (he decreed) and 'jaza'an' with hamzah meaning 'kafa' (sufficed). This indicates that the prescribed punishment is complete and perfect in its purpose, which is to respond to the transgression. The perfection requires that the transgression be pure against Allah's right, not mixed with a servant's right. Therefore, the inviolability of the property must be transferred to Allah, meaning the thief's ownership is not nullified, but the inviolability (protection from punishment) is lifted. The author then clarifies that the locus of transgression is inviolability, not ownership itself. Ownership is a quality of the owner, not of the property, so it cannot be the locus. Specifying the owner is only a procedural condition to identify the opponent in court, not a substantive requirement. Thus, inviolability can be transferred without transferring ownership, analogous to the case of wine where inviolability is removed. The passage ends by noting that from this principle the chapter on 'al-amr' (command) is derived.

Key Terms
عصمة (ismah): Legal inviolability; protection from penalty or liability.
جزاء (jaza'): Recompense or requital; here interpreted as a penalty that is a complete and sufficient response from God.
ملك (milk): Ownership; a legal relationship between an owner and property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, what does the word 'jaza'an' in the verse on cutting the thief's hand indicate?
A) That the penalty is a specific command nullifying ownership.
B) That the penalty is a recompense from God, implying the transgression is against His right.
C) That the penalty is a deterrent with no legal effect on inviolability.
D) That the penalty is identical to the Shafi'i interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The attached phrase 'jaza'an' in Q.5:38 qualifies the command to cut, resulting in transfer of inviolability but not nullification of ownership.

The Hanafi Definition of Amr and the Dispute Over Prophetic Actions
Dispute on whether 'amr' is confined to a fixed verbal form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of amr, state the opposing evidence from Quran and hadith, and explain the Hanafi rebuttal using the principle that expressions cannot fall short of meanings.

Core Idea
Al-amr is confined to a fixed linguistic form; prophetic actions alone do not create obligation.

This passage addresses a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: Is the term 'amr' (command) limited to a specific verbal form like the imperative, or do the Prophet's actions also carry binding force? The Hanafi school insists on the former, while some followers of Malik and al-Shafi‘i argue that actions can be commands. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly deriving obligations from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the opening lines: the Hanafi view is that 'amr' is confined to a fixed form (صيغة لازمة); the opposing view is that 'amr' has no fixed form and includes actions.
Note that the Hanafi position is attributed to 'us' (عندنا) and the opposing view is attributed to 'some people' (ومن الناس).
2
Locate the opposing evidence
The opponents cite Quran 11:97 ('the command of Pharaoh') interpreted as his action, and the hadith 'Pray as you see me praying' to argue that prophetic actions are binding like commands.
The hadith is quoted in full in the footnote; pay attention to how they use it.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis argue that linguistic expressions (العبارات) are specifically placed to convey meanings; it is not permissible for them to fall short of intended purposes. Therefore, commands must be verbal, not merely actions.
This is a linguistic argument about the function of language.
4
Consider the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference affects whether a specific action of the Prophet (e.g., a supererogatory prayer) becomes obligatory if performed without a verbal command. For Hanafis, it does not; for opponents, it might.
This shows how usul disputes impact fiqh rulings.
5
Note the Tarjih
The text sides with the Hanafi position, presenting it as decisive. The opponents' view is attributed to 'some' and the Hanafi evidence is given as a conclusive rebuttal.
Identify the authority: the text is from a Hanafi usul work.

Source Text

As for the transfer of inviolability: it is legislated, like in the case of wine. And Allah knows best what is correct. And from this principle: the chapter on command.

Chapter on command: Indeed the meaning of command is confined to a fixed verbal form according to us. And among people are those who said: The meaning of command does not have a fixed form. The conclusion of that is that the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) are binding according to them, and it is the saying of some of the followers of Malik and al-Shafi‘i (may Allah have mercy on them), like the command. And they argued with the saying of Allah Most High: "And the command of Pharaoh is not right" (Hud 11:97), meaning his action; and if the command were not derived from the action, it would not have been called by it. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "Pray as you see me praying," so he made following necessary.

And our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) argued that expressions are only placed as indications of intended meanings, and it is not permissible for expressions to fall short of the purposes and meanings.

(1) Hadith: "Pray as you see me praying" from Ibn al-Huwayrith who said: "We came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) while we were young men of similar age, and we stayed with him for twenty nights. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was merciful and kind, and he thought that we had missed our families, so he asked about who from our families (remained) and we told him. He said: 'Return to your families, stay with them, teach them, and command them to pray such-and-such prayer at such-and-such time and such-and-such prayer at such-and-such time. And when the prayer is present, let one of you call the adhan, and let the oldest among you lead you, and pray as you see me praying.'" Narrated by al-Bukhari, and they agreed upon it without his saying: "and pray..." etc.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the definition of 'amr' (command). It begins with a concluding remark from a previous discussion about ownership and inviolability, then transitions to the chapter on command. The Hanafi position (stated first) is that the term 'amr' specifically refers to a fixed linguistic form—the imperative verb form (صيغة لازمة). Some scholars, identified as followers of Malik and al-Shafi‘i, disagree and hold that the actions of the Prophet (s) are also binding (موجبة) like commands. Their evidence: (1) the Quranic verse about the 'command of Pharaoh' is interpreted as his action, so action can be called 'amr'; (2) the Prophet's hadith 'Pray as you see me praying' makes following his action necessary, implying it is a command. The Hanafis rebut that words (العبارات) are specifically designed to convey meanings; it is not permissible for them to fall short of expressing the intended meanings. Therefore, commands are conveyed through speech, not through actions. The footnote provides the full text of the hadith and notes its abridged version in the agreed-upon narrations.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The concept of command; according to Hanafis, it is confined to a fixed linguistic form (صيغة لازمة) that indicates obligation.
صيغة لازمة (sīgha lāzima): A fixed verbal form, i.e., the imperative mood (like 'if'al').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It includes both verbal statements and actions of the Prophet (s).
B) It is confined to a fixed linguistic form (صيغة لازمة).
C) It is derived from the context, not limited to a form.
D) It refers only to the actions of the Prophet (s).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'amr' is strictly a fixed linguistic form; prophetic actions do not constitute commands unless accompanied by an explicit verbal imperative.

6 min
The Imperative Form is Binding and Specific
Establishing that the imperative form is binding and specific, refuting the ambiguous view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the imperative form's binding nature and evaluate the counterargument from its varied Quranic uses.

Core Idea
The imperative form defaults to obligation; its varied uses in the Quran are contextual, not inherent ambiguity.

This passage addresses a foundational principle in Hanafi usul: the imperative form (sīghat al-amr) is specific and binding. The majority of jurists hold that a command obligates action without requiring additional evidence, while a minority argue it is ambiguous due to its multiple uses. We will explore the proof from the Prophet's command and the Hanafi response to the counterargument.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The imperative form is specific (khass) and binding. It obligates the commanded action without needing extra evidence. This is the view of the majority of jurists.
2
Examine the proof from hadith
The Prophet's command 'صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي' (Pray as you have seen me pray) uses the imperative form and calls for compliance, demonstrating its binding nature.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyze the opponent's argument
Opponents claim the imperative is ambiguous (mujmal) because it appears in many meanings (obligation, recommendation, permission, etc.) in the Quran. They argue that extra evidence is needed to determine which meaning applies.
Examples: 'أقيموا الصلاة' (obligation), 'وابتغوا من فضل الله' (recommendation), 'فاصطادوا' (permission).
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The majority responds that these varied uses are due to contextual qara'in (indications), not inherent ambiguity. The original linguistic default (asl al-wad') of the imperative is specificity. Polysemy is an exception caused by external factors.
In the absence of a qarina, the imperative remains binding.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a command is given without context, a Hanafi holds it is obligatory; the opponent holds it is suspended until evidence clarifies. This affects daily fiqh rulings like interpreting hadith commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ربي ويسقيني (١). فثبت أن صيغة الأمر لازمة. وس ولا ننكر تسميته مجازاً؛ لأن الفعل يجب به، فسُمِّي به مجازاً، والنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام دعا إلى الموافقة بلفظ الأمر بقوله: «صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي، فدل أن صيغة الأمر لازمةٌ. ومن ذلك : باب موجب الأمر : ***** (١) حديث النهي عن الوصال: عن ابن عمر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الوصال، قالوا: إنك تواصل ؟ قال : إني لستُ كهيئتكم، إني أُطعم وأُسقى». وفي رواية: «لستُ مثلكم». متفق عليه وللبخاري: «أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم واصل : فواصل الناس فشق عليهم، فنهاهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يواصلوا، قالوا: إنك تواصل ؟ قال : لستُ كهيئتكم، إني أظلُّ أُطعم وأسقى». وأخرج الموطأ وأبو داود الرواية الأولى. وعن أنس قال: «واصل رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في آخر شهر رمضان، فواصل ناس من المسلمين، فبلغه ذلك ، فقال : لو مُدَّ لنا الشهر لواصلنا وصالاً يدع المتعمقون تعمقهم، إنكم لستم مثلي، أو قال: لستُ مثلكم، إني أظل يطعمني ربي ويسقيني». وفي رواية : قال : قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا تواصلوا. قالوا: إنك تواصل؟ قال: لستُ كأحد منكم إني أبيت أطعم وأسقى». أخرجه البخاري ومسلم. وأخرج الترمذي الثانية، وقال: «إن ربي يطعمني ويسقيني». وأخرجاه من حديث أبي هريرة، وفيه أني أبيت يطعمني ربي ويسقيني. باب موجب الأمر و وإذا ثبت خصوص الصيغة : ثبت خصوص المراد في أصل الوضع، وهو قول عامة الفقهاء. ومن الناس من قال : إنه مُجمَل في حق الحكم، لا يجب به حكم إلا بدليل زائد، واحتجوا بأن صيغة الأمر استعملت في معان مختلفة : للإيجاب، مثل قوله تعالى: أَقِيمُوا الصَّلوةَ ( الأنعام / ۷۲. وللندب، مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَابْتَغُوا مِن فَضْلِ اللَّهِ ﴾ الجمعة / ١٠ . - وللإباحة، مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْهُمْ فَأَصْطَادُوا المائدة / ٢ . وللتقريع مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاسْتَفْزِزْ مَنِ اسْتَطَعْتَ مِنْهُم بِصَوْتِكَ ) الإسراء / ٦٤ . - وللتوبيخ مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِن وَمَن شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ ) الكهف / ٢٩ . فإذا اختلفت وجوهه : لم يجب العمل به إلا بدليل زائد. و ولعامة العلماء : أن صيغة الأمر لفظ خاص من تصاريف الفعل، فكما أن العبارات لا تقصر عن المعاني، فكذلك العبارات في أصل الوضع مختصة بالمراد، ولا يثبت الاشتراك إلا بعارض، فكذلك صيغة الأمر
My Lord gives me food and drink (1). Thus it is established that the imperative form is binding. And we do not deny that it is called metaphorical; because the action becomes obligatory by it, so it is named metaphorically. The Prophet, peace be upon him, called to compliance with the imperative wording by his saying: 'Pray as you have seen me pray,' so it indicates that the imperative form is binding. And from that: Chapter on the requirement of the command. ***** (1) Hadith forbidding continuous fasting: from Ibn Umar that the Prophet, peace be upon him, forbade continuous fasting. They said: 'But you practice continuous fasting?' He said: 'I am not like you; I am fed and given drink.' In another narration: 'I am not like you.' Agreed upon. And in Bukhari: 'The Prophet practiced continuous fasting, so the people practiced continuous fasting and it became hard on them. So the Messenger of Allah forbade them from continuous fasting. They said: But you practice continuous fasting? He said: I am not like you; I spend the night being fed and given drink.' Al-Muwatta and Abu Dawud narrated the first narration. And from Anas: 'The Messenger of Allah practiced continuous fasting at the end of Ramadan, so some Muslims practiced continuous fasting. That reached him, so he said: If the month were extended for us, we would practice continuous fasting such that the excessive ones would leave their excess. You are not like me, or he said: I am not like you. I spend the night being fed and given drink by my Lord.' In another narration: He said: The Prophet said: 'Do not practice continuous fasting.' They said: But you practice continuous fasting? He said: 'I am not like any of you; I spend the night being fed and given drink.' Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. At-Tirmidhi narrated the second, saying: 'My Lord feeds me and gives me drink.' And they both narrated from Abu Huraira, in which: 'I spend the night being fed and given drink by my Lord.' Chapter on the requirement of the command. When the specificity of the form is established, the specificity of the intended meaning in the original usage is established, and this is the saying of the majority of jurists. Some people said: It is ambiguous regarding the ruling; no ruling becomes obligatory by it except with additional evidence. They argued that the imperative form is used in different meanings: for obligation, like His statement: 'Establish prayer' (al-An'am 72); for recommendation, like His statement: 'Seek from the bounty of Allah' (al-Jumu'ah 10); for permission, like His statement: 'And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt' (al-Ma'idah 2); for rebuke, like His statement: 'And incite whom you can with your voice' (al-Isra 64); for reprimand, like His statement: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' (al-Kahf 29). So if its aspects differ, action by it is not required except with additional evidence. And the majority of scholars hold that the imperative form is a specific word from the conjugations of the verb. Just as expressions do not fall short of meanings, similarly expressions in original usage are specific to the intended meaning, and commonality (polysemy) is only established by a hindrance; likewise the imperative form.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a dispute. It first establishes that the imperative form (صيغة الأمر) is binding (لازمة), meaning it obligates the action it commands. The proof is the Prophet's statement 'Pray as you have seen me pray,' which is a command that required compliance. The author notes that calling it metaphorical does not negate its binding force; rather, it is metaphorically named because the action becomes obligatory by it. Then the chapter on 'موجب الأمر' begins: once the specificity of the form is established, the intended meaning in original usage is specific—this is the view of the majority of jurists (عامة الفقهاء). Some opponents claim that the imperative is ambiguous (مُجمَل) and needs extra evidence to determine obligation, citing its various uses in the Quran: for obligation, recommendation, permission, rebuke, and reprimand. The majority responds that the imperative is a specific verbal form; just as expressions inherently convey specific meanings, polysemy only arises due to contextual factors (عارض). Thus, in the absence of such factors, the imperative defaults to obligation. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul: the imperative is qat'i in its demand unless a qarina indicates otherwise.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (sīghat al-amr): The imperative verbal form, which is considered binding (لازمة) and specific (خاص) in meaning according to the majority.
مُجمَل (mujmal): Verbally ambiguous, not indicating a specific ruling without additional clarifying evidence.
خاص (khāṣṣ): Specific, unambiguous, indicating a particular meaning without need for external evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of scholars (عامة الفقهاء) in this passage, what is the status of the imperative form (صيغة الأمر)?
A) It is ambiguous (مُجمَل) and needs additional evidence to obligate action.
B) It is specific (خاص) and binding, obligating action without extra evidence.
C) It is metaphorical and has no legal effect.
D) It is only used for recommendation (ندب).

Key Takeaway
The imperative form (صيغة الأمر) is specific and binding; it obligates action by default, and its varied uses in revelation are due to contextual factors.

The Imperative Form: Generality and Repetition - Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute over whether imperative form indicates generality and repetition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether the imperative form implies generality and repetition, apply the relevant usul principles to a divorce scenario, and evaluate the opposing Shafiʿi argument.

Core Idea
The imperative form does not inherently imply generality or repetition; it is a singular expression requiring only the minimum fulfillment.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational usul dispute: does the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr) indicate that the commanded action must be done in all instances (ʿumūm) or repeated (tikrār)? The Hanafi school argues that the command alone does not; rather, it applies to the least of the action. Al-Shafiʿi holds that it at least admits probability. We will analyze the proofs, including the hadith of al-Aqraʿ ibn Ḥābis, and see how this affects a practical example like divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the positions
First, identify the two main positions: Hanafi (no generality/repetition) and Shafiʿi (admits probability). Also note the intermediate views.
The Hanafi position is that the imperative applies to the minimum (aqall jinsihī).
2
Examine the proofs for each side
Look at the proofs presented: the Hanafi proof from singular nature of the verb (lafẓ fard) and Qur'anic examples; the Shafiʿi proof from the infinitive as a generic noun; the third view using explicit texts.
Focus on the dalīl of each school.
3
Analyze the key evidence (hadith)
Analyze the hadith of al-Aqraʿ ibn Ḥābis. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'Hajj is once.' Understand how this proves that the command does not imply repetition.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but qatʿī al-dalāla on this point.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply the dispute to the divorce example: 'Divorce yourself.' Note the different rulings: Hanafi = one divorce; Shafiʿi = probable for multiple; other view = three.
This shows the practical consequence of the usul principle.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi position is preferred (muftā bihi) according to the majority of Hanafi scholars.
The tarjih is based on linguistic analysis and the apparent meaning of texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب
موجب الأمر في معنى العموم والتكرار
وقال بعضهم : صيغة الأمر توجب العموم والتكرار.
وقال بعضهم : لا ، بل تحتمله، وهو قول الشافعي وقال بعض مشايخنا لا توجبه ولا تحتمله إلا أن يكون معلقاً بشرط ، أو مخصوصاً بوصف. وقال عامة مشايخنا : لا توجبه ولا تحتمله بكل حال، غير أن الأمر بالفعل يقع على أقل جنسه، ويحتمل كله بدليله.
مثال هذا الأصل : رجل قال لامرأته : طلقي نفسك، أو قال ذلك لأجنبي، فإن ذلك واقع على الثلاث عند بعضهم.
وعند الشافعي : يحتمل الثلاث، والمثنى.
وعندنا يقع على الواحدة، إلا أن ينوي الكل.
وجه القول الأول: أن لفظ الأمر مختصر من طلب الفعل بالمصدر الذي هو اسم لجنس الفعل، والمختصر من الكلام والمطوّل سواء، واسم الفعل : اسم عام لجنسه ، فوجب العمل بعمومه، كسائر ألفاظ العموم. ووجه قول الشافعي هو ما ذكرنا غير أن المصدر اسم نكرة في موضع الإثبات، فأوجب الخصوص على احتمال العموم. ألا يُرى أن نية الثلاث صحيحة، وهو عدد لا محالة، فكذلك المثنى ألا يُرى إلى قول الأقرع بن حابس في السؤال عن الحج : ألعامنا هذا، أم للأبد ؟
ووجه القول الثالث : الاستدلال بالنصوص الواردة في الكتاب والسنة. مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَقِمِ الصَّلَوةَ لِدُلُوكِ الشَّمْسِ إِلَى غَسَقِ الَّيْلِ ﴾ [الإسراء/78]، ﴿ وَإِن كُنتُمْ جُنُباً فَأَطَهَرُوا ﴾ [المائدة/6]. واحتج من ادعى التكرار بحديث الأقرع بن حابس حين قال في الحج: «ألعامنا هذا يا رسول الله، أم للأبد ؟ فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: بل للأبد». فلو لم يحتمل اللفظ: لما أشكل عليه.
ولنا: أن لفظ الأمر صيغة اختصرت لمعناها من طلب الفعل، لكن لفظ الفعل فرد، وكذلك سائر الأسماء المفردة والمصادر.
(هامش) حديث الأقرع بن حابس عن ابن عباس قال: «خطبنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: يا أيها الناس كتب عليكم الحج، فقام الأقرع بن حابس فقال : أفي كل عام يا رسول الله؟ فقال: لو قلتها لوجبت، ولو وجبت لم تعملوا بها أو لم تستطيعوا أن تعملوا بها، الحج مرة، فمن زاد فهو تطوع». رواه أحمد والنسائي بمعناه. ولمسلم والنسائي عن أبي هريرة قال: «خطبنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: يا أيها الناس قد فُرض عليكم الحج فحُجُّوا، فقال رجل: أكل عام يا رسول الله؟ فسكت حتى قالها ثلاثاً، فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: لو قلت: نعم لوجبت ولما استطعتم».
Chapter: The Import of the Command in the Sense of Generality and Repetition. Some said: The imperative form necessitates generality and repetition. Some said: No, rather it admits it, and this is the saying of al-Shafi'i. And some of our shaykhs said: It neither necessitates nor admits it unless it is suspended on a condition or specified by a description. And the generality of our shaykhs said: It neither necessitates nor admits it in any case, except that the command for an action falls on the least of its kind, and it admits the whole by its evidence. An example of this principle: A man said to his wife: 'Divorce yourself,' or he said that to a stranger, then that falls as three according to some. According to al-Shafi'i: It admits three and two. According to us, it falls as one, unless he intends the whole. The proof of the first saying: That the word 'command' is abbreviated from the request of the action by the infinitive which is a name for the genus of the action, and the abbreviated form of speech and the extended are equal, and the noun of action is a general name for its genus, so it is necessary to act upon its generality, like other general expressions. The proof of al-Shafi'i's saying is what we mentioned, except that the infinitive is an indefinite noun in a positive position, so it necessitates specificity with the possibility of generality. Do you not see that the intention of three is valid, and it is a number without doubt, so likewise two. Do you not see the saying of al-Aqra' ibn Habis in his question about Hajj: 'Is it for this year or forever?' The proof of the third saying: The deduction from the texts in the Book and the Sunnah, such as His saying, 'Establish prayer at the decline of the sun until the darkness of the night' and 'if you are in a state of janabah, purify yourselves.' And the one who claimed repetition argued by the hadith of al-Aqra' ibn Habis when he said about Hajj: 'Is it for this year, O Messenger of Allah, or forever?' He said, 'Rather, forever.' If the expression did not admit it, it would not have been ambiguous to him. And for us: The word 'command' is a form abbreviated for its meaning from the request of the action, but the word 'action' is a singular, and likewise all other singular nouns and infinitives. (Margin note: Hadith of al-Aqra' ibn Habis...).
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute regarding the import of the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr). The chapter is titled 'The Import of the Command in the Sense of Generality and Repetition.' Several positions are recorded. Some scholars hold that the imperative form necessitates both generality (al-ʿumūm) and repetition (al-tikrār). Others, including al-Shafiʿi, hold that it only admits probability of generality/repetition without necessity. Some early Hanafi mashāyikh (shaykhs) restrict this to commands that are conditional or qualified. The majority of Hanafi mashāyikh (ʿāmmat mashāyikhnā) hold that the imperative form neither necessitates nor even admits generality/repetition inherently; rather, the command for an action applies only to the minimum that fulfills it (aqall jinsihī), and it may imply all instances only if supported by an external evidence. The passage then illustrates with a fiqh example: a man telling his wife 'Divorce yourself' or saying this to a stranger. According to the first view, this would effect three divorces. According to al-Shafiʿi, it is probable for three or two. According to the Hanafi school, it effects only one divorce unless the man intended all three. Then the proofs are given. The first view argues that the command is an abbreviation of a request using the infinitive (maṣdar), which is a generic noun, hence it carries generality. The Shafiʿi proof notes that the infinitive is an indefinite noun (nakira fī mawḍiʿ al-ithbāt), which suggests specificity while also admitting generality, as evidenced by the validity of intending three or two. The third view argues from explicit texts of Qur'an and Sunnah, like the prayers times and the command for purification, which are not repeated. The demand for repetition is refuted by the hadith of al-Aqraʿ ibn Ḥābis on Hajj, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicated that the command is for once, not annually. The Hanafi response is that the imperative form is a singular expression, and like other singulars, it does not imply repetition or generality without external evidence. The margin provides the full hadith: 'Hajj is once, and whoever adds is doing voluntary.' This is a key usul difference between the Hanafis and Shafiʿis on the nature of commands.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (ṣīghat al-amr): The imperative grammatical form used to express a command, e.g., ifʿal (do)
العموم (al-ʿumūm): Generality; the quality of a term covering all instances of its reference
التكرار (al-tikrār): Repetition; the implication that the commanded action must be performed multiple times

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Three divorces
B) One divorce
C) Two divorces
D) It depends on the wife's intention

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the imperative form does not inherently imply generality or repetition; it requires only the minimum fulfillment unless evidence indicates otherwise.

Does the Imperative Imply Repetition? The Hanafi Position on a Single Action
Analyzing the dispute on imperative implying repetition vs. singularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the imperative implies a single action and identify the linguistic basis for this view from the source text.

Core Idea
The imperative commands a single action; repetition requires a separate textual indicator.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute: does the imperative (amr) inherently demand repetition or only a single occurrence? The Hanafi school, represented by al-Bazdawi, argues that the imperative form is a singular construction and therefore indicates one action. The famous hadith of al-Aqra' ibn Habis about Hajj is used by opponents to claim repetition, but the Hanafi response shows that the Prophet's answer clarifies the perpetual nature of the obligation, not annual repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Recognize that the dispute is about the default implication of the imperative form: does it require repetition (takrar) or a single occurrence (fard)?
This is a linguistic usul issue, not a matter of the specific act commanded.
2
State the Hanafi Position and Its Evidence
The Hanafi position is that the imperative implies a single action. Evidence includes the linguistic analysis that the verbal noun (masdar) is singular and does not indicate multiplicity, and the hadith 'Hajj is once.'
The hadith is a clear dalil that the command for Hajj is fulfilled once.
3
Identify the Opposing Argument
Opponents cite the hadith where al-Aqra' asked 'Is it for this year or forever?' and the Prophet replied 'Rather forever.' They argue this shows the imperative can imply repetition.
The key is how 'forever' is interpreted.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response is that the Prophet's answer means the obligation remains for all time (abadiyyah), not that it must be repeated annually. The question itself proves the imperative is ambiguous, not that it implies repetition.
Linguistically, the singular form cannot bear multiplicity.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: if the imperative implied repetition, Hajj would be obligatory every year. According to the Hanafi view, it is obligatory once in a lifetime.
This is a significant difference in fiqh application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) باب موجب الأمر في معنى الخصوص والعموم: حديث الأقرع بن حابس: عن ابن عباس قال: «خطبنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: يا أيها الناس كتب عليكم الحج، فقام الأقرع بن حابس فقال: أفي كل عام يا رسول الله؟ فقال: لو قلتها: لوجبت، ولو وجبت لم تعملوا بها أو لم تستطيعوا أن تعملوا بها، الحج مرة، فمن زاد فهو تطوع. رواه أحمد والنسائي بمعناه. ولمسلم والنسائي عن أبي هريرة قال: «خطبنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقال: يا أيها الناس قد فرض عليكم الحج فحجوا، فقال رجل: أكل عام يا رسول الله؟ فسكت حتى قالها ثلاثا، فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: لو قلت: نعم: لوجبت ولما استطعتم. تطليقا مثل قول الرجل: طلقي: أي أوقعي الطلاق أو طلاقا، أو افعلي أو التطليق، وهما اسمان، فردان ليسا بصيغتي جمع ولا عدد وبين الفرد والعدد تناف فكما لا يحتمل العدد معنى الفرد لا يحتمل الفرد معنى العدد أيضا. وكذلك الأمر بسائر الأفعال أيضا، كقولك اضرب أي اكتس ضربا أو الضرب، وهو فرد بمنزلة: زيد وعمرو، وبكر، فلا يحتمل العدد، إلا أنه اسم جنس، له كل، وبعض، فالبعض منه الذي هو أقله: فرد حقيقة وحكما. وأما الطلقات الثلاث فليست بفرد حقيقة بل هي أجزاء متعددة، ولكنها فرد حكما؛ لأنها جنس واحد، فصارت من طريق الجنس واحدا. ألا يرى أنك إذا عددت الأجناس: كان هذا بأجزائه واحدا، فصار هذا واحدا من حيث هو جنس وله أبعاض، كالإنسان فرد من حيث هو آدمي، لكنه ذو أجزاء متعددة، فصار هذا الاسم الفرد واقعا على الكل بصفة أنه واحد، لكن الأقل فرد حقيقة وحكما من كل وجه، وكان أولى بالاسم الفرد عند إطلاقه، والآخر محتملا. فأما قَدْراً من الأقدار المتخللة بين الحدين: فلا.
Do you not see the statement of al-Aqra' ibn Habis when asking about Hajj: 'Is it for this year, or forever?' And the argument of the third opinion: deriving evidence from the texts in the Book and Sunnah, such as His statement 'Establish prayer from the decline of the sun until the darkness of the night' (17:78) and 'If you are in a state of major impurity, purify yourselves' (5:6). And those who claimed repetition argued with the hadith of al-Aqra' ibn Habis when he said about Hajj: 'Is it for this year, O Messenger of Allah, or forever?' He (peace be upon him) said: 'Rather forever.' For if the word did not bear [the possibility of repetition], it would not have been ambiguous to him. And our argument: the imperative form is a formula coined for its meaning of requesting an action, but the word of the action is a singular unit, and likewise all singular nouns and verbal nouns. [Footnote] Chapter on the implication of the imperative in the sense of specificity and generality: Hadith of al-Aqra' ibn Habis from Ibn Abbas: 'The Messenger of Allah addressed us and said: O people, Hajj has been prescribed for you. So al-Aqra' ibn Habis stood and said: Every year, O Messenger of Allah? He said: Had I said it, it would have become obligatory, and if it were obligatory, you would not have been able to perform it. Hajj is once, so whoever adds is doing voluntary. Reported by Ahmad and al-Nasa'i with its meaning. And Muslim and al-Nasa'i from Abu Hurairah: ... Then he said: Triple divorce is like the statement of a man: 'Divorce' meaning effect divorce or a divorce, or do it, or the divorce, and they are two names, both singular, not plural forms nor numbers, and between the singular and number there is contradiction: just as the number does not bear the meaning of singular, the singular does not bear the meaning of number either. And similarly the imperative for all actions, like you say 'hit' meaning acquire hitting or the hitting, and it is a singular like Zayd, Amr, and Bakr, so it does not bear number, except that it is a generic noun having a whole and a part; the part of it which is the least is singular in reality and by legal judgment. As for triple divorces, they are not singular in reality but are multiple parts, yet they are singular by legal judgment because they are one genus, so they become one from the aspect of genus. Do you not see that when you count the genera, this becomes one with its parts, so it becomes one in that it is a genus having parts, like man is singular in that he is human, but he has multiple parts. Thus this singular name applies to the whole with the description that it is one, but the least is singular in reality and by legal judgment from every aspect, and it is more entitled to the singular name when used absolutely, while the other is probable. As for a quantity among the quantities interpolated between the two limits: then no.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents a classic Hanafi usul dispute regarding whether the imperative (amr) implies repetition (takrar) or a single occurrence (marrah wahidah). The author, al-Bazdawi, begins by referencing the famous hadith of al-Aqra' ibn Habis, who asked whether the obligation of Hajj is for one year or forever. The Prophet's response 'rather forever' is used by some to argue that the imperative implies repetition. However, the author counters by analyzing the linguistic form: the imperative is a singular construction (fard), and singulars do not inherently convey multiplicity. The author then elaborates on the concept of 'fard' in language, using examples like divorce and generic nouns to show that the default is one instance, though the singular can sometimes be used for a genus that includes parts. The final ruling, consistent with Hanafi usul, is that the imperative commands one action unless there is a separate textual indication for repetition.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative form that requests an action
الفرد (al-fard): A singular unit; in this context, the verbal noun (masdar) or action word that denotes a single instance
التكرار (al-takrar): Repetition; performing the commanded action multiple times on different occasions
الصيغة (al-sighah): The linguistic form or construction, specifically here the imperative form

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the imperative (amr) commands a single action; repetition must be proven by separate textual evidence, as illustrated by the hadith 'Hajj is once'.

The Singular Command Does Not Imply Plurality
Rule on singular commands not implying number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a singular command does not bear number and apply it to the examples of divorce (talliqi) and hitting (idrib), distinguishing between real singular and ruling singular for three divorces.

Core Idea
A singular command indicates one instance, not multiple.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi establishes a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul: when the Lawgiver or a speaker uses a singular form in a command, it does not imply a number. The default is the minimum, unless there is evidence for more. We will examine the ruling for divorce and hitting, and the special case of three divorces as a singular by genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The singular form in a command does not imply number. This is based on the linguistic opposition between singular and number.
Singular (fard) and number (adad) are mutually exclusive.
2
Analyze the divorce example
When a man says 'talliqi' (divorce!), the word is a singular verbal noun. It applies to the minimum, i.e., one divorce.
The same applies to 'idrib' (hit!) – one hit.
3
Understand the three divorces exception
Three divorces are multiple in reality but considered singular in ruling because they belong to one genus. However, the minimum (one divorce) is more entitled to the unqualified singular.
The singular can cover the whole genus by indication, but the default is the minimum.
4
Apply the exclusion of intermediate numbers
What is between the minimum and the whole is pure number and not covered by the singular.
For example, two divorces are not implied by the singular command 'talliqi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تطليقاً مثل قول الرجل : طلقي : أي أوقعي الطلاق أو طلاقاً، أو افعلي أو التطليق، وهما اسمان ،فردان ليسا بصيغتي جمع ولا عدد وبين الفرد والعدد تناف فكما لا يحتمل العددُ معنى الفرد لا يحتمل الفرد معنى العدد أيضاً. وكذلك الأمر بسائر الأفعال أيضاً، كقولك اضرب أي اكتس ضرباً أو الضرب، وهو فرد بمنزلة : زيد وعمرو، وبكر، فلا يحتمل العدد، إلا أنه اسم جنس ، له كُلُّ ،وبعض، فالبعض منه الذي هو أقله : فرد حقيقة وحكماً. وأما الطلقات الثلاث فليست بفردِ حقيقة بل هي أجزاء متعددة، ولكنها فرد حكماً ؛ لأنها جنس واحد ، فصارت من طريق الجنس واحداً. ألا يُرى أنك إذا عددت الأجناس : كان هذا بأجزائه واحداً، فصار هذا واحداً من حيث هو جنس وله أبعاض، كالإنسان فرد من حيث هو آدمي، لكنه ذو أجزاء متعددة، فصار هذا الاسم الفرد واقعاً على الكل بصفة أنه واحد، لكن الأقل فرد حقيقةً وحكماً من كل وجه، وكان أولى بالاسم الفرد عند إطلاقه، والآخر محتملاً. فأما ما بين الأقل والكل : فعدد محض، ليس بفرد حقيقةً ولا حكماً، ولا صورةً ولا معنى، فلم يحتمله الفرد، وكذلك سائر أسماء الأجناس إذا كانت فرداً صيغة أو دلالة. أما الفرد صيغةً فمثل قول الرجل : والله لا أشرب ماء، أو : الماء: أنه يقع على الأقل، ويحتمل الكل، فأما قَدْراً من الأقدار المتخللة بين الحدين : فلا. وكذلك : لا أكل طعاماً، وما يُشبهه. وأما الفرد دلالةً فمثل قول الرجل : والله لا أتزوج النساء، ولا أشتري العبيد، ولا أكلم بني آدم ولا أشتري الثياب: أن ذلك يقع على الأقل، ويحتمل الكل؛ لأن هذا جمع صار مجازاً عن اسم الجنس؛ لأنا إذا بقيناه جمعاً لغا حرفُ العهد أصلاً، وإذا جعلناه جنساً بقيت اللام لتعريف الجنس، وبقي معنى الجمع من وجه في الجنس، فكان الجنس أولى. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ مِنْ بَعْدُ ﴾ الأحزاب/٥٢، وذلك لا يختص بالجمع، فصار هذا وسائر أسماء الجنس سواء. وإنما أشكل على الأقرع بن حابس ؛ لأنه اعتبر ذلك بسائر العبادات، والله أعلم. وعلى هذا يُخرَّج أن كل اسم فاعل دلّ على المصدر لغةً، مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَأَقَطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا ﴾ المائدة/٣٨: لم يحتمل العددَ ، حتى قلنا لا يجوز أن يُراد بالآية إلا الأيمان ؛ لأن كل السرقات غير مرادة بالإجماع، فصار الواحد مراداً، وبالفعل الواحد لا تُقطع إلا يدٌ واحدة، وموجب الأمر على ما فسرنا يتنوع نوعين، وكل نوع منهما يتنوع نوعين، وهذا تنويع في صفة الحكم، والله أعلم. وهذا باب يُلقب بـ : بيان صفة حكم الأمر:
As a verbal noun like the statement of a man: 'talliqi' meaning 'effect divorce' or 'a divorce', or 'do it' or 'the divorcing', and these are two nouns, both singular, not forms of plural or number, and between the singular and number there is opposition. So just as number does not bear the meaning of singular, the singular also does not bear the meaning of number. Similarly the command in all other actions, like your saying 'idrib' meaning 'acquire a hitting' or 'the hitting', and it is singular like Zayd, Amr, and Bakr, so it does not bear number, except that it is a generic noun which has a whole and a part. So the part which is its minimum is singular in reality and in ruling. As for the three divorces, they are not singular in reality but are multiple parts, but they are singular in ruling because they are one genus, so they become one via genus. Do you not see that if you count the genera, this with its parts is one? So this becomes one insofar as it is a genus having parts, like the human is singular insofar as he is a human, but he has multiple parts. So this singular noun applies to the whole with the description that it is one, but the minimum is singular in reality and in ruling from every aspect, and it is more entitled to the singular noun when it is unqualified, while the other is probable. As for what is between the minimum and the whole: it is pure number, not singular in reality nor in ruling, nor in form nor in meaning, so the singular does not bear it. Likewise all other generic nouns when they are singular in form or in indication. As for the singular in form, like the statement of a man: 'By Allah, I will not drink water' or 'water' – it applies to the minimum and also potentially to the whole, but as for a quantity intermediate between the two limits, it does not. Similarly: 'I will not eat food' and what resembles it. And as for the singular in indication, like the statement of a man: 'By Allah, I will not marry women', 'I will not buy slaves', 'I will not speak to the children of Adam', 'I will not buy clothes' – that it applies to the minimum and potentially to the whole, because this plural has become a metaphor for the generic noun; for if we retain it as a plural, the letter of covenant (al) becomes entirely invalid, but if we make it a genus, the lam remains for definition of the genus, and the meaning of plurality remains in some respect in the genus, so the genus is more appropriate. Allah Most High says: 'Women are not permissible for you after this' (al-Ahzab 52), and that is not specific to a plurality, so this becomes equal to all generic nouns. It was problematic for al-Aqra' ibn Habis because he considered it like other acts of worship, and Allah knows best. And upon this basis it is to be considered that every active participle that indicates the verbal noun linguistically, like His statement: 'The thief, male and female, cut off their hands' (al-Ma'ida 38): it does not bear number, so we say it is not permissible to intend by the verse except the minimum, because all thefts are not intended by consensus, so the one is intended, and by a single act only one hand is cut. And the necessary aspect of the command as we have explained divides into two types, each type dividing into two, and this is a division in the attribute of the ruling. And Allah knows best. This is a chapter titled: Explanation of the Attribute of the Ruling of Command.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh concerning the interpretation of singular forms in commands and oaths. It opens by explaining that a singular verbal noun like 'talliqi' (a command to divorce) is a singular form and therefore does not imply a plural number. The Hanafi position is that the singular form carries the minimum (aqall) and potentially the whole (kull), but not intermediate quantities. Thus, when a man says to his wife 'talliqi', it means one divorce, not three. The same principle applies to commands like 'idrib' (hit) – it means one hit. Then the text discusses the case of three divorces (talaqat thalath): although they are multiple parts, they are considered singular in ruling because they belong to one genus. But the minimum (one divorce) is singular both in reality and in ruling, and is thus more entitled to the unqualified singular noun. Next, the text covers oaths: 'I will not drink water' covers the minimum one drink (a handful) and potentially all water, but not an intermediate portion. Similarly, collective nouns like 'women' in the form of a plural (al-nisa') are treated as metaphors for the generic noun, so the oath 'I will not marry women' applies to one woman and potentially all, but not an intermediate number. The Quranic verse 'Women are not permissible for you after this' is cited to show that the plural 'al-nisa' is not specific to a plurality; it is like other generic nouns. Finally, the verse about thieves: 'al-sariq wal-sariqah' is a singular active participle that indicates the verbal noun (sariq = thief, implying the act of theft). It does not bear number, so the minimum is intended: one theft, thus one hand is cut. The ruling of the command (amr) is then said to divide into types, which is the topic of the next chapter. Throughout, the Hanafi approach emphasizes that the singular form is zanni (probable) in its scope, but the default is the minimum unless there is evidence for more.

Key Terms
الفرد (al-fard): The singular form of a noun or verb that denotes one unit; in commands and oaths it implies the minimum (one instance) and may potentially cover the whole genus, but not intermediate numbers.
العدد (al-adad): A number comprising more than one; it stands in opposition to the singular and does not bear the meaning of the singular.
اسم الجنس (ism al-jins): A generic noun that denotes a category or kind, such as 'water' or 'food'; it applies to the minimum and potentially to the entire genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi interpretation in this passage, what is the ruling when a man says to his wife 'talliqi' (divorce)?
A) It implies three divorces.
B) It implies one divorce.
C) It implies both one and three divorces equally.
D) It implies no divorce until explained.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a singular command indicates one instance, not multiple; three divorces are only included by ruling, not by literal meaning.

Singular Forms in Oaths and Generic Nouns
Rule on singular forms in oaths and generic nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the singular form in oaths and generic nouns covers the minimum and potentially the whole, but not intermediate amounts, using the examples of water, food, women, and the thief verse.

Core Idea
In oaths, singular nouns indicate the minimum and may indicate the whole, but not intermediate quantities.

The second part of the passage extends the same principle to oaths and generic nouns. Whether the noun is singular in form (like 'water') or singular in indication (like 'women' used as a generic), the ruling is the same: the minimum is intended, and the whole is possible, but intermediate numbers are excluded. The Quranic verse on the thief ('al-sariq') further illustrates that an active participle does not bear number, so one theft results in cutting one hand.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between singular in form and singular in indication
A noun can be singular in form (e.g., 'water') or singular in indication (e.g., 'women' used generically). Both follow the same rule.
Singular in form: the word itself is singular. Singular in indication: the word is plural but used as a generic noun.
2
Apply to oaths with singular nouns in form
When someone swears 'I will not drink water', the singular 'water' applies to the minimum (a handful) and potentially all water, not an intermediate amount.
The same applies to 'food' and similar nouns.
3
Apply to oaths with plural nouns used as generic
When someone swears 'I will not marry women', the plural 'women' is interpreted as a metaphor for the generic noun 'woman'. It applies to one woman and potentially all women, not an intermediate number.
The Quranic verse 33:52 supports this interpretation.
4
Analyze the active participle 'al-sariq' (the thief)
The word 'al-sariq' is an active participle that indicates the verbal noun (masdar) – the act of theft. It does not bear number, so the minimum (one theft) is intended, resulting in cutting one hand.
This is based on consensus that the verse does not intend all thefts.

الفرد (al-fard): The singular form of a noun or verb that denotes one unit; in commands and oaths it implies the minimum (one instance) and may potentially cover the whole genus, but not intermediate numbers.
العدد (al-adad): A number comprising more than one; it stands in opposition to the singular and does not bear the meaning of the singular.

A) The oath is broken because two handfuls is more than the minimum.
