Hajj Timing: Mudayyaq vs Musawwa' and the Absolute Command
Dispute on whether absolute command requires immediate performance, applied to Hajj timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that an absolute command (amr mutlaq) does not require immediate performance (fawr) to the dispute over whether Hajj is mudayyaq or musawwa', and evaluate the proofs for each position.

Core Idea
An absolute command does not demand immediate action; Hajj's timing is debated between immediate binding (mudayyaq) and lifetime scope (musawwa').

This passage dives into a classical usul dispute: does the general command 'Perform Hajj' obligate you in the very first year, or does it allow delay? The answer hinges on whether an absolute command (amr mutlaq) implies fawr (immediacy) or tarakhi (delay). The Hanafi school internally disagrees: Abu Yusuf says Hajj is mudayyaq (narrow), Muhammad says it is musawwa' (expansive). We will examine their proofs and the majority's stance on the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions on Hajj timing
Abu Yusuf holds that the first available Hajj season is specified for performance; Muhammad holds that the time is the entire lifetime. Write down each position clearly.
2
Trace the connection to amr mutlaq
Al-Karkhi links the dispute to the general principle: does an absolute command require immediate action? See how Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's views on Hajj reflect their broader positions on amr mutlaq.
The principle is debated separately from Hajj itself.
3
Analyze Muhammad's proof
Muhammad argues that if the first year were specified, supererogatory (nafl) Hajj would not be permissible in that same year, just as nafl fasting is not allowed in Ramadan. This shows the time is not exclusive.
Also uses analogy to qada' fasts.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that the command addresses the mukallaf in the first possible time; this time is unique because reaching another season is uncertain due to possible death. Uncertainty does not create a competitor.
Analogizes to the time of Zuhr prayer.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The majority of Hanafi scholars (عامة مشايخنا) affirm that the absolute command does not require fawr, siding with Muhammad on the principle. However, the specific ruling on Hajj remains debated.
The passage states: 'والذي عليه عامة مشايخنا... أن الأمر المطلق لا يوجب الفور'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مؤدياً.
ولا خلاف في الوصف الأول، حتى إذا أخر عن العام الأول: كان
فأما الوصف الثاني : فهو صحيح عند أبي يوسف رحمه الله في الحال، وأشهر الحج في هذا العام الذي لَحِقَه الخطاب به : بمنزلة وقت الصلاة، فإذا أدرك العام الثاني : صار ذلك بمنزلة العام الأول، لا يصير كذلك إلا بشرط الإدراك.
وقال محمد رحمه الله : بل لا يتعين هذا الوقت للأداء، وإنما وقته العمر، فيسعُه التأخيرُ بشرط أن لا يفوته عن العمر.
هذا وأشهر الحج من العام بمنزلة يوم أدركه في حق قضاء رمضان، وإنما يُعرف بأن الحج يجب مضيقاً أو موسعاً : الأول.
فقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : مضيَّقاً، لا يسع فيه التأخيرُ عن العام
وقال محمد رحمه الله : يجب موسعاً يسع تأخيره عن العام الأول.
وقال الكرخي وجماعةٌ من مشايخنا رحمهم الله تعالى : إن هذا إلى أن الأمر المطلَقُ عن الوقت أيوجب الفور، أم لا؟
مثل وجوب الزكاة وصدقة الفطر والعُشر والنذر بالصدقة المطلقة. فقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : على الفور.
وقال محمد رحمه الله : على التراخي، فكذلك الحج، فأما تعين الوقت : فلا.
والذي عليه عامة مشايخنا رحمهم الله تعالى: أن الأمر المطلق لا يوجب الفور بلا خلاف.
ذلك محمد فمسألة مبتدأة: فذهب في رحمه الله
فأما مسألة الحج إلى أن الحج فرض العمر بلا خلاف، إلا أنه لا يُتأدى في كل عام إلا في وقت خاص، فيكون وقته نوعاً من أنواع أشهر الحج في عمره، وإليه تعيينه، كصوم القضاء وقته النُّهَر، دون الليالي، وإلى العبد تعيينه، فلا يتعين الذي يليه إلا بتعيينه بطريق الأداء.
ألا يُرى أنه متى أداه كان مؤدِّياً، ولو كان الأول متعيناً لصار بالتأخير مفوتاً.
والدليل عليه : أنه بقي وقتاً للنفل، مع أنه لم يُشرع في مدة واحدة إلا حج واحد، ولو تعين للفرض: لما بقي النفل مشروعاً، كما في شهر رمضان، فثبت أنه غير متعيّن إلا بالأداء، ومتى تعين بالأداء: لم يبق النفل فيه مشروعاً.
ولأبي يوسف رحمه الله : أن أشهر الحج من العام الأول متعيّنةُ للأداء، فلا يحل له التأخير عنها، كوقت الظهر للظهر.
وإنما قلنا هذا؛ لأن الخطاب بالأداء لحقه في هذا الوقت، وهو واحدٌ لا مزاحِمَ له؛ لأن المزاحمة لا تثبت إلا بإدراك وقت آخر، وهو مشكوك ؛ لأنه لا يدركه إلا بالحياة ، وإليه الحياة والممات في هذه المدة سواء في الاحتمال، فلا يثبت الإدراك بالشك، فبقي هذا الوقت متعيّناً بلا معارضة.
ويصير الساقط بطريق التعارض كالساقط بالحقيقة، فيصير كوقت الظهر في التقدير، بخلاف صوم القضاء؛ لأن تأخيره عن اليوم الأول لا
In performing. There is no disagreement regarding the first description, so that if one delays from the first year, he is... As for the second description: it is correct according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) immediately, and the months of Hajj in this year in which the address reached him are like the time of prayer. So if he reaches the second year, that becomes like the first year, but it does not become so except with the condition of reaching it. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Rather, this time is not specified for performance; rather its time is a lifetime. So delay is permissible for him on condition that he does not miss it during his lifetime. This, and the months of Hajj from the year are like the day he reached in the case of the makeup fast of Ramadan. And it is known whether Hajj is obligated as narrow or expansive: the first. Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Narrow, delay from the first year is not allowed. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is obligated as expansive, allowing its delay beyond the first year. And al-Karkhi and a group of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said: This returns to whether the absolute command, not tied to a time, requires immediate or not? Like the obligation of zakat, sadaqat al-fitr, 'ushr, and the vow of absolute charity. Abu Yusuf said: It requires immediate. And Muhammad said: It allows delay. So also Hajj. As for the specification of the time: it does not. And the position of the majority of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) is that the absolute command does not require immediate without disagreement. That Muhammad (with) a new issue: he went in (his view)... As for the issue of Hajj: Hajj is an obligation of a lifetime without disagreement, except that it is not performed in every year except at a specific time. So its time is a type of the months of Hajj in one's lifetime, and its specification is up to him, like the makeup fast: its time is the daytime, not the nights, and its specification is up to the servant. So the first following (year) is not specified except by his specification (intention) in the mode of performance. Do you not see that when he performs it, he is performing it, and if the first had been specified, he would by delaying become a neglector. And the proof for it: that it remained a time for supererogatory, despite that in one period only one Hajj is legislated. If it were specified for the obligatory, then the supererogatory would not remain legislated, as in the month of Ramadan. So it is established that it is not specified except by performance, and when it is specified by performance, the supererogatory does not remain legislated in it. And for Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him): The months of Hajj of the first year are specified for performance, so it is not permissible for him to delay from them, like the time of Zuhr for Zuhr. And we only say this because the address of performance reached him in this time, and it is one with no competitor; because competition is not established except by reaching another time, and it is doubtful; because he only reaches it by life, and life and death are equal in likelihood in this period. So reaching is not established by doubt. So this time remains specified without opposition. And the one that falls by way of opposition becomes like the one that falls in reality, so it becomes like the time of Zuhr in assessment, contrary to the makeup fast; because its delay from the first day does not...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute on whether the obligation of Hajj is immediately binding (mudayyaq) or expansive (musawwa'), and connects it to the broader usul principle of whether an absolute command (amr mutlaq) requires performance immediately (fawr) or allows delay (tarakhi). Two descriptions (wasf) are discussed: the first is agreed upon, the second is disputed. Abu Yusuf holds that the time of the first available Hajj season is specified for performance, like the time of Zuhr prayer; delaying it is not allowed. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the time is not specified—Hajj is an obligation over one's lifetime, and one may delay to a later year as long as it is performed before death. The dispute is then tied to the general principle: al-Karkhi and others relate it to whether an absolute command (unqualified by time) requires immediate or delayed action. Abu Yusuf opts for fawr, Muhammad for tarakhi. However, the majority of Hanafi scholars (including the later authorities) affirm that the absolute command does not require fawr. Muhammad provides a detailed proof: if the first year were specified, delaying would make one a transgressor; further, supererogatory Hajj remains permissible in the same year, which would not be the case if it were specified for the obligatory Hajj (like Ramadan). Abu Yusuf's proof is based on the idea that the address (khatab) is directed to the first available time, and there is no competitor; alternative times are uncertain due to the possibility of death, so doubt does not remove specification. The passage ends with an analogy to makeup fasts (qada' Ramadan) where delay is allowed over multiple days.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): A command that is not qualified by a specific time or condition.
الفور (al-fawr): Immediate performance without delay.
التراخي (al-tarākhī): Permissibility of delay in performing an obligation.
مضيق (muḍayyaq): An obligation that must be performed in the first available time or face sin.
موسع (musawwa‘): An obligation that can be performed at any time within a broader period without sin for delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a person does not perform Hajj in the first year after becoming obligated, what is the ruling?
A) It is permissible; he may delay to any later year.
B) It is not permissible; he is sinful for delaying.
C) It becomes obligatory to perform it immediately in the next year.
D) It is recommended but not obligatory to delay.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school internal dispute on Hajj timing (mudayyaq vs musawwa') is rooted in the broader usul principle that an absolute command does not require immediate performance.

Two Types of Prohibition and Their Legal Effect in Hanafi Usul
Defines two types of nahy and presents the Hanafi-Shafi‘i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prohibition of sensory acts and prohibition of shar‘ī transactions, and explain the Hanafi position that the latter remains legislated despite the prohibition.

Core Idea
Prohibition of a shar‘ī act does not nullify its legal existence—only its permissibility is negated.

The passage from the chapter on prohibition (bāb al-nahy) introduces a fundamental distinction in Hanafi usul: not all prohibitions render the prohibited act devoid of legal effect. While some prohibitions indicate inherent ugliness (sensory acts), others indicate ugliness due to an external attribute, leaving the act's legal essence intact. This distinction has far-reaching consequences for how we understand prohibited acts like fasting on Eid or invalid sales. Let us parse the two types and the dispute that arises between the Hanafi and Shafi‘i schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types of Prohibition
Read the first part of the passage where the author divides absolute prohibition (nahy mutlaq) into two categories: prohibition of sensory acts (af‘āl ḥissiyya) and prohibition of shar‘ī transactions (taṣarrufāt shar‘iyya). Note the examples given for each.
Sensory acts: adultery, murder, drinking wine. Shar‘ī transactions: fasting, prayer, sale, lease.
2
Understand the Different Implications for Each Type
For sensory acts, prohibition indicates ugliness in the act's very essence (qabīḥa fī anfusihā). For shar‘ī transactions, it indicates ugliness due to an external attribute (ma‘nan fī ghayrihī), so the act remains legislated (mashrū‘). Grasp the key contrast: inherent vs. external defect.
The phrase 'li-ma‘nan fī ghayrihī, muttaṣil bihī' is crucial: the ugliness is in something else but connected to the act.
3
Locate the Hanafi-Shafi‘i Dispute
After stating the Hanafi position, the passage quotes al-Shāfi‘ī: he holds that prohibition of shar‘ī transactions also indicates inherent ugliness, rendering the act unlegislated (lā yabqā mashrū‘an aṣlan) unless evidence proves otherwise. Identify the point of disagreement.
The dispute is whether the prohibition affects the essence (dhāt) or only an external attribute (ṣifa).
4
Analyze the Proofs and Examples
The Hanafi proof is exemplified by fasting on Eid, ribā, and invalid sales—they argue these acts have some legal rulings (like knowledge of prohibition, requirement to separate in sales) even though prohibited. Al-Shāfi‘ī argues from the literal meaning (ḥaqīqa) of prohibition, which obliges acting upon the ugliness. Consider how each school uses examples to support its view.
The text does not provide a full rebuttal of the Shafi‘i argument but relies on the examples as counter-evidence.
5
Recognize the Practical Consequences (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The difference matters in rulings: For fasting on Eid, Hanafis say the fast is forbidden but the day is not valid for fasting (so no qada required if one fasts? Actually the text says it remains mashru‘ li-ahkamiha, meaning some rulings apply; the Shafi‘i view is the act is invalid and abrogated, with no rulings). Understand how this distinction permeates many chapters of fiqh.
The thamarah here is whether the prohibited act carries any legal effect at all—e.g., does an invalid sale require separation? Hanafis say yes, Shafi‘is say no.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا الأصل: باب النهي: النهي المطلق نوعان: نهي عن الأفعال الحسية، مثل الزنا والقتل، وشرب الخمر. و نهي عن التصرفات الشرعية، مثل الصوم والصلاة، والبيع، والإجارة، وما أشبه ذلك. فالنهي عن الأفعال الحسية دلالة على كونها قبيحةً في أنفسها؛ لمعنى في أعيانها بلا خلاف إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه. وأما النهي المطلق عن التصرفات الشرعية: فيقتضي قُبحاً لمعنى في غير المنهي عنه، عنه، لكن متصلاً به حتى يبقى المنهي عنه مشروعاً مع إطلاق النهي وحقيقته. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : بل يقتضي هذا القسم قبحاً في عينه، حتى لا يبقى مشروعاً أصلاً بمنزلة القسم الأول إلا أن يقوم الدليل، فيجب إثبات ما احتمله النهي وراء حقيقته على اختلاف الأصول. وبيان هذا الأصل في صوم يوم العيد، وأيام التشريق، والربا، والبيوع الفاسدة أنها مشروعة عندنا لأحكامها وعنده باطلة منسوخة، لا حكم لها . احتج الشافعي رحمه الله بأن العمل بحقيقة كل قسم واجب، لا محالة، إذ الحقيقة أصل في كل ،باب والنهي في اقتضاء القبح حقيقةٌ
And from this principle: the chapter of prohibition. Absolute prohibition is of two types: prohibition of sensory acts, such as adultery, murder, and drinking wine. And prohibition of shar‘ī transactions, such as fasting, prayer, sale, lease, and the like. Prohibition of sensory acts indicates their being ugly in themselves, due to a meaning in their essences, without disagreement unless evidence indicates otherwise. As for the absolute prohibition of shar‘ī transactions, it necessitates ugliness due to a meaning in something other than the prohibited, but connected to it, so that the prohibited remains legislated with the absolute nature of the prohibition and its reality. Al-Shāfi‘ī, may Allah have mercy on him, said: rather, this type necessitates ugliness in its essence, so that it does not remain legislated at all, like the first type, unless evidence [indicates otherwise], so it is necessary to confirm what the prohibition implies beyond its reality according to the difference of principles. The explanation of this principle is in fasting on the day of Eid and the days of tashrīq, and ribā, and invalid sales: they are legislated with us for their rulings, while with him they are invalid, abrogated, having no ruling. Al-Shāfi‘ī argued by saying: acting upon the literal meaning of every category is obligatory, without doubt, since the literal meaning is a principle in every chapter, and prohibition in implying ugliness is a literal meaning.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on prohibition (bāb al-nahy). It first divides absolute prohibition into two types: (1) prohibition of sensory acts (af‘āl ḥissiyya) like adultery, murder, and drinking wine; (2) prohibition of shar‘ī transactions (taṣarrufāt shar‘iyya) like fasting, prayer, sale, lease, etc. For the first type, the prohibition indicates that these acts are inherently ugly (qabīḥ) due to an attribute in their very essence, and this is agreed upon unless evidence shows otherwise (e.g., killing in jihad). For the second type, the prohibition indicates ugliness due to an attribute outside the prohibited act but connected to it, so the act remains legislated (mashrū‘) in its essence; the prohibition does not nullify its legal existence entirely. Imām al-Shāfi‘ī disagreed, holding that this second type also indicates inherent ugliness, rendering the act null and void unless evidence proves otherwise. The Hanafi view is supported by examples: fasting on Eid, ribā, and invalid sales—according to Hanafis, these actions have some legal rulings (e.g., requiring knowledge of their prohibition, or the requirement to separate in case of invalid sale), while Shāfi‘īs consider them invalid and abrogated, with no rulings whatsoever. Al-Shāfi‘ī argued that acting upon the literal meaning (ḥaqīqa) of every category is obligatory, and the literal meaning of prohibition is to indicate ugliness. The Hanafi response is implicit: the literal meaning of prohibition in shar‘ī transactions is overridden by evidence that the act remains legislated; the ugliness is attached to an external attribute, not the act itself. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each type and the dispute.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): Prohibition; divided into two types: prohibition of sensory acts and prohibition of shar‘ī transactions.
الأفعال الحسية (al-af‘āl al-ḥissiyya): Sensory acts like adultery, murder, drinking; inherently ugly due to essence.
التصرفات الشرعية (al-taṣarrufāt al-shar‘iyya): Shar‘ī transactions like prayer, sale, lease; prohibition indicates ugliness due to external attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the effect of prohibition on shar‘ī transactions such as fasting on the day of Eid?
A) The act is invalid and has no rulings whatsoever.
B) The act remains legislated (mashrū‘) and some rulings apply.
C) The act is permissible but disliked.
D) The act is absolutely forbidden and has no legal existence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, prohibition of a shar‘ī transaction does not strip it of all legal status; the act remains legislated and carries some rulings.

Prohibited Acts Cannot Be Legal Causes
Illustration of the usul principle through six fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a prohibited act (manhiyy 'anhu) cannot serve as a valid legal cause (sabab) for a beneficial ruling and distinguish between prohibitions for their own sake (li-'aynihi) and for something else (li-ghayrihi).

Core Idea
A prohibited act cannot be a cause for a beneficial legal ruling.

This lesson explores a foundational Hanafi usul principle: that the Lawgiver does not make a prohibited act a valid cause for a ruling that is a blessing or concession. The passage from al-Bazdawi illustrates this through six concrete examples, each demonstrating how the prohibition inherent in an act prevents it from generating legal effects. Understanding this principle is key to consistent istinbat.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Identify the core claim: a manhiyy 'anhu cannot be a sabab for a hukm that is beneficial (ni'mah, rukhsah, etc.).
The passage refers to 'hukm shar'i huwa ni'mah'.
2
Analyze the zina case
Zina is pure haram, so it cannot be a cause for marriage (a blessing) nor for lineage.
Contrast with marriage which is halal.
3
Analyze the ghasp case
Usurpation is haram, so it cannot transfer ownership.
Ownership requires a lawful cause.
4
Analyze the ihram case
Intercourse in ihram is haram due to the act itself, but ihram remains binding (it does not become invalid) because the prohibition is on the intercourse, not on ihram itself.
The ihram becomes fasid but not batil.
5
Analyze the divorce case
Divorce during menstruation is prohibited due to harm to the woman (prolonging iddah), so it does not take effect.
The prohibition is li-ghayrihi.
6
Extend to other cases
Travel for sin does not allow rukhsas, and a kafir cannot own Muslim property by force.
Consistent application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنها شرعت نعمةً وكرامةً تُلحق بها الأجنبية بالأمهات، والزنا حرام محض، وعدوان محض، فلم يصلح سبباً لحكم شرعي هو نعمة. وكذلك الغصب لا يفيد الملك؛ لما قلنا. ولا يلزم إذا جامع المحرم أو أحرم مجامعاً أنه يبقى مشروعاً مع كونه فاسداً؛ لأن الإحرام منهي؛ لمعنى في الجماع، وهو غيره، لا محالة، لكنه محظور، فصار مفسداً، والإحرام لازم شرعاً، لا يحتمل الخروج باختيار العبد: ففسد، ولم ينقطع بجناية الجاني، وكلامنا فيما ينعدم شرعاً، لا فيما لا ينقطع بجناية الجاني. ولا يلزم الطلاق في حالة الحيض، أو في طهر جامعها فيه؛ لأنه منهي عنه؛ لمعنى في غيره، وهو الضرر بالمرأة، بتطويل العدة، أو بتلبيس أمر العدة عليها. ولهذا لم يكن سفر المعصية سبباً للرخصة؛ للنهي. ولا يملك الكافرُ مالَ المسلم بالاستيلاء؛ للنهي أيضاً، فلم يصلح.
Because it (marriage) was legislated as a blessing and honor, by which a foreign woman is joined to mothers (in prohibition), while zina is pure prohibition and pure transgression, so it does not qualify as a cause for a legal ruling that is a blessing. Likewise, usurpation does not confer ownership, due to what we said. And it does not follow that if a muhrim (pilgrim in ihram) has intercourse or enters ihram while committing intercourse, it remains valid despite being corrupt; because ihram is prohibited for a meaning in intercourse, and it is other than it necessarily, but it is prohibited, so it becomes corrupting, and ihram is binding by law, not subject to removing by the slave's choice, so it becomes corrupt, and does not terminate by the aggression of the aggressor, and our discussion is about what ceases legally, not about what does not terminate by the aggression of the aggressor. And it does not follow that divorce in a state of menstruation or in a purity in which he had intercourse with her is effective; because it is prohibited for a meaning in something else, which is harm to the woman by prolonging the waiting period or confusing her waiting period. And for this reason, travel for sin does not cause a concession, due to the prohibition. And a non-Muslim does not own the property of a Muslim by usurpation, due to the prohibition as well, so it does not become valid.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Bazdawi illustrates a key usul principle: a prohibited act (manhiyy 'anhu) cannot serve as a valid legal cause (sabab) for a beneficial ruling. The author provides multiple examples. First, zina is pure prohibition and aggression, so it cannot establish lineage or create the marriage bond that is a blessing. Second, usurpation (ghasp) does not transfer ownership. Third, if a muhrim commits intercourse, his ihram does not become invalid entirely; it remains binding but becomes corrupt (fasad) because the act is prohibited due to its own nature (intercourse), while ihram is prohibited due to something else (the prohibition of intercourse during ihram). The ihram is a legal obligation that the person cannot exit by choice, so it remains, but the act makes it defective. Fourth, divorce during menstruation or after intercourse in a state of purity is prohibited due to harm to the woman (prolonging iddah or confusing it), so it does not take effect—it is sinful and ineffective. Fifth, travel for sin does not allow concessions like shortening prayer. Sixth, a non-Muslim cannot acquire ownership of a Muslim's property by force because it is prohibited. The common thread is that the prohibition on the act itself prevents it from being a valid cause for a legal effect that would be beneficial or a concession.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): Prohibition; in this passage, it refers to the legal prohibition of an act (e.g., zina, ghasp, intercourse during ihram, divorce during menses) that prevents that act from being a valid legal cause.
السبب (al-sabab): Legal cause; the means by which a ruling is established. The passage argues that a prohibited act cannot be a valid cause for a beneficial legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does divorce during menstruation not take effect?
A) Because the act is forbidden for its own inherent evil
B) Because it causes harm to the woman by prolonging or confusing her waiting period
C) Because the husband lacks the capacity to divorce
D) Because the divorce is not pronounced verbally

Key Takeaway
A prohibited act cannot be a valid cause for a beneficial legal ruling, a key usul principle in the Hanafi school.

The Hanafi Position on Prohibition and Validity of Acts
Explaining that prohibition does not invalidate an act's existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a prohibited act remains valid in essence and distinguish between the effects of nahy and naskh on the existence of an act.

Core Idea
Prohibition does not negate existence; an act can be ḥarām yet ṣaḥīḥ in essence.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul clarifies a fundamental Hanafi principle: when an act is prohibited, the prohibition does not destroy the act's existence. Rather, the act continues to exist physically and legally, but it becomes sinful and is described as 'corrupt' (fasid). This contrasts with abrogation, which completely removes the act's legal existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings in cases like fasting on Eid or performing a prohibited divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core argument
The passage argues that prohibition does not fall on what does not exist. Locate the proof from Imam Muhammad about fasting on Eid.
2
Distinguish nahy from naskh
Note the difference: naskh removes legal existence, while nahy assumes existence and commands refraining.
3
Understand the attribute of qubh
The prohibited act is described as 'ugly' (qabih), but this does not invalidate its essence.
4
Apply the Hanafi position to examples
Consider cases like corrupt ihram, prohibited divorce, prohibited prayer, and prohibited fasting.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i view
The Shafi'i school holds that legal acts are purely constructual, so prohibition invalidates them entirely. The Hanafi response cites examples where legal acts bear the attribute of corruption without complete nullification.

سبباً مشروعاً. ولا يلزم الظهار ؛ لأن كلامنا وقع في حكم مطلوب تعلَّق مشروع له أيبقى سبباً له، والحكم به مشروعاً مع وقوع النهي عليه؟ * بسبب فأما ما هو حرام غير مشروع تعلق به جزاء زاجر عنه : فيعتمد حرمة سببه، كالقصاص ليس بحكم مطلوب بسبب مشروع، بل جزاء شرع زاجراً، فاعتمد حرمة سببه. ولنا ما احتج به محمد رحمه الله في كتاب الطلاق أن صيام العيد وأيام التشريق منهي والنهي لا يقع على ما لا يتكون. وبيانه أن النهي يُراد به عدم الفعل مضافاً إلى اختيار العباد وكسبهم، فيَعتمد تصوره ليكون العبد مبتلى بين أن يكفّ عنه باختياره، فيُثاب عليه، وبين أن يفعله باختياره، فیلزمه جزاؤه. والنسخ لإعدام الشيء شرعاً؛ لينعدم فعل العبد؛ لعدم المشروع بنفسه؛ ليصير امتناعه بناء على عدمه، وفي النهي يكون عدمه بناء على امتناعه، وهما في طرفي نقيض، فلا يصح الجمع بينهما بحال، والحكم أصلي في النهي ما ذكرنا. فأما القبح : فوصف قائم بالمنهي عنه، مقتضى به؛ تحقيقاً لحكمه فكان تابعاً فلا يجوز تحقيقه على وجه يبطل به ما أوجبه واقتضاه، فيصير المقتضى دليلاً على الفساد بعد أن كان دليلاً على الصحة. بل يجب العمل بالأصل في موضعه، والعمل بالمقتضى بقدر الإمكان، وهو أن يُجعل القبحُ وصفاً للمشروع، فيصير مشروعاً بأصله، غير مشروع بوصفه، فيصير فاسداً. هذا غاية تحقيق هذا الأصل. فأما الشافعي رحمه الله ، فقد حقق المقتضى، وأبطل المقتضي، وهذا في غاية المناقضة والفساد. * فإن قيل: هذا صحيح في الأفعال الحسية؛ لأنها لا تنعدم بصفة القبح، فأما الشرعية فتنعدم ؛ لما قلنا فلا بدَّ من إقامة الدلالة على أن المشروعات تحتمل هذا الوصف. قيل له: قد وجدنا المشروع يحتمل الفساد بالنهي، كالإحرام الفاسد والطلاق الحرام والصلاة الحرام، والصوم المحظور في
A lawful cause. And zihar is not necessary; because our speech has occurred regarding a ruling of a demanded object that has a lawful cause attached to it, does it remain a cause for it, with the ruling being lawful while prohibition occurs upon it? * Because. As for what is unlawful and not lawful, a deterrent penalty is attached to it: it relies on the prohibition of its cause, like qisas is not a ruling of a demanded object due to a lawful cause, but rather a penalty legislated as a deterrent, so it relies on the prohibition of its cause. And we have what Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, argued in Kitab al-Talaq: that fasting on the day of Eid and the days of Tashriq is prohibited, and prohibition does not fall on what does not come into existence. And its explanation is that prohibition intends non-existence of the act added to the choice and acquisition of servants, so it relies on its conceptualization so that the servant is tested between refraining from it by his choice, so he is rewarded, and between doing it by his choice, so his penalty becomes binding. And abrogation is for the removal of something legally; so that the servant's act ceases to exist due to the lack of legality itself; so that his refraining becomes based on its non-existence. And in prohibition, its non-existence is based on his refraining. And they are at opposite extremes, so it is not valid to combine them in any case. And the original ruling in prohibition is what we mentioned. As for ugliness: it is an attribute subsisting in the prohibited thing, necessitated by it; to realize its ruling, so it was subsidiary. So it is not permissible to realize it in a way that invalidates what it necessitated and required. Thus the necessitator becomes evidence for corruption after it was evidence for validity. Rather, it is obligatory to act on the original in its place, and to act on the necessitator to the extent possible, which is to make ugliness an attribute of the lawful act, so it becomes lawful in its essence, not lawful in its description, so it becomes corrupt. This is the ultimate realization of this principle. As for al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, he realized the necessitator and invalidated the necessitator, and this is in extreme contradiction and corruption. * If it is said: This is correct for physical acts because they do not cease to exist by the attribute of ugliness. As for legal acts, they cease to exist; due to what we said, so it is necessary to establish evidence that lawful acts bear this attribute. It is said to him: We have found that the lawful act may bear corruption by prohibition, like corrupt ihram, prohibited divorce, prohibited prayer, and prohibited fasting.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh discussion on the nature of prohibition (nahy) and its effect on the validity of acts, particularly in the Hanafi school. The author begins by distinguishing between acts that are unlawful in themselves (haram li-dhatihi) versus those that are lawful in essence but prohibited due to an external attribute. The key point is that prohibition does not negate the existence of the act; rather, it makes it sinful. Using the example of fasting on Eid and the days of Tashriq, the author cites Imam Muhammad's argument from Kitab al-Talaq: prohibition does not fall on something that does not exist, because the act is still physically possible. The servant is tested between refraining and doing, so the act exists. This is contrasted with naskh (abrogation), which completely removes the legal existence of the act. The author then discusses the attribute of qabih (ugliness): it is a subsidiary attribute that does not destroy the essence of the act. Therefore, a prohibited act remains valid in its essence (sahih) but is described as corrupt (fasid) due to the prohibition. This is the Hanafi position: the act is valid but sinful. The author criticizes al-Shafi'i, who allegedly invalidates the act entirely by emphasizing the necessitator (qabih) over the essence. Finally, the author addresses an objection that legal acts are different from physical acts, and responds by citing examples like corrupt ihram, prohibited divorce, prohibited prayer, and prohibited fasting, showing that legal acts can bear the attribute of corruption without becoming completely null.

Key Terms
النهي (an-nahy): A demand to refrain from an act, implying the act's existence and possibility.
القبح (al-qubh): An attribute of ugliness or evil that attaches to a prohibited act without negating its existence.
الفاسد (al-fasid): An act that is lawful in essence but unlawful in description, thus valid but sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the difference between prohibition (nahy) and abrogation (naskh) according to the passage?
A) Naskh removes the act's legal existence; nahy does not.
B) Naskh makes an act sinful; nahy removes it.
C) Both remove the act's existence.
D) Neither affects existence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a prohibited act remains existent and valid in essence; prohibition only adds the attribute of ugliness (qubh) making it corrupt (fasid) but not void (batil).

Asl vs Wasf: When Prohibition Targets Description, Not Origin
Expands the usul rule of asl vs wasf through multiple furu' examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a prohibition may target only the description (wasf) of an act while leaving the origin (asl) valid, and classify the resulting ruling as fasid (corrupt) rather than batil (void).

Core Idea
Prohibition may corrupt the description of an act, not its origin, making it fasid not batil.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces a core Hanafi principle: when a prohibition applies, it may only affect the description (wasf) of an act, leaving its origin (asl) intact. This distinction is vital for determining whether an act is batil (totally void) or fasid (corrupt but salvageable). The author illustrates with examples from prayer, fasting, and transactions, and defends the principle against objections about definitiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Rule
Understand that the Hanafi school distinguishes between the origin (asl) and the description (wasf) of an act. A prohibition may nullify only the description, leaving the origin valid, which results in fasid (corrupt) rather than batil (void).
The rule applies across ibadat and muamalat.
2
Examine the Initial Examples
The passage begins with prohibitions on prayer during certain times and in specific places. Note that these prohibitions do not make the prayer itself batil; rather, they corrupt its description (time/place).
The hadith on three hours and seven places are cited, though some are weak.
3
Analyze the Transactional Examples
Focus on selling alcohol, buying alcohol with a slave, riba sales, and corrupt conditions. In each case, the contract's form (asl) is valid, but the description (price, excess) is prohibited, making the contract fasid.
Contrast with carrion and its hide, which are not property at all, making the sale batil.
4
Apply to Acts of Worship
Fasting on the day of 'Eid and the days of Tashriq: the origin (fasting) is good, but the prohibition on that day corrupts the description, so it is fasid.
The same logic applies to the Quranic verse about rejecting testimony of slanderers.
5
Contrast with Batil Cases
Identify when an act is batil: when the origin itself is invalid (e.g., sale of carrion lacks a subject matter). This clarifies the boundary of the principle.

الشك (١) ، وما أشبه ذلك، فوجب إثباته على هذا الوجه؛ رعاية لمنازل المشروعات، ومحافظة لحدودها، وعلى هذا الأصل تُخرج الفروع كلها. و قال: «ثلاث ساعات كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينهانا أن نصلي فيهن، وأن نقبر فيهن موتانا : حين تطلع الشمس بازغة حتى ترتفع، وحين يقوم قائم الظهيرة حتى تزول، وحين تَضيَّفُ الشمس للغروب حتى تغرب». رواه الجماعة إلا البخاري، واللفظ لمسلم. قال : والمواطن السبعة أخرج ابن ماجه والترمذي من طريق زيد بن جبيرة عن ابن عمر «أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى أن يُصلَّى في سبعة مواطن: في المزبلة والمجزرة والمقبرة، وقارعة الطريق وفي الحمام، وفي معاطن الإبل، وفوق بيت الله تعالى». قال الترمذي : ليس إسناده بذاك القوي، وقد تكلّم في زيد بن جبيرة من قِبَل حفظه. قلت : قد قالوا: إن الحرام ما كان بقطعي، فكيف أطلق هنا، ولا قاطع؟! قوله : والصوم المحظور يوم الشك : عن صلة بن زفر قال: كنا عند عمار بن ياسر في اليوم الذي يُشَكٍّ فيه، فأُتي بشاة مَصْليَّة، فتنحى بعض القوم، فقال عمار: من صام هذا اليوم فقد عصا أبا القاسم». رواه الخمسة، وصححه ابن خزيمة وابن حبان، وأخرج الخطيب في تاريخ بغداد عن ابن عباس قال: «مَن صام اليوم الذي يُشك فيه: فقد عصى الله ورسوله»، وذَكَرَ له متابعاً. ووقع في «الهداية» بلفظ : « لا يُصام اليوم الذي يُشك فيه»، مرفوعاً، قال المخرجون لا يُعرف ولا أصل له قلتُ : بل له أصل، وهو ما روى الإمام أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه عن عبد الملك بن عمير عن قَزعة عن أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه : أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن صيام اليوم الذي يُشك فيه من رمضان». منها: أن البيع بالخمر منهي بوصفه، وهو الثمن ؛ لأن الخمر مالٌ غير متقوم ، فصلح ثمناً من وجه دون وجه فصار فاسداً، لا باطلاً، ولا خلل في ركن العقد، ولا في محله : فصار قبيحاً بوصفه، مشروعاً بأصله. وكذلك إذا اشترى خمراً بعبد ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما ثمن لصاحبه، فلم ينعقد في الخمر ؛ لعدم محله وانعقد في العبد ؛ لوجود محله، وفسد بفساد ثمنه. بخلاف الميتة ؛ لأنها ليست بمال ولا بمتقومة، فوقع البيع بلا ثمن وهو غير مشروع. وكذلك جلد الميتة ؛ لأنه ليس بمال ولا بمتقوم. وكذلك بيع الربا مشروع بأصله، وهو وجود ركنه في محله، غيرُ مشروع بوصفه، وهو الفضل في العوض، فصار فاسداً، لا باطلاً. وكذلك الشرط الفاسد في البيع : مثل الربا. ولهذا قلنا في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَدًا﴾ [النور:4] : إن النهي يُعْدِمُ الوصف من شهادته، وهو الأداء، ويُبْقِي الأصل، فيصير فاسداً. 19 ومنها: صوم يوم العيد وأيام التشريق حَسَنُ مشروع بأصله، وهو أصل الصوم، ولكن النهي يُعْدِمُ الوصف، وهو الصوم في هذا اليوم، فيصير فاسداً.
The doubt (1) and similar matters; so it is necessary to establish it in this manner, out of consideration for the ranks of legislated acts and preservation of their limits. Upon this principle all branches are derived. He said: 'Three hours the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade us to pray in them and to bury our dead in them: when the sun rises until it rises high, when the sun is at its zenith until it declines, and when the sun inclines to set until it sets.' Narrated by the group except al-Bukhari, and the wording is Muslim's. He said: 'The seven places: Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi narrated from Zayd ibn Jubayrah from Ibn 'Umar that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade praying in seven places: in the trash dump, the slaughterhouse, the cemetery, the middle of the road, the bathhouse, the resting places of camels, and on top of the House of Allah.' Al-Tirmidhi said: 'Its chain is not strong, and Zayd ibn Jubayrah was criticized regarding his memory.' I said: 'They said that the forbidden is that which is definitive, so how is it applied here without a definitive proof?' His statement: 'The prohibited fasting on the day of doubt: from Silah ibn Zufar who said: We were with 'Ammar ibn Yasir on the day that is doubted, and a roasted sheep was brought, so some of the people withdrew, and 'Ammar said: Whoever fasts this day has disobeyed Abu al-Qasim.' Narrated by the five, authenticated by Ibn Khuzaymah and Ibn Hibban, and al-Khatib narrated in Tarikh Baghdad from Ibn 'Abbas who said: 'Whoever fasts the day that is doubted has disobeyed Allah and His Messenger,' and he mentioned a supporter for him. And in al-Hidayah it appears as: 'The day that is doubted is not fasted,' raised, the editors said it is unknown and has no basis. I said: Rather it has a basis: what Imam Abu Hanifah, may Allah be pleased with him, narrated from 'Abd al-Malik ibn 'Umayr from Qaza'ah from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade fasting the day that is doubted of Ramadan.' Among them: that selling alcohol is prohibited in its description, i.e., the price, because alcohol is property that is not legally valuable, so it became valid as a price from one aspect and not from another, so it became fasid (corrupt), not batil (void), and there is no defect in the pillar of the contract or in its object: so it became reprehensible in its description, valid in its origin. Similarly, if one buys alcohol with a slave, because each of them is a price for the other, so the contract did not conclude regarding the alcohol due to lack of its object, and it concluded regarding the slave due to existence of its object, and it became corrupt due to corruption of its price. Unlike carrion, because it is not property nor legally valuable, so the sale occurred without a price and it is not valid. Similarly, the hide of carrion, because it is not property nor legally valuable. Similarly, riba (usury) sale is valid in its origin, i.e., the existence of its pillar in its object, not valid in its description, i.e., the excess in the exchange, so it becomes fasid, not batil. Similarly, a corrupt condition in sale: like riba. Therefore we said regarding the verse: 'And never accept their testimony' [al-Nur:4]: that the prohibition removes the description from his testimony, i.e., the performance, and preserves the origin, so it becomes fasid. 19 And among them: fasting on the day of 'Eid and the days of Tashriq; it is good, valid in its origin, i.e., the origin of fasting, but the prohibition removes the description, i.e., fasting on that day, so it becomes fasid.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a fundamental usul principle: when a prohibition applies to an act, it may target only the description (wasf) of the act while leaving the original (asl) intact. The author first discusses the day of doubt and prohibited times/places for prayer as examples where the prohibition does not necessarily imply definitive (qat'i) proof. Then he dives into the core rule: a prohibited transaction like selling alcohol is fasid (corrupt) not batil (void) because the prohibition attaches to the description (the price being non-valuable property) while the original contract form is valid. The same applies to buying alcohol with a slave, riba sales, corrupt conditions in sales, and the prohibition of accepting testimony from a slanderer (qadhf) where the testimony's performance is corrupted but the underlying right remains. Finally, fasting on 'Eid and Tashriq days is prohibited in description but the act of fasting itself is good in origin, so it becomes fasid. The Hanafi school consistently applies this rule to distinguish between batil (totally invalid) and fasid (corrupt but not void) in transactions and acts of worship.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): The original, fundamental aspect of an act or contract that is valid and legitimate.
وصف (wasf): A description or attribute of an act that may be prohibited without invalidating the asl.
فاسد (fasid): An act that is valid in origin but invalid in description; corrupt but not void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A prohibition may only corrupt the description of an act, leaving its origin valid, resulting in fasid (corrupt) rather than batil (void).

Prohibition and Its Effect: Essential vs. External Attributes
Discusses whether prohibition invalidates when attached to external attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a prohibition invalidates an act only when attached to an essential attribute (wasf), and identify when a prohibition does not invalidate.

Core Idea
A prohibition invalidates an act only if it attaches to its essence (wasf); if attached to place or time, the act remains valid but sinful.

This lesson explores a foundational Hanafi usul principle: not every prohibition renders an act invalid. Using the classic examples of prayer in usurped land and sale during the Friday call to prayer, the Hanafi school demonstrates that the attachment point matters. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying prohibitions in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
State the Hanafi position: a prohibition (nahy) does not automatically invalidate an act. It depends on whether the prohibition attaches to an essential attribute (wasf) or an external factor like place or time.
2
Examine the Examples
Consider the two examples given: prayer in usurped land and sale during the call to prayer. In both, the prohibition attaches to an external factor (land, time), so the act remains valid but sinful.
3
Contrast with Invalid Contracts
Contrast with the sale of a free person or unborn camels (madamin/malaqih). Here the prohibition attaches to the object itself, which is not a valid subject of contract, so the contract is invalid.
This shows that the prohibition can indeed invalidate when it relates to the essence of the object.
4
Determine the Key Distinction
The core distinction is between a wasf (essential attribute) and the mahall (locus) or external factor. Only prohibitions that touch the essence invalidate; those touching external factors do not.
5
Apply to a New Scenario
Take a new scenario: a person buys a stolen item. The prohibition relates to ownership (an essential attribute of the contract), so the sale is invalid. In contrast, praying in a stolen garment involves an external attribute, so the prayer is valid but sinful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنهي عن الصلاة في أرض مغصوبة) متعلق بما ليس بوصف، فلم تفسد، فكذلك البيع وقت النداء.
وهذا يخالف بيع الحر، والمضامين، والملاقيح؛ لأنه أضيف إلى غير محله، فلم ينعقد، فصار النهي مجازاً عن النفي، وهذه الاستعارة صحيحة لما بينهما من المشابهة، ولا خلاف فيه، إنما الخلاف في حكم حقيقته.
The prohibition of prayer in usurped land is attached to what is not a description (of the prayer), so it does not invalidate (the prayer). Likewise, sale during the call to prayer. And this differs from the sale of a free person and the sales of 'madamin' and 'malaqih', because (in those) it is added to other than its proper place, so it is not concluded. Thus the prohibition becomes metaphorical for negation. This metaphor is valid due to the similarity between them, and there is no dispute about it; the dispute is only about the ruling of its reality.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul discussion on the effect of prohibition (nahy) on the validity of an act. The key principle is that a prohibition does not automatically render an act invalid (fasid); rather, its effect depends on what it attaches to. Here, the prohibition of prayer in a usurped land attaches to the land (place), which is an external attribute of the prayer, not the prayer's essence. Hence the prayer remains valid but sinful. Similarly, the prohibition of sale during the Friday call to prayer attaches to the time, not the contract itself, so the sale is valid but sinful. This contrasts with prohibitions that attach to the object of a contract itself, such as selling a free person (a human cannot be owned) or selling an unborn camel (madamin/malaqih), where the object is not legally valid—thus the contract is invalid (batil). The author notes that the metaphorical use of prohibition to mean negation (i.e., the ruling of invalidity) is agreed upon among scholars for those cases, but the actual ruling of the reality (whether the contract is valid or not) is disputed. This distinction is foundational in Hanafi usul: a prohibition (nahy) may imply invalidity only when it relates to an essential attribute or condition of the act, but not when it relates to an external factor.

Key Terms
نهي (nahy): A prohibition that indicates an act is sinful, but does not necessarily invalidate it unless attached to an essential attribute.
وصف (wasf): An attribute or description integral to the act or object; the prohibition's attachment to a non-wasf (external factor) does not invalidate.
محل (mahall): The locus or place of the act; here, the place of prayer (land) is not part of the prayer's essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the land is not owned by the person.

Key Takeaway
A prohibition invalidates an act only when it attaches to an essential attribute (wasf); if attached to an external factor like place or time, the act remains valid but sinful.

The Hanafi Classification of Prohibited Acts
Defining the taxonomy of ugly (qabīḥ) acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four categories of prohibited actions (qabīḥ) in Hanafi usul and apply them to given examples.

Core Idea
Prohibition is not always inherent; Hanafi usul divides ugliness into four grades based on source and attachment.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul provides a foundational classification of prohibited acts (qabīḥ) that shapes how Hanafi jurists derive rulings. Understanding this taxonomy is critical for distinguishing between acts that are prohibited in themselves (like kufr) and those prohibited due to external circumstances (like selling during Friday call). Let's dissect the four categories and see how they apply to everyday legal scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall division
Start by reading the final paragraph of the source where the author states that prohibition in terms of ugliness divides like the division of command. This is your anchor.
The author explicitly lists four categories; note the parallel with the division of command (amr).
2
Enumerate the four categories
List them: (1) qabīḥ li-aynihi wad'an (by convention), (2) qabīḥ li-aynihi shar'an (by law, attached to the first), (3) qabīḥ li-ghayrihi (external meaning), (4) qabīḥ li-ghayrihi attached in description (mulḥaq bihi wāṣfan). Understand the basis for each.
Examples: kufr (1), selling a free person (2), selling at call time (3), invalid sale (4).
3
Relate categories to act type
The author notes that prohibition of physical acts falls on the first category, while prohibition of legal acts falls on the fourth. Understand why the third category exists as a middle ground.
Physical acts like lying are inherently ugly; legal acts like invalid sales are externally ugly but treated as attached.
4
Test with given examples
Apply the classification to examples from the text: praying in usurped land (3), fasting on Eid (4), traveling for disobedience (not inherently prohibited). Confirm each fits its category.
For travel, the prohibition is adjacent (sin in the journey) not inherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا تَنكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ آبَاؤُكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ ﴾ [النساء 22] مستعار عن النفي. - وأما استيلاء أهل الحرب، فإنما صار منهياً بواسطة العصمة، ثابتة في حقنا، دون أهل الحرب؛ لانقطاع ولايتنا عنهم، ولأن العصمة متناهية بتناهي سببها، وهو الإحرازُ بالدار، فسقط النهي في حكم الدنيا. - وأما الملك بالغصب فلا يثبت مقصوداً به، بل شرطاً لحكم شرعي، وهو الضمان؛ لأنه شرع جبراً، ولا جبر مع بقاء الأصل على ملكه، إذ الجبر يعتمد الفوات، وشَرْطُ الحكم تابع له، فصار حَسَنَاً لحسنه، وإنما قبح لو كان مقصوداً به.
وفي ضمان المدبَّر: قلنا بزوال المدبَّر عن ملك المولى؛ لكونه مالاً مملوكاً؛ تحقيقاً لشرط المشروع، وهو وجوب الضمان، ولا يدخل في ملك المشتري صيانةً لحقه، ولأن ضمان المدبّر جعل مقابلاً بالفائت، وهو اليد دون الرقبة وهذا طريق جائز، لكن لا يُصار إليه عن المقابلة بالرقبة إلا عند العجز والضرورة، فالطريقُ الأول واجب، وهذا جائز. وأما الزنا: فلا يوجب حرمة المصاهرة أصلاً بنفسه، وإنما هو سبب للماء، والماء سبب للولد وجوداً والولد هو الأصل في استحقاق الحرمات، ولا عصيان ولا عدوان فيه.
و
28
ثم يتعدى منه إلى أطرافه، ويتعدى إلى أسبابه، وما يعمل بقيامه مقام غيره: فإنما يعمل بعلة الأصل.
ألا يُرى أن التراب لما قام مقامَ الماء: نظر إلى كون الماء مطهراً، وسقط وصفُ التراب، فكذلك يُهدَر وصف الزنا بالحرمة؛ لقيامه مقام ما لا يوصف بذلك في إيجاب حرمة المصاهرة.
وأما سفر المعصية: فغير منهي لمعنى فيه؛ لأنه من حيث إنه خروج مديدٌ مباح، وإنما العصيان في فعل قطع الطريق، أو التمرد على المولى، وهو مجاور له، فكان كالبيع وقت النداء.
ولا يلزم على هذا النهي عن الأفعال الحسية؛ لأن القول بكمال القبح فيها، وهو مقتضى مع كمال المقصود، ممكن، على ما قلنا.
و
والنهي في صفة القبح ينقسم انقسام الأمر:
- ما قبح لعينه وضعاً، مثل الكفر، والكذب، والعبث.
و
- وما قبح لعينه شرعاً مُلحقاً بالقسم الأول، وهو بيعُ الحُرِّ، والمضامين، والملاقيح؛ لأن البيع لما وضع لتمليك المال: كان باطلاً في غير محله.
وما قبح لمعنى في غيره، وهو البيع وقت النداء، والصلاة في أرض مغصوبة.
وما قبح لمعنى في غيره، وهو ملحق به وصفاً، وذلك مثل البيع الفاسد، وصيام يوم النحر.
و
الأمور
والنهي عن الأفعال الحسية: يقع على القسم الأول، وعن الشرعية: يقعُ على هذا القسم، الذي قلنا إنه ملحق به وصفاً، والله أعلم.
The Almighty said: 'And do not marry those women whom your fathers married' [al-Nisa 22] — it is used metaphorically for negation. As for the seizure by the people of war, it becomes prohibited only by means of inviolability, which is established in our favor, not in favor of the people of war, because our authority over them is severed, and because inviolability terminates with the termination of its cause, which is protection by the abode, so the prohibition falls in the worldly ruling. As for ownership by usurpation, it is not established as intended, but as a condition for a legal ruling, which is compensation; because it is legislated as a remedy, and there is no remedy while the original remains in his ownership, since remedy depends on loss, and the condition of the ruling is subordinate to it, so it becomes good because of its goodness; it would be ugly only if it were intended. Regarding compensation for a mudabbar slave: we say the mudabbar leaves the ownership of the master, because it is owned property, to fulfill the condition of the legislation, which is the obligation of compensation, and it does not enter the ownership of the buyer to protect his right, and because compensation for a mudabbar is made in exchange for the lost thing, which is the hand not the neck, and this is a permissible method, but it is not resorted to in exchange for the neck except in case of inability and necessity; so the first method is obligatory, and this is permissible. As for adultery: it does not establish the prohibition of marriage by affinity at all by itself, but it is a cause for the fluid, and the fluid is a cause for the child in existence, and the child is the basis for deserving prohibitions, and there is no disobedience or transgression in it. Then it extends from it to its extremities, and extends to its causes, and what operates by taking the place of another: it operates by the cause of the original. Do you not see that when dust takes the place of water: consideration is given to water being purifying, and the description of dust falls, so likewise the description of adultery regarding prohibition is disregarded because it takes the place of that which is not described as such in establishing the prohibition of marriage by affinity. As for travel for disobedience: it is not prohibited for a meaning in it; because it is, as such, an extended permissible departure, and disobedience is only in the act of highway robbery or rebellion against the master, and it is adjacent to it, so it is like selling at the time of the call. This does not necessitate the prohibition of physical acts; because the statement of complete ugliness in them, which is required along with completeness of purpose, is possible, as we have said. And prohibition in terms of ugliness divides like the division of command: What is ugly inherently by convention, such as disbelief, lying, and frivolity. And what is ugly inherently by law, attached to the first category, which is selling a free person, and a clam, and a fetus? because when selling was placed for transferring ownership, it was invalid in other than its place. And what is ugly due to an external meaning, which is selling at the time of the call, and praying in usurped land. And what is ugly due to an external meaning, but attached to it in description, such as an invalid sale and fasting on the day of sacrifice. And prohibition of physical acts falls on the first category, and of legal acts falls on this category, which we said is attached to it in description. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul continues the discussion on prohibitions (nahy). The author first presents a Quranic verse prohibiting marriage to women one's father married, treating the prohibition metaphorically as negation. He then examines several cases: seizure by enemies (prohibition drops because our authority is severed), ownership by usurpation (ugly if intended but permissible as a condition for compensation), and compensation for a mudabbar slave (ownership transfers for compensation). He states that adultery does not itself create marriage prohibitions by affinity; it only provides the fluid that causes the child, which is the basis for such prohibitions. He then classifies prohibited actions (qabīḥ) into categories: (1) inherently ugly by nature (e.g., kufr, lying), (2) inherently ugly by law (e.g., selling a free person), (3) ugly due to an external meaning (e.g., selling at Friday call, praying in usurped land), and (4) ugly due to an external meaning but attached in description (e.g., invalid sale, fasting on Eid). The final point distinguishes between physical acts (falling under the first category) and legal acts (falling under the fourth). This classification is key in Hanafi usul for understanding how the ruling of prohibition applies.

Key Terms
النهي (an-nahy): Prohibition – a demand to abstain, with the connotation of ugliness (qabīḥ).
قبيح (qabīḥ): Ugly – an action that is legally objectionable, divided into that which is ugly inherently (li-aynihi) or due to an external meaning (li-ghayrihi).
مستعار (musta'ar): Used metaphorically – here, the prohibition is treated as metaphorical negation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the source, which of the following is an example of qabīḥ li-aynihi shar'an (inherently ugly by law, attached to the first category)?
A) Selling at the time of the call (Friday)
B) Fasting on the day of sacrifice
C) Selling a free person
D) Praying in usurped land

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of prohibited acts into four grades (inherent by convention, inherent by law, external meaning, external meaning attached) is the key to determining whether a prohibition affects the validity of the act itself.

Metaphorical Negation and External Prohibitions
Applying the concept of metaphorical prohibition and external ugliness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a prohibition is used metaphorically as negation and apply the Hanafi principle that prohibitions can arise from external adjacency rather than inherent ugliness.

Core Idea
A textual prohibition may be metaphoric negation, and an act can be prohibited due to adjacent sin, not its own nature.

This part of the passage delves into the concept that not all prohibitions are literal; sometimes the word 'do not' functions as negation. Moreover, the author examines cases like travel for disobedience and ownership by usurpation to show how ugliness can arise from external factors. Understanding these nuances prevents over-application of prohibition to acts that are actually permissible in essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand metaphorical negation
Read the opening example: the Quranic verse 'Do not marry women your fathers married' is interpreted as negation, not a literal prohibition. Understand why the Hanafi school uses this interpretation.
The term musta'ar (metaphorical) indicates the prohibition is not meant to establish haram but to negate the validity of the marriage.
2
Analyze external prohibition through adjacency
Examine the case of travel for disobedience: the travel itself is permissible, but the sin (highway robbery) is adjacent. The prohibition attaches to the sin, not the travel. This is like selling at the time of the call.
The author uses qiyas: it is like al-bay' waqt al-nida'.
3
Apply to ownership by usurpation
Ownership by usurpation (al-milk bi al-ghasb) is not intended as a valid ownership; it is allowed only as a condition for compensation. Understand the principle of 'jabr' (remedy).
The act becomes 'good' (hasan) because it serves a legal purpose, even though it would be ugly if intended independently.
4
Connect to the four-category framework
Link these examples to the four categories. Travel for disobedience falls under li-ghayrihi (3rd), usurpation under li-ghayrihi attached? Actually, the author treats it separately but it aligns with external meaning.
Not all examples are explicitly placed in the list, but the reasoning applies.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul continues the discussion on prohibitions (nahy). The author first presents a Quranic verse prohibiting marriage to women one's father married, treating the prohibition metaphorically as negation. He then examines several cases: seizure by enemies (prohibition drops because our authority is severed), ownership by usurpation (ugly if intended but permissible as a condition for compensation), and compensation for a mudabbar slave (ownership transfers for compensation). He then classifies prohibited actions (qabīḥ) into categories: (1) inherently ugly by nature (e.g., kufr, lying), (2) inherently ugly by law (e.g., selling a free person), (3) ugly due to an external meaning (e.g., selling at Friday call, praying in usurped land), and (4) ugly due to an external meaning but attached in description (e.g., invalid sale, fasting on Eid). The final point distinguishes between physical acts (falling under the first category) and legal acts (falling under the fourth). This classification is key in Hanafi usul for understanding how the ruling of prohibition applies.

قبيح (qabīḥ): Ugly – an action that is legally objectionable, divided into that which is ugly inherently (li-aynihi) or due to an external meaning (li-ghayrihi).
مستعار (musta'ar): Used metaphorically – here, the prohibition is treated as metaphorical negation.

Why does the author treat travel for disobedience (safar al-ma'ṣiya) as not prohibited for its own sake?

Key Takeaway
Prohibitions can be metaphorical negations or arise from external adjacency; the act itself may remain valid even though the overall situation is haram.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Hanafi Rule: General Term (Amm) is Decisive (Qat'i)
Stating the Hanafi rule that 'amm is qati and providing proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that 'amm is qat'i, identify the proofs from Abu Hanifa and hadith, and apply this rule to distinguish Hanafi from Shafi'i usul.

Core Idea
The general term is decisive like the specific; it can even abrogate a specific ruling.

This passage from the chapter on general terms establishes a foundational Hanafi usul principle. Unlike the Shafi'i school which treats general terms as speculative (zanni) until specified, the Hanafi school holds that a general term conveys its ruling with certainty (qat'an). This principle has far-reaching consequences for how texts are interpreted and how apparent contradictions between general and specific texts are resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
Read the first sentence: 'العام عندنا يوجب الحكم فيما تناوله قطعاً ويقيناً'. What does it assert about the general term?
Note the phrase 'عندنا' indicating this is the Hanafi position.
2
Extract Abu Hanifa's proof
Find the statement attributed to Abu Hanifa: 'إن الخاص لا يقضي على العام'. What does this imply about the relative strength of general and specific?
This is a direct textual proof for the Hanafi position.
3
Examine the hadith examples
Look at the two hadith examples: the 'Urayniyyun hadith (specific permission) and the 'avoid urine' hadith (general command). How does this demonstrate abrogation of the specific by the general?
The specific permission was abrogated by the later general command, showing the general's decisiveness.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
Consider how a Shafi'i usuli would read these same texts. Would they accept that the general abrogates the specific? Why or why not?
This contrast is implicit in the passage's emphasis on 'عندنا'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب معرفة أحكام العموم العام عندنا يوجب الحكم فيما تناوله قطعاً ويقيناً، بمنزلة الخاص فيما يتناوله.
والدليل على أن المذهب عندنا هذا الذي حكينا : أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله قال : إن الخاص لا يقضي على العام، بل يجوز أن يُنسخ الخاص به، مثل حديث العرنيين في بول ما يُؤكل لحمه : نُسخ وهو خاص بقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «استنزهوا من البول».
ومثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «ليس فيما دون خمسة أوسقٍ»
(۱) باب معرفة أحكام العموم حديث العرنيين: عن أنس بن مالك رضي عنه أن رهطاً من عُكَل أو قال : عُرَيْنَة، قدموا فاجتَوَوا المدينة، فأمر لهم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بلقاح، وأمرهم أن يشربوا من أبوالها، وألبانها». متفق عليه.
(۲) حديث استنزهوا من البول: عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «استنزهوا من البول فإن عامة عذاب القبر منه، رواه الدارقطني. وللحاكم: أكثر عذاب القبر من البول وإسناده صحيح، أخرجه الدارقطني من جهة أزهر بن سعد السمان وقد وثقه ابنُ سعد عن ابن عَون عن ابن سيرين عن أبي هريرة، وهذان ممن روى له الجماعة، وقال الحاكم: صحيح، لا أعلم له علة.
Chapter on understanding the rules of generality (al-'umum). The general term according to us obligates the ruling concerning what it encompasses with certainty and assurance, like the specific term concerning what it encompasses. And the evidence that the school doctrine according to us is what we have narrated is that Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, said: Indeed, the specific does not override the general, but rather it is permissible that the specific be abrogated by the general, like the hadith of al-'Urayniyyun concerning the urine of what is eaten for its meat: it was abrogated, and it was specific, by the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Avoid urine.' And like his saying, peace be upon him: 'There is no [zakat] on less than five wasqs.' (Note: The footnote provides references: Hadith of al-'Urayniyyun from Anas ibn Malik about a group from 'Ukl or 'Urayna who came and found the climate adverse, so the Prophet ordered them to drink from the urine and milk of the camels. Agreed upon. Hadith of 'Avoid urine' from Abu Hurayra: The Prophet said: 'Avoid urine, for indeed most of the punishment of the grave is from it,' narrated by al-Daraqutni. Al-Hakim: 'Most of the punishment of the grave is from urine,' and its chain is authentic; al-Daraqutni narrated it from Azhar ibn Sa'd al-Samman... etc.)
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on general terms (al-'umum) establishes a core Hanafi usul principle: the general term ('amm) is decisive (qat'i) in conveying its ruling, just as the specific term (khass) is. This is the Hanafi school's position, in contrast to some other schools that consider 'amm to be speculative (zanni) until specified. The author provides two proofs. First, a statement from Abu Hanifa that the specific does not override the general; instead, it is possible for the specific to be abrogated by the general. This is illustrated by the hadith of the 'Urayniyyun (or 'Ukl) who were permitted to drink the urine of camels for medicinal purposes. That hadith is specific (khass) to that situation, but it is abrogated by the general command 'Avoid urine' which applies to all urine from animals whose meat is eaten. Thus, the general term abrogated the specific ruling. Second, the hadith 'There is no zakat on less than five wasqs' is another example of a specific ruling that is abrogated by the general obligation of zakat. The footnotes provide the chains of transmission for these hadith, confirming their authenticity. This principle has significant fiqh consequences: a general text cannot be dismissed by a specific text; rather, the specific is either a qualification (takhsis) of the general or subject to abrogation.

Key Terms
العام (al-'amm): A word that includes all individuals of its category without limitation; here it is said to obligate the ruling with certainty (qat'an).
الخاص (al-khass): A word that is limited to a specific individual or group; it does not override the general but may be abrogated by it.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a earlier ruling by a later one; here it is shown that a specific ruling can be abrogated by a general ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul passage, what is the relationship between a general term ('amm) and a specific term (khass)?
A) The specific term always overrides the general term
B) The general term is weaker and must be qualified by the specific
C) The specific term does not override the general; instead, the specific may be abrogated by the general
D) Both are of equal weight and neither can abrogate the other

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the general term is decisive (qat'i) and can abrogate a specific term, not the other way around.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Definitiveness of 'Amm in Hanafi Usul
Defining the definitive nature of 'amm in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that 'amm is qat'i and explain its implications for specification by khabar al-wahid and qiyas.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, the general ('amm) is as definitive (qat'i) as the specific (khass) and cannot be weakened by uncertain evidence.

This passage from al-Bazdawi establishes a cornerstone of Hanafi usul: the general term (al-'amm) carries certainty (qat') in its ruling, just like the specific (al-khass). This means that a general text cannot be overridden by a specific one; rather, if conflict arises, the specific may be abrogated by the general. The passage provides textual evidence, illustrative examples, and the well-known Hanafi position that a general whose specificity is not proven cannot be limited by a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) or analogical reasoning (qiyas). Understanding this principle is crucial for deriving fiqh rulings and appreciating the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about 'amm
Read the opening sentence: 'العام عندنا يوجب الحكم فيما تناوله قطعاً ويقيناً، بمنزلة الخاص فيما يتناوله.' Recognize that Hanafis treat 'amm as qat'i, equivalent to khass in certainty.
This is the axiomatic claim of the passage.
2
Trace the evidence from Abu Hanifa and the two hadith
The passage cites Abu Hanifa's statement and two examples: the specificity of the hadith of 'Uraniyyin was abrogated by the general command 'استنزهوا من البول', and the specific threshold of 5 wasq was abrogated by the general 'ما سقته السماء ففيه العشر'. These show that a general can replace a prior specific ruling.
The evidence is dalil naqli: hadith (zanni thubut but qat'i dalala in Hanafi view).
3
Apply the legal definition dissector to 'amm
Use the provided legal_definition_dissector: sabab (generality of wording), shart (no proven takhsis), mani (specification by khabar al-wahid/qiyas), athar (certain application to all included). This dissects the concept.
This step helps internalize the structure of 'amm.
4
Analyze the dispute about specification
The passage states that the general whose specificity is not established does not admit specification by khabar al-wahid or qiyas. This is the well-known Hanafi view (al-mashhur). Compare this to the Shafi'i position that allows specification by such evidence.
The thamarah al-khilaf appears in fiqh applications, e.g., zakat on less than 5 wasq.
5
Consider the practical examples: ring bequest and mudarib dispute
The ring-fas example shows that a general bequest (the ring) is given to the first legatee, while the specific gem is shared. Similarly, in a mudarib dispute, the one claiming generality is believed. These illustrate the default to generality.
This step anchors the abstract rule in concrete fiqh scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب معرفة أحكام العموم
العام عندنا يوجب الحكم فيما تناوله قطعاً ويقيناً، بمنزلة الخاص فيما يتناوله.
والدليل على أن المذهب عندنا هذا الذي حكينا: أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله قال: إن الخاص لا يقضي على العام، بل يجوز أن يُنسخ الخاص به، مثل حديث العرنيين في بول ما يُؤكل لحمه: نُسخ وهو خاص بقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «استنزهوا من البول».
ومثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «ليس فيما دون خمسة أوسقٍ صدقة» نُسخ بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «ما سَقَتْه السماء ففيه العُشر».
ولِمَا ذَكَرَ محمد رحمه الله فيمن أوصى بخاتمه لإنسان، ثم بالفص منه لآخر بكلام مفصول: أن الحَلْقةَ للأول، والفَصَّ بينهما.
وإنما استحقه الأول بالعموم، والثاني بالخصوص، وهذا قولهم جميعاً.
وقالوا في رب المال والمضارب إذا اختلفا في العموم والخصوص: إن القول قولُ مَن يدعي العموم، ولولا استواءهما، وقيام المعارضة بينهما: لما وجب الترجيح بدلالة العقد.
وقد قال عامة مشايخنا رحمهم الله إن العام الذي لم يثبت خصوصه: لا يحتمل الخصوص بخبر الواحد والقياس هذا هو المشهور، واختاره القاضي الشهيد رحمه الله في كتاب: «الغرر».
فثبت بهذه الجملة أن المذهب عندنا ما قلنا.
Chapter on Knowing the Rulings of the General. The general (أم) according to us necessitates the ruling regarding what it encompasses definitively and with certainty, like the specific (خاص) regarding what it encompasses. The evidence that the school (مذهب) according to us is this which we have narrated is that Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Indeed, the specific does not override the general, rather it is permissible for the specific to be abrogated by it.' For example, the hadith of the ‘Uraniyyin regarding the urine of what is eaten for its meat: it was abrogated, and it is specific, by the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Guard against urine.' And similarly, His saying, peace be upon him: 'There is no sadaqah on less than five wasqs' was abrogated by his saying, peace be upon him: 'What the sky waters, therein is one-tenth.' And for what Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned regarding one who bequeaths his ring to a person, then the gem from it to another, with separate speech: the ring is for the first, and the gem is shared between them. The first claims it by generality, and the second by specificity, and this is their statement all together. And they said regarding the owner of wealth and the mudarib when they differ regarding generality and specificity: the statement is that of the one who claims generality, and were it not for their equivalence and the existence of opposition between them, preference would not be necessary by the indication of the contract. And most of our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, have said: 'Indeed, the general whose specificity has not been established does not admit specificity through a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) or analogy (qiyas).' This is the well-known view, chosen by al-Qadi al-Shahid, may Allah have mercy on him, in the book al-Ghurar. So it is established by these statements that the school (مذهب) according to us is what we have said.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a foundational usul definition from al-Bazdawi. It establishes that in the Hanafi school, the general (عام) is definitive (قطعي) in its application, just like the specific (خاص). The key point is that a specific text does not override a general one; rather, if there is a conflict, the specific may be abrogated by the general. Evidence is provided from two hadith: the specific ruling about the urine of edible animals was abrogated by the general command to avoid urine, and the specific hadith about the minimum threshold for zakat (5 wasq) was abrogated by the general hadith about one-tenth on crops watered by rain. Imam Muhammad’s ruling on a bequest of a ring and its gem illustrates that a general disposition (the ring) takes precedence, while the specific gem is shared. In a dispute between a fund owner and a mudarib, the one claiming generality is believed because generality is the default. Finally, the passage states that a general term whose specificity has not been proven cannot be limited by a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) or analogy (qiyas)—a distinctively Hanafi position that reflects their cautious approach to weakening general proofs.

Key Terms
العام (al-‘āmm): A word that encompasses all individuals or items it refers to without limit; in Hanafi usul, it yields certainty (قطعية) in its ruling.
الخاص (al-khāṣṣ): A word confined to a specific individual or limited set; it is also definitive within its scope.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, how does a specific text (خاص) relate to a general text (عام)?
A) The specific overrides the general.
B) The general overrides the specific.
C) The specific may be abrogated by the general.
D) The specific has no effect on the general.

Key Takeaway
The general ('amm) in Hanafi usul is as definitive as the specific (khass) and cannot be specified by uncertain evidence; it can only be abrogated by an equally general or stronger text.
