General Text Cannot Be Specified by Solitary Report or Analogy
Stating the Hanafi principle that a general text not specified cannot be specified by khabar wahid or qiyas, with examples and a dispute with Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a general text (amm) remains definitive and cannot be specified by khabar wahid or qiyas, and apply it to analyze the verse 6:121.

Core Idea
A definitive general text cannot be limited by weaker evidence like a solitary hadith or analogy.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi usul principle: a general text (amm) that has not already been specified remains qat'i (definitive) and cannot be narrowed by zanni evidence such as a solitary report (khabar wahid) or analogical reasoning (qiyas). This principle has major implications for interpreting Quranic commands and juristic disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the passage and locate the core ruling: 'the general that has not had its specification established does not admit specification by a solitary report and analogy.' Understand that this is the well-known (mashhur) Hanafi view.
2
Examine the Quranic Dalil
Focus on the verse 6:121 ('Do not eat of that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned'). The author treats this as an example of a general text that remains general. Identify why it is considered qat'i al-dalala.
The forgetful is equated with the one who remembers due to necessity, so specification does not occur.
3
Understand the Shafi'i Objection
Contrast with Imam al-Shafi'i's position that the general is zanni and can be specified by khabar wahid. Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): for Hanafis, the verse forbids all meat not slaughtered with tasmiyah; Shafi'is allow it if slaughter is by a Muslim.
4
Apply the Principle to a New Text
Consider the verse 3:97 ('Whoever enters it is secure'). According to the passage, it also cannot be specified by solitary reports. Test your understanding by stating what the Hanafi ruling would be: sanctuary is guaranteed to all entrants.
The Madhhab Dispute Map in the toolbox provides further detail.

صدقة): نُسخ بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «ما سَقَتْه السماء ففيه العُشر» (۲). وِلِمَا ذَكَرَ محمد رحمه الله فيمن أوصى بخاتمه لإنسان، ثم بالفص منه لآخر بكلام مفصول : أن الحَلْقةَ للأول، والفَصَّ بينهما. وإنما استحقه الأول بالعموم، والثاني بالخصوص، وهذا قولهم جميعاً. وقالوا في رب المال والمضارب إذا اختلفا في العموم والخصوص: إن القول قولُ مَن يدعي العموم، ولولا استواءهما، وقيام المعارضة بينهما : لما وجب الترجيح بدلالة العقد. وقد قال عامة مشايخنا رحمهم الله إن العام الذي لم يثبت خصوصه: لا يحتمل الخصوص بخبر الواحد والقياس هذا هو المشهور، واختاره القاضي الشهيد رحمه الله في كتاب: «الغرر». فثبت بهذه الجملة أن المذهب عندنا ما قلنا. ولهذا قلنا : إن قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا مِمَّا لَمْ يُذْكَرِ اسْمُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ﴾ الأنعام/١٢١ : عام لم يلحقه خصوص؛ لأن الناسي في معنى الذاكر؛ لقيام الملة مقام الذكر، فلا يجوز تخصيصه بالقياس وبخبر الواحد. (۱) وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن دَخَلَهُ كَانَ عَامِنَا﴾ آل عمران / ۹۷ : لم يلحقه الخصوص، فلا يجوز تخصيصه بالآحاد (۳) والقياس. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : العام يوجب الحكم لا على اليقين، وعلى هذا دلت مسائله. (۱) قوله : وخبر الواحد قال الشارح: هو حديث : المسلم يذبح على اسم الله ، سمَّى أو لم يُسم»، قال المخرجون لأحاديث الهداية»: لم نجده بهذا اللفظ، وإنما أخرج الدارقطني عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «المسلم يكفيه اسمه، فإن نسي أن يسمي حين يذبح فليسم، وليذكر اسم الله ، ثم ليأكل ، وصحح وقـ وقفه على ابن عباس، وفي سنده مقال. قلت : وهذا لا حجة فيه للمخالف، بل هو دليل لنا في الناسي. وأولى منه في الاستدلال لهم : ما أخرج أبو داود في «المراسيل» عن الصلت أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ذبيحة المسلم حلال ذكر اسم الله أو لم يذكر»، لكنهم لا يَرَوْن المرسل حجةً، والله أعلم. (۳) قوله بالآحاد قال الشارح هو حديث قتل ابن خَطَل عن أنس بن مالك رضي الله عنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم دخل عام الفتح وعلى رأسه مِغْفَرُ، فلما نزعه: جاءه رجل فقال : يا رسول الله ! ابنُ خَطَل متعلق بأستار الكعبة، فقال: اقتلوه». متفق عليه. قال: وحديث : إن الحرم لا يُعيذُ عاصياً : المحفوظ أن هذا من كلام عمرو بن سعيد الأشدق ، أخرجاه في حديث أبي شريح العدوي رضي الله عنه.
Sadaqah: it was abrogated by his saying, peace be upon him: 'What the sky waters, in it is the tithe.' And because Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned regarding someone who bequeathed his ring to a person, then the gem from it to another with separate speech: that the band is for the first, and the gem is between them. And the first only deserves it due to generality, and the second due to specificity, and this is their statement all together. And they said regarding the owner of capital and the mudarib if they differ regarding generality and specificity: the statement is the statement of the one who claims generality, and were it not for their equivalence and the existence of contradiction between them, preference would not be required by the indication of the contract. And the generality of our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, said: Indeed, the general that has not had its specification established does not admit specification by a solitary report and analogy; this is the well-known (view), and the judge al-Shahid, may Allah have mercy on him, chose it in the book 'al-Ghurar'. So it is established by these sentences that the madhhab according to us is what we said. And for this we said: Indeed, the saying of Allah Most High: 'And do not eat of that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned' [al-An'am 6:121] is a general that has not been reached by specification; because the forgetful is in the meaning of the one who remembers, due to the inability standing in place of remembrance, so it is not permissible to specify it by analogy and by a solitary report. And likewise His saying: 'And whoever enters it is secure' [Al 'Imran 3:97] has not been reached by specification, so it is not permissible to specify it by solitary reports and analogy. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The general gives a ruling not with certainty, and upon this his issues indicate. (1) His saying: 'and by a solitary report' - the commentator said: It is the hadith: 'The Muslim slaughters in the name of Allah, whether he named or not.' The editors of the hadiths of al-Hidayah said: We did not find it with this wording. Rather, al-Daraqutni narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'The Muslim is sufficient his name; if he forgets to name when he slaughters, let him name, and remember the name of Allah, then let him eat.' And he authenticated it and it was stopped at Ibn Abbas, and in its chain there is discussion. I said: This is no proof for the opponent, rather it is evidence for us regarding the forgetful. And more appropriate for their evidence is what Abu Dawud narrated in 'al-Marasil' from al-Salt that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'The slaughter of the Muslim is lawful whether he mentioned the name of Allah or did not mention.' But they do not consider the mursal as proof, and Allah knows best. (3) His saying: 'by solitary reports' - the commentator said: It is the hadith of killing Ibn Khatal from Anas ibn Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Prophet, peace be upon him, entered on the day of the conquest while on his head was a helmet. When he removed it, a man came and said: O Messenger of Allah, Ibn Khatal is clinging to the curtains of the Kaaba. He said: 'Kill him.' Agreed upon. He said: And the hadith: 'Indeed the sanctuary does not give refuge to a sinner' - the preserved is that this is from the speech of Amr ibn Sa'id al-Ashdaq, both narrated in the hadith of Abu Shurayh al-Adawi, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul, with commentary, establishes a foundational Hanafi principle: a general text (amm) that has not been subject to specification remains definitive and cannot be limited or specified by a solitary report (khabar wahid) or analogy (qiyas). The author begins by citing an example of abrogation (naskh) regarding zakat, showing how a general ruling can be superseded by a stronger text. He then provides a legal example from inheritance law: a bequest of a ring to one person and its gem to another—the generality of the word 'ring' includes the gem for the first recipient, while the second recipient takes the gem by specific designation. This illustrates how 'amm and khass operate in practice. The text also discusses a dispute between the owner of capital and the mudarib over general and specific terms, where the one claiming generality prevails. The core ruling is attributed to the majority of Hanafi scholars and preferred by al-Qadi al-Shahid. The proof is drawn from two Quranic verses: first, the prohibition of eating meat without mentioning Allah's name (6:121), which remains general despite a potential specification for the forgetful (the hadith citing a Muslim's slaughter), but according to Hanafis, the forgetful is considered like the one who remembers due to necessity, so the general remains. Second, the verse about security in the Haram (3:97) also remains general. The author then contrasts the Hanafi view with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that 'amm is not definitive and thus can be specified by weaker evidence. The footnotes discuss the specific hadiths cited by the opponent and critique their authenticity and applicability.

Key Terms
العام (al-amm): A word that includes all individuals of its category without limitation.
الخصوص (al-khusus): The specification of a general term, limiting its application.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A hadith narrated by one or a few narrators, not reaching the level of tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does the general Quranic verse (6:121) not get specified by the hadith about a Muslim's slaughter?
A) Because the hadith is mursal and thus not acceptable as evidence.
B) Because the general is qat'i and cannot be specified by a solitary report or analogy, and the forgetful is treated like the one who remembers due to necessity.
C) Because Imam al-Shafi'i rejected that hadith.
D) Because the verse was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
A general text (amm) that has not been specified is definitive in Hanafi usul and cannot be restricted by a solitary report or analogy; this principle safeguards the certainty of divine commands.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Certainty of General Terms Despite Potential Specification
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i on certainty of general terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the certainty of 'amm and identify the usul principle that distinguishes it from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
The Hanafi 'amm is binding until specification appears; we judge by the apparent form, not hidden intentions.

In this lesson, we explore the key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the certainty of general terms ('amm). The Shafi'i school argues that every general term could be intended as specific, thus introducing doubt and undermining certainty. The Hanafi school counters that the linguistic form is placed for a meaning and we are not required to know the speaker's inner intention. We will walk through the Hanafi argument, the supporting dalil, and the practical impact on rulings such as the prohibition of combining two sisters in marriage.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position on 'amm
Identify the claim that every general term can potentially be intended as specific by the speaker, which introduces doubt (shubha) and removes certainty (yaqin).
This is the starting point of the dispute.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the linguistic form (sigha) is placed for a meaning, and that meaning is binding (wajib) until evidence of specification appears. Human beings are not tasked with knowing the inner intention of the speaker (ghayb).
The key principle: al-sigha tujibu ma wudi‘at lahu.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The proof is based on the Quranic usage itself and a rational principle: if a form is established for a meaning, its indication is authoritative until proven otherwise. The inner intention is not evidence because we cannot access it.
Consider the example of the two Quranic verses cited.
4
Understand the refinement about muhkam
The Hanafi author distinguishes between 'obligating' (mujib) and 'unequivocal' (muhkam). A general term is mujib, not muhkam, so it can be reinforced (tawkid) by additional evidence to become decisive, just as a specific term can be reinforced against metaphor.
The reinforcement does not change the basic obligation; it only confirms it.
5
Apply to the example of combining two sisters
Apply the principle: the general prohibition in Surah al-Nisa' 23 is binding until a specific exception is proven. The general permission in Surah al-Mu'minun 6 does not automatically create an exception. The Hanafi ruling is that combining two sisters is haram, and this is the mufta bihi position.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf: the practical consequence of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اليمين، فقال: أحلَّتْهما آيةٌ، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا عَلَى أَزْوَاجِهِمْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَتُهُمْ ﴾ المؤمنون/٦ ، وحرَّمتهما آيةٌ، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَن تَجْمَعُواْ بَيْنَ الأُختين﴾ النساء/٢٣ ، فصار التحريم أولى، وذلك عام كلّه. ثم قال الشافعي رحمه الله : كل عام يحتمل إرادة الخصوص من المتكلم، فتمكنت فيه شبهة، فذهب اليقين. ولنا أن الصيغة متى وضعت لمعنى كان ذلك المعنى واجباً به حتى يقوم الدليل على خلافه، وإرادة الباطن لا تصلح دليلاً؛ لأنا لم تكلف دَركَ الغيب، فلا تبقى له عبرة أصلاً. والجواب عما احتج به طائفة أهل المقالة الأولى: أنا ندعي أنه موجب لما وضع له ، لا أنه مُحكَمٌ لِمَا وُضع له، فكان محتملاً أن يُراد به بعضه، فصلح توكيده بما يحسم باب الاحتمال؛ ليصير محكماً كالخاص يحتمل المجاز، فتوكيده بما يقطعه، لا بما يفسره فيقال: جاءني زيد نفسه؛ لأنه قد يحتمل غير المجيء مجازاً. ذلك إلا جعلته نكالاً قال ابن شهاب : أراه علي بن أبي طالب. الله قلت : قد صرح به في رواية عبيد عبد الله بن عبد بن عتبة. الله و عبيد الله بن عبد كما أخرجها ابن أبي شيبة ثنا غندر عن معمر عن الزهري عن عبيد بن عتبة قال: سأل رجل عثمان بن الأختين يُجمع بينهما ؟ فقال : أحلَّتْهما آية، وحرمتهما آية، ولا آمرك ولا أنهاك، فلقي علياً بالباب، فقال: عمّ سألته؟ ،ه، فقال : لكني أنهاك ولو كان لي عليك سبيل ، ثم فعلت ذلك : لأوجعتك. فأخبره،
As for the oath, he said: Two verses made them permissible: the verse 'except on their spouses or what their right hands possess' [al-Mu'minun 6], and two verses made them forbidden: the verse 'and that you combine two sisters' [al-Nisa' 23]. So prohibition takes precedence, and that is all general. Then al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Every general term could potentially be intended with specificity by the speaker, so doubt enters into it, and certainty departs. Our argument: The linguistic form, when placed for a meaning, makes that meaning binding until evidence to the contrary appears. The intention of the inner meaning is not valid as evidence, because we are not tasked with knowing the unseen, so it has no consideration whatsoever. The response to what the group of the first school argued: We claim that it is obligating for what it is placed for, not that it is unequivocal for what it is placed for. Thus, it is possible that some of it is intended, so it is suitable to be reinforced by what eliminates the possibility of probability, to become unequivocal, like the specific that admits metaphor, so it is reinforced by what cuts it off, not by what interprets it, as in 'Zaid himself came' because it could potentially be a metaphorical coming. That except you make it a punishment? Ibn Shihab said: I think he meant Ali ibn Abi Talib. Allah said: He made it clear in the narration of Ubayd Allah ibn Abd ibn Utbah. Allah and Ubayd Allah ibn Abd, as reported by Ibn Abi Shayba from Ghandar from Ma'mar from al-Zuhri from Ubayd ibn Utbah, said: A man asked Uthman about combining two sisters, and he said: Two verses made them permissible, and two verses made them forbidden, and I do not command you nor forbid you. Then he met Ali at the door and said: What did you ask him? He said: But I forbid you, and if I had authority over you, and you did that, I would punish you. So he informed him.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i position on the certainty of general terms ('amm). The Shafi'i argument holds that every general term ('amm) can potentially be intended as specific (khass), thereby creating doubt and undermining certainty. The Hanafi response asserts that the linguistic form (sigha) is established for a meaning, and that meaning is binding (wajib) until there is clear evidence to the contrary. The inward intention of the speaker is not a valid proof because human beings are not required to know the unseen (ghayb). The author further refines the argument: a general term is 'obligating' (mujib) for its literal meaning, not 'unequivocal' (muhkam) in the sense of being immune to specification. This allows it to be reinforced (tawkid) by additional evidence to cut off possibility of specification, making it decisive like a specific term. An example: 'Zaid himself came' eliminates metaphorical interpretation. The passage then cites a historical dispute between Uthman and Ali regarding combining two sisters in marriage, where Ali forbids it. This illustrates the practical application of these principles: the general prohibition (combining two sisters) is given weight despite a general permission (marriage to slave women). The Hanafi position maintains that general terms remain probative until specification is proven, contrary to the Shafi'i skepticism.

Key Terms
عام ('amm): A term that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction.
خصوص (khussus): The specification of a general term, limiting its application to some individuals or circumstances.
يقين (yaqin): Certainty; the absence of doubt about the legal ruling derived from the text.
صيغة (sigha): The linguistic form or construction of the text.
مُحكَم (muhkam): Unequivocal; a text that is clear and decisive with no possibility of interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in this passage, why does the possibility of specification not destroy the certainty of a general term?
A) Because general terms are never actually specified.
B) Because human beings are not required to know the speaker's inner intention.
C) Because the Shafi'i school agrees that general terms are certain.
D) Because the Quranic verses on this topic are unequivocal (muhkam).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a general term ('amm) remains binding and certain in its indication until explicit evidence of specification (takhsis) is established; mere possibility does not undermine its authority.

Three Opinions on the General Term After Specification
Presents three scholarly opinions on general term after specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on the probative value of a general term ('amm) after specification (takhsis) and apply each opinion to a given example to determine whether inference is valid.

Core Idea
A general term may remain proof after specification, but only if the exception is known; otherwise its ruling falls.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh presents a classic dispute among early Hanafi scholars about what happens to a general text after it has been specified by an exception. The answer determines whether we can still use that general text as evidence for the remaining cases. Three distinct positions are recorded, each with different implications for how we derive rulings from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the passage and list the three positions: Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi's view, the 'others' (second opinion), and 'some' (third opinion). Note how they treat the general term when the exception is known (ma'lum) vs unknown (majhul).
2
Understand the key variables
There are two variables: (1) whether the general term remains a proof (hujja) or not, and (2) whether the exception is known or unknown. Each opinion gives a different combination.
Known (ma'lum) means the scope and identity of the exception are clear; unknown (majhul) means they are ambiguous.
3
Work through the example of theft verse
Apply the second opinion to the verse on cutting the thief's hand (al-sariqa: 5:38). The verse is general for all theft, but the hadith specifies 'no cutting for less than a shield's value' – and that value is unknown to later generations. According to the second opinion, because the exception is unknown, the ruling of generality falls, so the verse cannot be used as proof for any theft case.
This shows the practical impact of the choice of opinion.
4
Contrast with the sale verse example
The verse 'Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury' (al-Baqara: 2:276) generalizes all trade. The riba prohibition is a known specification. According to the second opinion, since the exception is known, the general term remains proof for the remainder – so trade is still permitted except for riba. According to al-Karkhi, even this would be invalid.
5
Evaluate the consequences
Consider the implications: al-Karkhi's view would invalidate almost all inference from general texts, since most have some specification. The majority Hanafi position follows the second or third opinion, preserving the usability of general texts with known exceptions.
This is a key usul debate with far-reaching fiqh consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العام إذا لَحِقَه الخصوص فإن لَحِقَ هذا العام خصوص، فقد اختلف فيه : فقال أبو الحسن الكرخي رحمه الله : لا يبقى حجةً أصلاً، سواء كان المخصوص معلوماً أو مجهولاً. وقال غيره : إن كان المخصوص معلوماً بقي العام فيما وراء المخصوص على ما كان، وإن كان المخصوص مجهولاً : يَسقط حكم العموم. وقال بعضهم: إن كان المخصوص معلوماً : بقي العام فيما وراءه على ما كان وأما إذا كان مجهولاً : فإن دليل الخصوص يَسقط. فعلى قول الكرخي رحمه الله : يبطل الاستدلال بعامة العمومات لما دخلها من الخصوص. وعلى القول الثاني: لا يصح الاستدلال بآية السرقة، وآية البيع؛ لأن ما دون ثمن المِجَنِّ خُص من آية السرقة، وهو مجهول، وخص الربا من
Chapter: On the general term when specification attaches to it. If specification attaches to this general term, then there is disagreement regarding it: Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It remains no proof at all, whether the specified part is known or unknown. And others said: If the specified part is known, the general remains in what is beyond the specified as it was; and if the specified part is unknown, the ruling of generality falls. And some said: If the specified part is known, the general remains beyond it as it was; but if it is unknown, then the evidence of specification falls. So according to al-Karkhi's statement, inference by all general terms is invalid because of the specification that enters them. And according to the second statement, inference by the verse of theft and the verse of sale is not valid, because less than the price of a shield is specified from the verse of theft, and that is unknown, and usury is specified from the verse of sale.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This is a chapter (bab) from the matn of Al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh, presenting three opinions on the status of a general term ('amm) after it has been specified (takhsis). The first opinion, attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi (a leading Hanafi usuli), is that the general term loses all probative value (hujja) entirely, regardless of whether the specific exception is known (ma'lum) or unknown (majhul). The second opinion (qawl) distinguishes: if the exception is known, the general term remains proof for the remainder; if unknown, the ruling of the general term (hukm al-'umum) falls. The third opinion reverses the consequence for the unknown: the general term remains, but the evidence for the specification (dalil al-khussus) falls. The practical implications: According to al-Karkhi, all general terms—like those in the Quran and sunna—are invalid as proofs because they have all undergone some specification. This renders much of Islamic law inaccessible. According to the second opinion, specific examples: the verse of theft (al-sariqa) is general for cutting the hand for any theft, but it was specified by the hadith that the hand is not cut for less than a shield's value; this exception is unknown in quantity (for later generations), so the verse cannot be used to prove the general rule. Similarly, the verse of sale (al-bay') is general for all transactions, but it was specified by the prohibition of riba (usury); since the specific prohibition of riba is known, the general permissibility of sale remains? Actually the text says the second opinion invalidates inference from these verses because the exception is unknown (in theft) and in sale it says 'and usury is specified from' but cuts off. The third opinion would keep the general term but drop the evidence for the specification if unknown. This lesson sets out the usul foundations for how to handle general texts that have exceptions.

Key Terms
حجة (hujja): A proof or evidence that can be used to derive a legal ruling.
مخصوص معلوم (makhsus ma'lum): The specific exception is known in its scope and content.
مخصوص مجهول (makhsus majhul): The specific exception is unknown, either in quantity or identity.
دليل الخصوص (dalil al-khussus): The evidence that indicates the specification, such as a hadith or verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion mentioned in this passage, what happens to the general term if the specified part is unknown (majhul)?
A) The general term remains a proof for the remainder.
B) The ruling of generality (hukm al-umum) falls.
C) The evidence for specification (dalil al-khussus) falls.
D) The general term becomes invalid entirely.

Key Takeaway
The probative value of a general text after specification depends on whether the exception is known: if known, the general remains proof for the remainder (majority view); if unknown, the ruling of generality falls.

The General Remains a Proof After Specification
Dispute over the general's evidentiary status after specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the status of a general text after specification and explain the ijma-based proof that supports it.

Core Idea
A general text remains a binding proof after specification, though with a shade of doubt.

What happens to a general verse or hadith when a specific evidence excludes some of its individuals? Does it lose all force or remain useful? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi resolution to this classic usul problem. Through the lens of al-Bazdawi's text, we examine three competing views, the practical examples of theft and sale, and the consensus evidence that anchors the correct position.

Guided Path
1
List the three views on the general after specification
From the passage, identify the three opinions: (1) al-Karkhi: the general ceases to be a proof entirely; (2) some: if the specified part is known the general remains, if unknown the general falls; (3) others: if specified is known the general remains, if unknown then the evidence for specification falls. Note that the third is not further developed.
Focus on the first two, as the third is not elaborated.
2
Work through the concrete examples
The passage gives two examples: the verse of theft (5:38) specified by the hadith of the shield's price, and the verse of sale and riba (2:275) specified by the prohibition of certain transactions. In both, the specified portion is unknown (the exact threshold, the precise definition of riba). According to the second view, these verses cannot be used as proof.
Understand why 'unknown' matters in this debate.
3
State the correct Hanafi position and its nuance
Al-Bazdawi declares: 'والصحيح من مذهبنا أن العام يبقى حجةً بعد الخصوص' — the general remains a proof after specification, whether the specified is known or unknown. However, it carries a kind of doubt (shubha). This doubt does not negate its probative value.
The doubt is like that in a general text before specification according to al-Shafi'i.
4
Analyze the ijma proof
Two consensuses are invoked: (a) the Salaf used general texts as proof — proving they are valid evidence; (b) they permitted specification by qiyas and ahad reports — proving the general is not absolutely definitive and therefore susceptible to doubt. The doubt explains the permissibility of specification, but the general remains hujja.
This is a two-part evidence: one for proof status, one for the existence of doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العام إذا لَحِقَه الخصوص
فإن لَحِقَ هذا العام خصوص، فقد اختلف فيه :
فقال أبو الحسن الكرخي رحمه الله : لا يبقى حجةً أصلاً، سواء كان المخصوص معلوماً أو مجهولاً.
وقال غيره : إن كان المخصوص معلوماً بقي العام فيما وراء المخصوص على ما كان، وإن كان المخصوص مجهولاً : يَسقط حكم العموم.
وقال بعضهم: إن كان المخصوص معلوماً : بقي العام فيما وراءه على ما كان وأما إذا كان مجهولاً : فإن دليل الخصوص يَسقط. فعلى قول الكرخي رحمه الله : يبطل الاستدلال بعامة العمومات لما دخلها من الخصوص.
وعلى القول الثاني: لا يصح الاستدلال بآية السرقة، وآية البيع؛ لأن ما دون ثمن المِجَنِّ خُص من آية السرقة، وهو مجهول، وخص الربا من قوله تعالى: وَأَحَلَّ اللهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرّبوا، وهو مجهول.
وكذلك نصوص الحدود ؛ لأن مواضع الشبهة منها مخصوصة وفيه ضَرْبُ جهالة واختلاف.
والصحيح من مذهبنا أن العام يبقى حجةً بعد الخصوص، معلوماً كان المخصوص أو مجهولاً ، إلا أن فيه ضَرْبَ شبهة، وذلك مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في العموم قبل الخصوص.
ودلالة صحة هذا المذهب : إجماعُ السلف على الاحتجاج بالعموم. ودلالة أن في ذلك شبهةً : إجماعُهم على جواز التخصيص بالقياس والآحاد، وذلك دون خبر الواحد، حتى صحت معارضته بالقياس.
Chapter: The General when Specifics Attach to It
If a specific attaches to this general, there is disagreement about it:
Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It does not remain a proof at all, whether the specified part is known or unknown.
Others said: If the specified part is known, the general remains in what is beyond the specified as it was; if the specified part is unknown, the ruling of generality falls.
And some said: If the specified part is known, the general remains in what is beyond it as it was; as for if it is unknown, then the evidence of specificity falls. According to al-Karkhi's view, reasoning with all general texts is invalidated because specificity has entered them.
And according to the second view, reasoning with the verse of theft and the verse of sale is not valid because what is less than the price of a shield is specified from the verse of theft, and it is unknown; and riba is specified from His statement: And Allah has permitted sale and forbidden riba, and it is unknown.
Similarly, the texts of hadd punishments, because the places of doubt are specified from them, and there is a kind of ignorance and disagreement in it.
The correct [view] in our school is that the general remains a proof after specification, whether the specified is known or unknown, except that there is a kind of doubt in it. This is like the statement of al-Shafi'i regarding the general before specification.
The evidence for the correctness of this school is the consensus of the Salaf on using general texts as proof. And the evidence that there is doubt in it is their consensus on the permissibility of specification by analogy and ahad [reports], which are below a single report [in strength], to the point that it can be opposed by analogy.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh addresses the status of a general ('amm) text after a specific (khas) has been applied to it (takhsis). The chapter is titled 'The General when Specifics Attach to It.' The author presents three views: (1) Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi holds that the general ceases entirely to be a proof (hujja), regardless of whether the specified portion is known or unknown. (2) Another view distinguishes: if the specified portion is known (ma'lum), the general remains a proof for the remainder; if unknown (majhul), the general's ruling falls completely. (3) A third view says that if the specified is known, the general remains; if unknown, it is the evidence for specification (dalil al-khusus) that falls, not the general. The author then gives examples: the verse of theft (Quran 5:38) is specified to exclude less than the price of a shield (a known hadith), but the exact value is unknown in the verse itself; similarly, the verse permitting sale and forbidding riba is specified to exclude certain transactions, yet the scope of riba is not explicitly defined in the verse. Texts of hadd punishments are also specified by places of doubt (shubuhat), introducing ignorance and disagreement. The correct Hanafi position is that the general remains a proof after specification, whether the specified is known or unknown, but with a degree of doubt (shubha). This is similar to al-Shafi'i's view on the general before specification. The evidence for this is twofold: first, the consensus of the Salaf on using general texts as proof; second, their consensus on allowing specification by qiyas (analogy) and ahad reports—which are weaker than mutawatir—shows that there is doubt in the general's definitiveness, yet it remains usable as proof.

Key Terms
العام (al-‘āmm): A word that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction.
الخصوص / التخصيص (al-khuṣūṣ / al-takhṣīṣ): The act of excluding some individuals from the general meaning, either by a connected or separate evidence.
حجة (ḥujja): A proof or evidence that can be used to establish a legal ruling.
المخصوص (al-makhṣūṣ): The portion or individuals excluded from the general by specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi position, what is the status of a general text after specification?
A) It ceases to be a proof entirely.
B) It remains a proof only if the specified part is known.
C) It remains a proof but with a kind of doubt.
D) It remains a proof only if the specification is by a mutawatir text.

Key Takeaway
The general text remains a binding proof after specification; the doubt it carries does not remove its evidentiary force.

General Text Remains Authoritative After Specification
Debating the authority of general texts after specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the authority of a general text after specification and evaluate the differing view of al-Karkhi.

Core Idea
A general text remains a proof after specification, albeit weakened — the Salaf's consensus upholds it.

In this passage from Kanz al-Wusul, the author addresses a critical usul question: does a general text (ʿāmm) lose its authority when it is specified (takhṣīṣ)? The Hanafi school holds that it retains its binding force, but with a degree of doubt. The discussion includes evidence from consensus and a counterargument from the notable Hanafi scholar al-Karkhī.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine whether a general text (ʿāmm) remains authoritative (ḥujja) after being specified by a separate evidence.
2
State the Hanafi position
The correct view: the general remains a proof, but with a type of doubt (shubha). This is similar to al-Shafi‘i’s view on general texts before specification.
3
Examine the primary evidence
The consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Salaf on using general texts as proof, and their consensus on the permissibility of specification by qiyās and āḥād reports.
4
Understand the opposing view
Al-Karkhī argues that if the specification evidence is unknown (jahālah), it functions like an exception (istithnāʾ), showing that the specified never fell under the general. If known, it may be based on a legal cause (ʿillah), creating uncertainty about the remaining scope.
5
Recognize the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: a general command like 'kill the polytheists' specified by an unknown exception still binds the majority, but with caution due to the remaining doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله تعالى: وَأَحَلَّ اللهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرّبوا ) البقرة/ ٢٧٥، وهو مجهول. وكذلك نصوص الحدود ؛ لأن مواضع الشبهة منها مخصوصة وفيه ضَرْبُ جهالة واختلاف. والصحيح من مذهبنا أن العام يبقى حجةً بعد الخصوص، معلوماً كان المخصوص أو مجهولاً ، إلا أن فيه ضَرْبَ شبهة، وذلك مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في العموم قبل الخصوص. ودلالة صحة هذا المذهب : إجماعُ السلف على الاحتجاج بالعموم. ودلالة أن في ذلك شبهةً : إجماعُهم على جواز التخصيص بالقياس والآحاد، وذلك دون خبر الواحد، حتى صحت معارضته بالقياس. أما الكرخي فقد احتج بأن دليل الخصوص إذا كان مجهولاً: أوجب في الباقي ؛ لأن الخصوص بمنزلة الاستثناء؛ لأنه يُبين أنه لم يدخل تحت الجملة، كالاستثناء. وإذا كان معلوماً احتمل أن يكون معلولاً، وهو الظاهر؛ لأن دليل الخصوص نص قائم بنفسه، فصَلَحَ تعليله، ولا يُدرى أيُّ القَدْر من الباقي صار مستثنى فيصير بمنزلة جهالة المخصوص.
His statement, the Exalted: 'And Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury' (Al-Baqarah 2:275), and it is unknown. Similarly, the texts of hadd penalties, because the places of doubt from them are specified, and in it is a type of ignorance and disagreement. The correct view in our madhhab is that the general remains a proof after specification, whether the specified is known or unknown, except that there is in it a type of doubt, and that is like the saying of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, regarding the general before specification. Evidence for the correctness of this madhhab is the consensus of the Salaf on using the general as proof. And evidence that there is doubt in it is their consensus on the permissibility of specification by analogy and ahad (reports), and that is less than khabar al-wahid, such that its opposition by analogy is valid. As for al-Karkhi, he argued that when the evidence of specification is unknown, it necessitates [the ruling] in the remaining part because specification is like an exception, as it shows that it did not enter under the general statement, like an exception. And when it is known, it is possible that it is 'illah-based, and that is apparent, because the evidence of specification is a text standing by itself, so it is suitable for 'illah reasoning, and it is not known how much of the remaining part became excluded, so it becomes like the unknown specification.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage discusses the authority of a general text (ʿāmm) after it has been specified (takhṣīṣ). The author first gives an example of a general text that is specified but the specific evidence is unknown (majhūl): the verse on trade and usury, and texts on ḥadd penalties. He then states the correct Hanafi position: the general text remains a proof (ḥujja) after specification, whether the specific evidence is known or unknown, but it suffers from a type of doubt (shubha). This is similar to al-Shafiʿī's view regarding general texts before specification (i.e., they are also weakened). The proof for this position is the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Salaf that they used general texts as proof. The proof that there is doubt is their consensus on the permissibility of specification by qiyās and āḥād reports, which are weaker than khabar al-wāḥid, so opposing the general by qiyās is valid. Al-Karkhī then presents a differing argument: if the specification evidence is unknown, it is like an exception (istithnāʾ)—it shows that the specified item never entered the general statement, so the remaining text is unaffected. If the specification evidence is known, it may be based on a legal cause (ʿillah), making it suitable for analogical reasoning, but since we cannot determine how much of the remaining text is excluded, it becomes like an unknown specification. The Hanafī position is that the general remains binding but weakened.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm): A term that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction.
خاص (khāṣṣ): A term that specifies a part of the general, whether by exception or other means.
جهالة (jahālah): Unknown or unclear specification evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, what is the status of a general text (ʿāmm) after it has been specified (takhṣīṣ)?
A) It becomes completely invalid and cannot be used as proof.
B) It remains a proof (ḥujja) but with a type of doubt (shubha).
C) It remains a proof without any change in strength.
D) It becomes speculative (ẓannī) only if the specification is unknown.

Key Takeaway
A general text remains authoritative after specification, but its binding force is weakened by the presence of doubt (shubha).

Effect of Unknown Specification Evidence on the General Text
Analyzing a Hanafi internal dispute on jahala of khas's evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the three Hanafi views on the effect of unknown specification evidence on a general text, and EVALUATE the author's synthesis that balances resemblance to both exception and abrogator.

Core Idea
An unknown specification evidence creates ambiguity, not nullification; the general remains operative in the remainder.

This passage delves into a nuanced Hanafi dispute: when a dalil al-khusus (specification evidence) is unknown (majhul), what happens to the remaining general text? Three positions are recorded, and the author, likely al-Pazdawi, offers a synthesis. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to handle uncertain textual evidence in istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Read the passage and list the three positions: (1) al-Karkhi's view on known vs unknown, (2) the second view (general remains operative for known evidence), (3) the third view (unknown evidence falls by itself). Note the dalil for each.
2
Analyze the author's synthesis
The author argues that dalil al-khusus resembles exception (istithna') in ruling but abrogator (nasikh) in wording. Understand why he rejects reducing it to either alone.
3
Apply to each scenario
Work through the two scenarios: when the evidence is unknown, and when it is known. For each, apply the author's dual-aspect reasoning to see how ambiguity arises without invalidation.
4
Extract practical implications
From the thamarah al-khilaf, state the practical fiqh consequence when a specification evidence is unknown (e.g., weak hadith). The general text's scope becomes uncertain but still operative.
5
Complete the proof chain
Use the hadith example ('We are not inherited from...') to trace a derivation chain. Fill in the missing steps in the proof_chain block below.

أما الكرخي فقد احتج بأن دليل الخصوص إذا كان مجهولاً: أوجب في الباقي؛ لأن الخصوص بمنزلة الاستثناء؛ لأنه يُبين أنه لم يدخل تحت الجملة، كالاستثناء. وإذا كان معلوماً احتمل أن يكون معلولاً، وهو الظاهر؛ لأن دليل الخصوص نص قائم بنفسه، فصَلَحَ تعليله، ولا يُدرى أيُّ القَدْر من الباقي صار مستثنى فيصير بمنزلة جهالة المخصوص. ومنها ما أخرجه الشيخان عن أبي هريرة قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا نورث ما تركنا صدقة». متفق عليه. ومنها ما أخرج البخاري عن زيد بن ثابت أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أملَى علي: ﴿وَلَا يَسْتَوِي الْقَعِدُونَ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ... وَالْمُجَاهِدُونَ فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ﴾، فجاءه ابنُ أمِّ مكتوم وهو يُمِلها عليَّ، فقال : والله يا رسول الله لو أستطيع الجهاد لجاهدت، وكان أعمى، فأنزل الله عزّ وجلَّ على رسوله وفَخِذه على فخذي، فَثَقُلَتْ عليَّ حتى خِفْتُ أن تَرُضَّ فخذي، ثم سُرِّي عنه، فأنزل الله عزّ وجل : ﴿غَيْرُ أُولِي الضَّرَرِ﴾. وأخرجه الترمذي والنسائي. ومنها : ما عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال: «لما توفي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم واستخلف أبو بكر بعده، وكَفَرَ مَن كَفَرَ من العرب، قال عمر بن الخطاب لأبي بكر رضي الله عنهما : كيف تقاتل الناس وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: أُمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يقولوا: لا إله إلا الله، فمن قال: لا إله إلا الله عَصَمَ مني ماله ونفسه إلا بحقه وحسابه على الله؟ فقال أبو بكر : والله لأقاتلنَّ مَن فرَّق بين الصلاة والزكاة، فإن الزكاة حق المال، والله لو منعوني عَنَاقاً كانوا يؤدُّونها إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لقاتلتهم على منعها، قال عمر : والله ما هو إلا أن رأيتُ أن الله شرح صدر أبي بكر للقتال، فعرفتُ أنه الحق». أخرجه الجماعة. ووجه القول الثاني: أن دليل الخصوص إذا كان مجهولاً: فعلى ما قلنا، وإذا كان معلوماً : بقي العام موجباً في الباقي؛ لأن دليل الخصوص بمنزلة الاستثناء، على ما قلنا فلا يؤثر في الباقي؛ لأن الاستثناء لا يحتمل التعليل، فكذلك هذا. ووجه القول الآخر: أن دليل الخصوص لمَّا كان مستقلاً بنفسه، حتى لو تراخى كان ناسخاً سقط بنفسه إذا كان مجهولاً؛ لأن المجهول لا يصلح دليلاً، بخلاف الاستثناء؛ لأنه وصف قائم بالأول، فأوجب جهالةً فيه، وهذا قائم بنفسه معارض للأول. ودليل ما قلنا أن دليل الخصوص يُشبه الاستثناء بحكمه؛ لما قلنا إنه يُبين أنه لم يدخل في الجملة، ألا يُرى أنه لا يكون إلا مقارناً. ويُشبه الناسخ بصيغته؛ لأنه نص قائم بنفسه، فلم يجز إلحاقه بأحدهما بعينه، بل وجب اعتباره في كل باب بنظيره، فقلنا : إذا كان دليل الخصوص مجهولاً أوجب جهالةً في الأول بحكمه إذا اعتبر بالاستثناء، وسقط في نفسه بصيغته إذا اعتبر بالناسخ، وحكمه قائم بصيغته، فصار الدليل مشتبهاً، فلم تبطله بالشك. وكذلك إذا كان المخصوص معلوماً؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون معلولاً، وعلى احتمال التعليل : يصيرُ مخصوصاً من الجملة، كأنه لم يدخل لا على سبيل المعارضة للنص، فوجب العمل به فيصير قدرُ ما تناوله النص مجهولاً، هذا على اعتبار صيغة النص. وعلى اعتبار حكمه : لا يصح التعليل؛ لأنه شبيه بالاستثناء، وهو عدمٌ، والعدمُ لا يُعلَّل، فدخلت الشبهة أيضاً، وقد عُرف موجباً : فلا يبطل.
As for al-Karkhi, he argued that when the evidence of specification is unknown, it necessitates [that the general remains effective?] in the remainder, because specification is like exception; it indicates that [the specified] did not enter under the general sentence, like exception. And when it is known, it may be that it has a cause, and this is apparent, because the evidence of specification is a text standing by itself, so its being caused is valid, and it is not known how much of the remainder becomes excepted, so it becomes like ignorance of the specified. Among these [examples] is what the two Shaykhs narrated from Abu Hurairah: The Messenger of Allah said: 'We are not inherited from; what we leave is charity.' Agreed upon. And among them is what al-Bukhari narrated from Zayd ibn Thabit that the Messenger of Allah dictated to me: 'And not equal are those who sit among the believers... and those who strive in the way of Allah,' then Ibn Umm Maktum came to him while he was dictating it to me, and said: By Allah, O Messenger of Allah, if I were able to strive I would strive, and he was blind. Then Allah revealed to His Messenger with his thigh on my thigh, and it became heavy until I feared that my thigh would break, then it was lifted from him, and Allah revealed: 'other than those with hardship.' And al-Tirmidhi and al-Nasa'i narrated it. And among them: from Abu Hurairah: When the Messenger of Allah died and Abu Bakr succeeded him, and some of the Arabs disbelieved, Umar ibn al-Khattab said to Abu Bakr: How can you fight people when the Messenger of Allah said: 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say: La ilaha illallah, and whoever says La ilaha illallah, his wealth and life are protected from me except by its right, and his reckoning is with Allah'? Abu Bakr said: By Allah, I will fight whoever separates between prayer and zakat, for zakat is the due right of wealth. By Allah, if they withhold from me a young she-goat that they used to give to the Messenger of Allah, I would fight them for withholding it. Umar said: By Allah, I saw that Allah had expanded Abu Bakr's chest for fighting, and I knew it was the truth. Narrated by the group. And the argument of the second view: That the evidence of specification, if it is unknown, is as we said, and if it is known, the general remains operative over the remainder, because the evidence of specification is like exception, as we said, so it does not affect the remainder, because exception does not admit causation, and likewise this. And the argument of the other view: That when the evidence of specification was independent by itself, so that if it were delayed it would be abrogating, it would fall by itself if it were unknown, because the unknown is not suitable as evidence, unlike exception because it is a description dependent on the first, so it causes ignorance in it, and this is independent, opposing the first. And the proof of what we have said is that the evidence of specification resembles exception in its ruling, because we said it indicates that it did not enter the sentence; do you not see that it is only concurrent? And it resembles the abrogator in its wording, because it is a text standing by itself, so it was not permissible to attach it to one of them exclusively, but it must be considered in every chapter according to its equivalent. So we say: When the evidence of specification is unknown, it necessitates ignorance in the first by its ruling when considered as exception, and it falls in itself by its wording when considered as abrogator, and its ruling stands by its wording, so the evidence becomes ambiguous, so it is not invalidated by doubt. Similarly, when the specified is known, because it may have a cause, and on the probability of causation, it becomes specified from the sentence, as if it did not enter, not by way of opposing the text, so it is obliged to act by it, so the extent covered by the text becomes unknown. This is on the consideration of the wording of the text. And on the consideration of its ruling, causation is not valid because it is like exception, and it is a non-existence, and non-existence is not caused, so doubt also enters, and it is known to be effective, so it is not invalidated.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi usul discussion on the effect of jahala (ignorance/unknown status) of dalil al-khusus (evidence of specification) on the generality of a text. The author presents three views and then gives his preferred position. First, he recounts the view of al-Karkhi: if the specification evidence is unknown (majhul), it creates ignorance in the remainder of the general text, because it is like an exception (istithna')—it indicates that the specified portion never fell under the general ruling. If the evidence is known (ma'lum), it might have a cause ('illa), making it like an independent text, but then it is unknown how much of the remainder is excepted, leading to ignorance of the specified. The author then gives three hadith examples of specification: (1) the hadith that prophets' estates are not inherited (specifying the general inheritance verses), (2) the verse on those who sit and those who strive, specified by the exception of the blind, (3) the hadith of Abu Bakr insisting on fighting those who withhold zakat, specifying the general command to fight until people say the shahada. After these examples, he presents the second view: if the specification evidence is unknown, it is as before; if known, the general remains operative in the remainder because the evidence is like exception and does not admit causation. The third view: because the evidence is independent, if it were delayed it would be abrogating (naskh); if unknown, it falls by itself. The author's own view (دليل ما قلنا) reconciles these: dalil al-khusus resembles exception in its ruling (it indicates non-inclusion and must be concurrent) and resembles the abrogator in its wording (it is an independent text). Therefore, it cannot be reduced to either alone; each aspect must be considered. When the evidence is unknown, it creates ignorance in the general by its ruling-as-exception, and it falls in itself by its wording-as-naskh, so the evidence is ambiguous—but not invalidated by doubt. When the specified is known (i.e., the evidence is known), it may have a cause, and on that probability it becomes specified from the sentence without opposing the text, so the text's scope becomes unknown. On the ruling side, causation is invalid because it resembles exception (a non-existence), so doubt enters but the known effective ruling is not invalidated. This nuanced position shows the Hanafi method of balancing textual and rational considerations, especially the careful treatment of jahala and its effect on 'amm.

Key Terms
دليل الخصوص (dalil al-khusus): The evidence that specifies a part of a general text, removing it from the general ruling.
جهالة (jahala): Ignorance or unknown status of the specification evidence, leading to uncertainty in the scope of the general text.
استثناء (istithna'): Exception; a grammatical tool that excludes part of a statement, which the specification evidence is likened to in ruling.
ناسخ (nasikh): Abrogator; a later text that supersedes an earlier one, which the specification evidence is likened to in wording because it is an independent text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view, why is a known specification evidence (dalil al-khusus) not treated solely as an exception?
A) Because it is always unknown.
B) Because it resembles an abrogator in wording and exception in ruling.
C) Because it is a non-existence.
D) Because it must be concurrent.

Key Takeaway
The specification evidence's dual nature (like exception in ruling, like abrogator in wording) means that ignorance (jahala) creates ambiguity in the general text but does not invalidate its operativity.

The Nature of Dalil al-Khusus: Comparison with Istithna and Naskh
Analyzing the legal nature of evidence of specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi view on the nature of Dalil al-Khusus, compare it to both exception and abrogation, and apply the analysis to sales examples.

Core Idea
Dalil al-Khusus combines features of exception (in ruling) and abrogator (in form); it cannot be reduced to either alone.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the nature of evidence of specification (Dalil al-Khusus) is critical for interpreting general texts. This passage presents a dispute: is it like an exception, an abrogator, or a hybrid? The author (from al-Bazdawi) argues for a nuanced view that balances both similarities, and illustrates with practical fiqh branches from sales law. By working through this lesson, you will learn to analyze such proofs and apply the correct framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
First, the passage presents two opposing views: one treats Dalil al-Khusus purely like an exception, the other like an abrogator. The author then gives his own view (ما قلنا) that it shares features of both.
The first view says specifier does not affect remainder; second says it falls if unknown.
2
Analyze the author's evidence
The author argues that the specifier resembles an exception in its ruling (it clarifies non-entry) and resembles an abrogator in its form (it is an independent text). Therefore it cannot be attached exclusively to either.
Concurrency vs independence are key distinguishing features.
3
Apply to unknown specifier scenario
When the specifier is unknown, it causes uncertainty in the general (like an exception) and falls by itself (like an abrogator). The overall evidence becomes ambiguous but is not invalidated by doubt.
Consideration of both aspects prevents total invalidation.
4
Examine the fiqh examples
The passage gives examples from sales: combining valid and invalid items (free+slave, living+dead, vinegar+wine) makes the contract invalid because the valid item alone was not intended. But if one perishes after a valid contract, the sale remains for the other. This illustrates the specifier's effect.
The examples show how the principle operates in practice.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of the dispute: under the pure exception view, a known specifier does not affect remainder; under pure abrogator view, it would override part of the general. The author's view balances both, leading to the rulings in the sales examples.
The difference is not merely theoretical; it changes fiqh outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه القول الثاني: أن دليل الخصوص إذا كان مجهولاً: فعلى ما قلنا، وإذا كان معلوماً : بقي العام موجباً في الباقي؛ لأن دليل الخصوص بمنزلة الاستثناء، على ما قلنا فلا يؤثر في الباقي ؛ لأن الاستثناء لا يحتمل التعليل، فكذلك هذا.
ووجه القول الآخر: أن دليل الخصوص لمَّا كان مستقلاً بنفسه، حتى لو تراخى كان ناسخاً سقط بنفسه إذا كان مجهولاً؛ لأن المجهول لا يصلح دليلاً، بخلاف الاستثناء؛ لأنه وصف قائم بالأول، فأوجب جهالةً فيه، وهذا قائم بنفسه معارض للأول.
ودليل ما قلنا أن دليل الخصوص يُشبه الاستثناء بحكمه ؛ لما قلنا إنه : يُبين أنه لم يدخل في الجملة، ألا يُرى أنه لا يكون إلا مقارناً.
وويُشبه الناسخ بصيغته ؛ لأنه نص قائم بنفسه، فلم يجز إلحاقه بأحدهما بعينه، بل وجب اعتباره في كل باب بنظيره، فقلنا : إذا كان دليل الخصوص مجهولاً أوجب جهالةً في الأول بحكمه إذا اعتبر بالاستثناء، وسقط في نفسه بصيغته إذا اعتبر بالناسخ، وحكمه قائم بصيغته، فصار الدليل مشتبهاً، فلم تبطله بالشك.
وكذلك إذا كان المخصوص معلوماً ؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون معلولاً، وعلى احتمال التعليل : يصيرُ مخصوصاً من الجملة، كأنه لم يدخل لا على سبيل المعارضة للنص، فوجب العمل به فيصير قدرُ ما تناوله النص مجهولاً، هذا على اعتبار صيغة النص.
وعلى اعتبار حكمه : لا يصح التعليل؛ لأنه شبيه بالاستثناء، وهو عدمٌ، والعدمُ لا يُعلَّل، فدخلت الشبهة أيضاً ، وقد عُرف موجباً : فلا يبطل بالاحتمال.
وهذا بخلاف الناسخ إذا ورد في بعض ما تناوله النص معلوماً، فإن الحكم فيما بقي لا يتغير ؛ لاحتمال التعليل ؛ لأن الناسخ إنما يعمل على طريق المعارضة للنص، لا على تبين أنه لم يدخل تحت الصيغة، فتصير العلة معارضةً للنص.
وأما ها هنا : فإن التعليل يقع على ما وُضع له دليل الخصوص، وهو أن لا يدخل تحت الجملة، فلا يصير معارضاً للنص، فإذا ثبت الاحتمال، فلم يخرج عن الدلالة بالشك: صار الدليل مشكوكاً بأصله، فأشبه دليل القياس فاستقام أن يعارضه القياس.
بخلاف ما ثبت بخبر الواحد؛ لأنه يقين بأصله، فلم يصلح أن يعارضه القياس.
ونظير هذه الجملة من الفروع أن البيع إذا أُضيف إلى حُرِّ وعبدِ بثمن معلوم واحد، أو إلى حي وميت، أو خل وخمر: فهو باطل؛ لأن أحدهما لم يدخل تحت العقد، فبقي الآخر وحده بحصته ابتداءً.
وكذلك إذا قال بعتُ منك هذين العبدين بألف درهم، إلا هذا بحصته من الألف، فصارت هذه الجملة نظير الاستثناء.
وإذا باع عبدين فمات أحدهما قبل التسليم، أو استحق، أو وُجد أحدهما مكاتباً أو مدبَّراً صح البيع في الباقي ؛ لأن الآخر داخل في البيع.
وكذلك المدبر والمكاتبُ يدخلان في البيع، وإنما امتنع الحكم؛ صيانة لحقهما، فصار الآخر باقياً في العقد بحصته، فصار هذا من قسم
And the justification of the second opinion: That evidence of specification, if unknown, is as we said, and if known, the general remains effective in the remainder because the evidence of specification is like an exception, as we said, so it does not affect the remainder because an exception does not admit causation, so likewise this.
And the justification of the other opinion: That the evidence of specification, since it is independent in itself, to the point that if it were delayed it would be an abrogator, falls by itself if it is unknown, because the unknown is not suitable as evidence, unlike the exception because it is a quality subsisting in the first, so it necessitates uncertainty in it, and this (specifier) is independent in itself opposing the first.
And the evidence for what we said: that the evidence of specification resembles the exception in its ruling, because we said that it clarifies that (the specified) did not enter into the general statement; do you not see that it is only concurrent (with the general)?
And it resembles the abrogator in its form, because it is a text subsisting by itself, so it was not permissible to attach it exclusively to one of them, but rather it must be considered in each chapter according to its analogue. So we said: If the evidence of specification is unknown, it necessitates uncertainty in the first (general) by its ruling when considered by the exception, and it falls in itself by its form when considered by the abrogator, and its ruling subsists by its form, so the evidence becomes ambiguous, and it is not invalidated by doubt.
And likewise if the specified is known, because it is possible that it is caused (by an illa), and on the possibility of causation, it becomes specified from the general, as if it had not entered, not by way of opposing the text, so it is necessary to act upon it, and thus the extent of what the text covers becomes unknown; this is considering the form of the text.
And considering its ruling: causation is not valid, because it is similar to the exception, and it is non-existence, and non-existence is not caused, so doubt also enters, and it is known as effective, so it is not invalidated by probability.
And this is unlike the abrogator if it occurs in part of what the text covers, known, for the ruling in the remainder does not change due to the possibility of causation; because the abrogator operates by way of opposing the text, not by way of clarifying that it did not enter under the wording, so the cause becomes opposing the text.
But here: causation occurs upon what the evidence of specification was placed for, namely that (the specified) does not enter under the general, so it does not become opposing the text. So when probability is established, it does not exit from signification due to doubt: the evidence becomes doubtful in its origin, so it resembles evidence by analogy, and thus analogy can oppose it.
Unlike what is established by a solitary report, because it is certain in its origin, so analogy is not suitable to oppose it.
And a parallel to this whole discussion from the branches: that if a sale is concluded involving a free person and a slave for a single known price, or involving a living being and a dead one, or vinegar and wine, it is invalid, because one of them did not enter under the contract, so the other remains alone with its share from the beginning.
And likewise if one says, 'I sold you these two slaves for one thousand dirhams, except this one for its share of the thousand,' then this becomes parallel to the exception.
And if he sold two slaves and one of them died before delivery, or was found to be legally claimed, or one of them was found to be a mukatab or mudabbar, the sale is valid for the remaining one, because the other entered into the sale.
And likewise the mudabbar and mukatab enter into the sale, but the ruling is prevented to safeguard their rights, so the other remains in the contract with its share, thus this falls under the category of...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding the legal nature of 'دليل الخصوص' (evidence of specification) — a text that specifies a general ruling. The passage begins by stating the second opinion (القول الثاني): when the specifier is unknown, the ruling is as previously stated; when known, the general remains effective in the remainder because the specifier is like an exception (استثناء), which does not admit causation (تعليل). Then the other opinion (القول الآخر) argues that the specifier is independent and, if delayed, would be an abrogator (ناسخ), so if unknown it falls by itself; unlike an exception, which is a quality of the first text. The author then presents his own evidence (دليل ما قلنا): the specifier resembles an exception in its ruling (it clarifies non-entry) and resembles an abrogator in its form (it is an independent text). Therefore, when the specifier is unknown, it causes uncertainty in the general (like an exception) and falls in itself (like an abrogator), making the overall evidence ambiguous but not invalidated by doubt. When the specified is known, the author explains that causation is possible, so the general's extent becomes unknown from the perspective of the text's wording; but from the perspective of its ruling, causation is invalid because the specifier is like non-existence (عدم) which cannot be a cause. This differs from an abrogator (ناسخ) which works by opposing the text, so the cause opposes the text. The passage then analogizes: the specifier, when doubtful in origin, resembles qiyas (analogical evidence), so qiyas can oppose it, unlike a solitary report (خبر الواحد) which is certain in origin. Finally, the passage gives fiqh examples (فروع) from sales: a sale combining a free person and a slave, or a living being and a dead, or vinegar and wine, is invalid because only one item is validly sold; likewise an explicit exception in a sale of two slaves for a price; but if one slave dies before delivery or is found defective, the sale of the remaining one is valid because the other was validly included in the contract. These examples illustrate the principle that a specifier, like an exception, removes the specified from the general, but does not affect the remainder.

Key Terms
دليل الخصوص (dalil al-khusus): Evidence that specifies a general ruling, indicating that a particular item or category was not intended by the general text.
الاستثناء (al-istithna): An exception that removes part of the general statement, operating as a qualifier and not independent.
الناسخ (al-nasikh): An abrogator that overrules a previous ruling, operating as an independent and opposing text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's view (ما قلنا), what happens when دليل الخصوص is unknown (مجهول)?
A) It is ignored and the general remains fully effective.
B) It causes uncertainty (جهالة) in the general ruling and itself falls due to its form.
C) It is treated as a valid abrogator and overrides the general.
D) It is considered like an exception and does not affect the remainder.

Key Takeaway
Dalil al-Khusus is neither pure exception nor pure abrogation; it has a hybrid nature requiring case-by-case analysis.

Types of General Terms: General in Form and Meaning
Definition and classification of general terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of general terms, state the Hanafi position on the minimum plural (three), and explain how the definite article shifts a plural to metaphorical genus.

Core Idea
A true general term is a plural form covering three or more; with the definite article, it becomes metaphorical for the genus.

This lesson unpacks the foundational classification of general terms (alfaz al-‘umum) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. We begin with the clearest type: a term that is general both in its linguistic form and its meaning. This covers plural nouns such as 'men' and 'women'. We will also address the minimum number required for a plural (three, as established by Imam Muhammad), and a subtle but crucial shift: when the definite article is attached to such a plural, it often becomes a metaphor for the genus rather than a reference to specific individuals. This distinction directly affects how we interpret general commands in the Quran and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of 'umum
The passage opens by dividing general expressions into two categories: (1) general in its form and meaning (عام بصيغته ومعناه) and (2) general in its meaning only, not its form (عام بمعناه دون صيغته). We focus on the first type here.
The second type is discussed later in the text.
2
Identify 'amm bi-sighatahu wa ma'anahu
This type is every plural form (جمع) such as رجال, نساء, مسلمون, etc. The form (sigha) indicates the plural, and the meaning (ma'na) encompasses all individuals of that category.
Examples given are all sound or broken plurals.
3
Determine the minimum plural
The Hanafi school holds that the minimum number for a true plural is three, as explicitly stated by Imam Muhammad in al-Siyar. This affects legal rulings: e.g., an oath to buy 'slaves' (عبيد) only triggers if three or more are bought.
This is a point of ikhtilaf with some other schools that consider two sufficient.
4
Analyse the effect of the definite article
When the definite article (لام المعرفة) enters a plural noun (e.g., النساء), it shifts from literal plural to metaphorical genus. This is because the definite article assumes prior mention (عهد), but plurals as a category often lack a specific prior reference, so it is used for the genus. The Quranic verse 'لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ مِنْ بَعْدُ' (al-Ahzab 33:52) illustrates this: 'women' here means the genus of women, not specific women.
This operation preserves the meaning of both the plural and the definite article.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ألفاظ العموم :قسمان عام بصيغته ،ومعناه وعام بمعناه، دون صيغته. أما العام بصيغته ومعناه : فهو صيغةً كلِّ جَمْع مثل: الرجال والنساءِ والمسلمين والمسلمات والمشركين والمشركات، وما أشبه ذلك. أما صيغته فموضوعةٌ للجمع، وأما معناه: فكذلك، وذلك شامل لكل ما ينطلق عليه اسم الجمع. وأدنى الجمع: ثلاثةٌ ، ذَكَرَ ذلك محمد رحمه الله صريحاً في كتاب «السَّير) في الأنفال، وفي غيرها، فصار هذا الاسم عاماً متناولاً جميع ما ينطلق عليه اسم الجمع ، غير أن الثلاثة أقل ما يتناوله، فصار أولى. ولهذا قلنا في رجل قال : إن اشتريتُ عبيداً فعليه كذا، أو : إن تزوجتُ نساء : إن ذلك يقع على الثلاثة فصاعداً؛ لما قلنا. والكلمة عامة لكل قسم تتناوله، وقد يصير هذا النوع مجازاً مجازاً عن الجنس إذا دخله لام المعرفة ؛ لأن لام المعرفة للعهد، ولا عهد في أقسام الجُموع، فجعل للجنس ؛ ليستقيم تعريفه، وفيه معنى الجمع أيضاً؛ لأن كل جنس يتضمَّن الجمعَ ، فكان فيه عمل بالوصفين، ولو حمل على حقيقته : بطل حكم اللام أصلاً ، فصار الجنس أولى، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ مِنْ بَعْدُ ﴾ الأحزاب / ٥٢ . وقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله فيمن قال: إن تزوجتُ النساء، أو : اشتريت
Chapter on the expressions of generality: Two types: general in its form and meaning, and general in its meaning only, not its form. As for general in its form and meaning: it is every plural form, such as men, women, Muslims (masc. pl.), Muslimas (fem. pl.), polytheists (masc. pl.), polytheist women (fem. pl.), and the like. As for its form: it is established for the plural, and as for its meaning: likewise, and that includes everything to which the name 'plural' applies. And the minimum of the plural is three. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned this explicitly in the book al-Siyar concerning the anfal and elsewhere. So this noun becomes general, encompassing everything to which the name 'plural' applies, but three is the least it includes, so it is more appropriate. For this reason, we said concerning a man who says: 'If I buy slaves, then he owes such,' or 'If I marry women,' that that applies to three and above, due to what we said. And the word is general for every category it encompasses. And this type may become a metaphor for the genus if the definite article enters it, because the definite article is for prior mention, and there is no prior mention in categories of plurals, so it is made for the genus in order to correct its definition. And in it there is also the meaning of plural, because every genus entails the plural. So there is operation of both descriptions. And if it were carried on its literal meaning, the ruling of the laam would be invalid altogether, so the genus is more appropriate. Allah Most High said: 'Women are not lawful for you after this' (al-Ahzab 33:52). And our companions, may Allah have mercy on them, said concerning one who says: 'If I marry women,' or 'If I buy...'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on general expressions (alfaz al-`umum) in usul al-fiqh. It defines two categories: (1) General in form and meaning (عام بصيغته ومعناه): these are plural forms like 'men', 'women', 'Muslims' etc. The form indicates the plural, and the meaning applies to all individuals. (2) General in meaning only (عام بمعناه دون صيغته): this is discussed later. The minimum number for a plural is three in Hanafi usul, contrary to some who say two. This is established from Imam Muhammad's work al-Siyar. An example: if a man says 'If I buy slaves' (عبيد) or 'If I marry women' (نساء), it applies to three or more, not just two. The passage also discusses the effect of the definite article (لام المعرفة) on plural nouns. When the definite article enters a plural like 'النساء', it becomes metaphorical for the genus (جنس) rather than indicating a specific group, because there is no prior 'ahd (reference). This is necessary to give meaning to the definite article. The verse from al-Ahzab (33:52) is cited as an example: 'النساء' here means the genus of women. Finally, the passage begins an example from Hanafi scholars about the oath 'If I marry women,' but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
عموم (umum): Linguistic or legal generality; encompassing all individuals of a category.
صيغة الجمع (sighat al-jam`): The linguistic form indicating plural (e.g., broken or sound plural patterns).
لام المعرفة (lam al-ma`rifa): The definite article 'al-' which when attached to a plural noun can indicate the genus via metaphor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul defines 'amm bi-sighatahu wa ma'anahu as plural forms covering three or more; when the definite article is attached, the term becomes metaphorical for the genus, a crucial distinction for interpreting general Quranic and hadith texts.

General by Wording and Meaning: Plural Nouns and the Definite Article
Defining the first type of general term and its implications when combined with the definite article.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a plural noun (jamʿ) as a general term (ʿāmm bi-ṣīghatihi wa-maʿnāhu) and explain how the definite article transforms it into a metaphor for the genus (majāz ʿan al-jins), applying the Hanafi rule to determine the scope of its application.

Core Idea
A plural noun with the definite article without prior covenant refers to the genus, not the literal plural, and applies to one or more.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's usul defines the first category of general terms: those that are general in both wording and meaning. Understanding this classification is foundational for interpreting commands and prohibitions in the Qur'an and Hadith. The discussion of the definite article's effect is a distinctive Hanafi position that affects how we understand verses and oaths.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of general terms
Identify that 'general by wording and meaning' refers to plural nouns like 'men' and 'women', while 'general by meaning only' refers to other forms like singulars with general meaning.
The passage focuses only on the first type.
2
Define the minimum of plural
Note the Hanafi position that the minimum for a plural is three, as stated by Imam Muhammad in al-Siyar.
This is a linguistic minimum, not a legal one.
3
Understand the effect of the definite article
Recognize that when the definite article 'al-' enters a plural without prior covenant, it becomes a metaphor for the genus, not the literal plural.
The literal meaning would conflict with the article's function.
4
Apply the rule to oaths and conditions
Follow the Hanafi ruling: such phrases apply to one or more, not necessarily three.
Example: 'If I marry the women' applies to one woman.
5
Examine the Quranic evidence
Analyze the verse 'لا يحل لك النساء من بعد' (al-Ahzab 33:52) to see how this principle is applied.
The verse prohibits the Prophet from marrying any woman.
