باب ألفاظ العموم :قسمان عام بصيغته ،ومعناه وعام بمعناه، دون صيغته. أما العام بصيغته ومعناه : فهو صيغةً كلِّ جَمْع مثل: الرجال والنساءِ والمسلمين والمسلمات والمشركين والمشركات، وما أشبه ذلك. أما صيغته فموضوعةٌ للجمع، وأما معناه: فكذلك، وذلك شامل لكل ما ينطلق عليه اسم الجمع. وأدنى الجمع: ثلاثةٌ ، ذَكَرَ ذلك محمد رحمه الله صريحاً في كتاب «السَّير) في الأنفال، وفي غيرها، فصار هذا الاسم عاماً متناولاً جميع ما ينطلق عليه اسم الجمع ، غير أن الثلاثة أقل ما يتناوله، فصار أولى. ولهذا قلنا في رجل قال : إن اشتريتُ عبيداً فعليه كذا، أو : إن تزوجتُ نساء : إن ذلك يقع على الثلاثة فصاعداً؛ لما قلنا. والكلمة عامة لكل قسم تتناوله، وقد يصير هذا النوع مجازاً عن الجنس إذا دخله لام المعرفة ؛ لأن لام المعرفة للعهد، ولا عهد في أقسام الجُموع، فجعل للجنس ؛ ليستقيم تعريفه، وفيه معنى الجمع أيضاً؛ لأن كل جنس يتضمَّن الجمعَ ، فكان فيه عمل بالوصفين، ولو حمل على حقيقته : بطل حكم اللام أصلاً ، فصار الجنس أولى، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ مِنْ بَعْدُ الأحزاب / ٥٢ . وقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله فيمن قال: إن تزوجتُ النساء، أو : اشتريت العبيد فامرأته طالق إن ذلك يقع على الواحد فصاعداً؛ لما قلنا إنه صار عبارة عن الجنس فسقطت حقيقة الجمع، واسم الجنس يقع على الواحد، على أنه كل الجنس. ألا يُرى أنه لولا غيره : لكان كلاً، فإن آدم صلاة الله عليه وحده كان كل الجنس للرجال وحواء رضي الله عنها وحدها كانت كل الجنس للنساء، فلا تسقط هذه الحقيقة بالمزاحمة فصار الواحد للجنس: مثل الثلاثة للجمع ، فكما كان اسم الجمع واقعاً على الثلاثة فصاعداً : كان اسم الجنس واقعاً على الواحد ، فصاعداً، وكان كمَن حلف لا يشرب الماء: أنه يقع على القليل على احتمال الكل.
Chapter of General Terms: Two types: general by its form and meaning, and general by its meaning not by its form. As for general by its form and meaning: it is in form every plural like: men, women, Muslims (pl.), Muslim women (pl.), polytheists (pl.), polytheist women (pl.), and the like. As for its form, it is placed for the plural, and as for its meaning, likewise, and that includes everything to which the name of plural applies. And the minimum of plural is three; Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned that explicitly in the book 'al-Siyar' in al-Anfal and elsewhere, so this name became general encompassing all to which the name of plural applies, except that three is the least it encompasses, so it became more appropriate. And because of this we said regarding a man who said: 'If I buy slaves, then upon him such and such,' or 'If I marry women,' that it applies to three or more, due to what we said. And the word is general for every division it encompasses, and this type may become a metaphor for the genus if the definite article enters it, because the definite article is for specification (al-ʿahd), and there is no specification in the plurals, so it is made for the genus to make its definition correct, and there is also the meaning of plural in it because every genus implies plural, so there is operation with both attributes, and if it were taken literally, the ruling of the article would be invalidated entirely, so the genus is more appropriate. Allah Most High said: 'No women are lawful for you after this' (al-Ahzab 33:52). And our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding one who said: 'If I marry women' or 'buy slaves,' his wife is divorced, that it applies to one or more, due to what we said because it became an expression for the genus, so the reality of plural fell away, and the name of genus applies to one, while being all the genus. Do you not see that without others, it would be all? For Adam (peace be upon him) alone was all the genus of men, and Eve (may Allah be pleased with her) alone was all the genus of women, so this reality is not negated by competition, so the one became for the genus like the three for the plural, just as the name of plural applied to three or more, so the name of genus applied to one or more, and it is like one who swore not to drink water: that it applies to a small amount with the probability of the whole.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines the first category of general terms (عام): one that is general in both wording (صيغة) and meaning (معنى). The author explains that such terms are plurals like رجال and نساء. The minimum of plural is three, as stated by Imam Muhammad in al-Siyar. Thus, if someone says 'I buy slaves' or 'I marry women,' it is understood as three or more. However, when the definite article (ال) enters a plural noun with no prior covenant, it becomes a metaphor (مجاز) for the genus, not the literal plural. The verse 'لا يحل لك النساء من بعد' is cited as evidence. The Hanafis rule that in such cases it applies to one or more, because the definite article makes it denote the genus, and the genus can be instantiated by a single individual, as Adam was the entire genus of men. Hence, the phrase 'the women' in an oath operates similarly to the genus, covering even a single woman.

Key Terms
عام بصيغته ومعناه (ʿāmm bi-ṣīghatihi wa-maʿnāhu): A term that is general in its morphological form (plural noun) and in its meaning (applying to multiple individuals).
الجمع (al-jamʿ): The plural form; the minimum number for a plural is three according to Imam Muhammad.
لام المعرفة (lām al-maʿrifah): The definite article 'al-' which indicates a specific known entity (al-ʿahd).
مجاز عن الجنس (majāz ʿan al-jins): A metaphorical use of a plural noun with the definite article to denote the genus rather than a specific plural.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The definite article transforms a plural noun into a metaphor for the genus, so the term applies to one or more, not necessarily three.

General in Meaning Only: Terms with Singular Form but Plural Meaning
Definition and examples of general-in-meaning terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify terms that are 'عام بمعناه دون صيغته' and distinguish between default rules (three or more) and special cases like طائفة, using Hanafi usul classification and textual criticism.

Core Idea
Words singular in form but plural in meaning are generally for three or more; طائفة is exceptional due to conflicting narrations.

In this passage, the author introduces the second category of general terms: those whose wording is singular but whose meaning is plural. You will learn the default rule for such words and examine a disputed example (طائفة) that requires careful textual criticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that 'عام بمعناه دون صيغته' refers to words that are singular in morphological form (ṣīghah) but denote a plural meaning. The default is that they apply to three or more.
Examples from the text: قوم (people), رهط (group), جماعة (community), طائفة (party/group).
2
Analyze the form-meaning relationship
Note that the form of these words is like proper nouns (e.g., 'Zayd' is singular), yet their meaning points to a collection. This distinguishes them from obvious plural forms like 'مؤمنون'.
The author compares the form of 'رهط' and 'قوم' to the singular names 'زيد' and 'عمرو'.
3
Apply the default minimum rule
For most words in this category, the minimum number is three or more, because the singular form is used for a plural concept that exceeds two.
This is the Hanafi default: 'اسم للثلاثة فصاعداً'.
4
Examine the exception: طائفة
The word طائفة is reported from Ibn Abbas to apply to one or more. However, the author flags this narration as weak (without a strong chain). A stronger narration from Ibn Abbas defines طائفة as 'العصبة' (a band), implying a group.
The verse referenced is Quran 9:122: ﴿فَلَوْلَا نَفَرَ مِن كُلِّ فرْقَةِ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ﴾.
5
Evaluate the riwayah
The author shows usul al-hadith awareness: the weak narration is cited but not relied upon. The stronger view aligns with the default rule.
This demonstrates how textual criticism ('ilm al-hadith) impacts usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العام بمعناه دون صيغته فأنواع: منها: ما هو فرد وضع للجمع ، مثلُ: الرَّهْط، والقوم، ونحو ذلك، ومثل : الطائفة، والجماعة. فصيغة رَهْطِ وقوم مثل: زيد وعمرو، ومعناهما: الجمع، ولما كان فرداً بصيغته جمعاً بمعناه : كان اسماً للثلاثة فصاعداً، إلا الطائفة : فإنها اسم للواحد، فصاعداً. كذلك قال ابن عباس(۱) رضي الله عنهما في قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَلَوْلَا نَفَرَ مِن كُلِّ فرْقَةِ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ : إنه يقع على الواحد فصاعداً: لم أقف عليه مسنداً عنه، وإنما قاله ابن جرير عنه بغير سند وأسند هو وابن أبي حاتم وابن المنذر عنه أنه قال: الطائفة : العُصبة. أخرجوا ذلك من طريق معاوية عن علي بن أبي طلحة عنه.
As for general in meaning without its form, there are types: including what is a singular word placed for the plural, like: ar-rahṭ, al-qawm, and the like, and like: aṭ-ṭāʾifah, al-jamāʿah. So the form of rahṭ and qawm is like Zayd and ʿAmr, but their meaning is the plural, and since it is singular in form plural in meaning, it is a name for three or more, except aṭ-ṭāʾifah: it is a name for one or more. Likewise said Ibn ʿAbbās (may Allah be pleased with them both) regarding the saying of Allah Most High: 'If only a party (ṭāʾifah) from every section of them had gone forth,' that it applies to one or more. I have not found it with a chain from him, and Ibn Jarīr narrated it from him without a chain, and he, Ibn Abī Ḥātim, and Ibn al-Mundhir narrated from him that he said: aṭ-ṭāʾifah is the group (al-ʿuṣbah). They extracted that via the route of Muʿāwiyah from ʿAlī ibn Abī Ṭalḥah from him.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage explains the second category of general terms: those that are general in meaning but not in form. Examples include words like قوم (people) and رهط (group), which are singular in their morphological form (like proper nouns) but denote a plural meaning. According to the author, these apply to three or more. However, طائفة (party/group) is an exception: according to Ibn Abbas, it applies even to one person. The author includes a critical note (footnote) questioning the authenticity of the narration from Ibn Abbas, noting that the chain is weak. The stronger narration from Ibn Abbas defines طائفة as 'العصبة' (a band/group), which typically implies more than one. So while the literal meaning of طائفة can include one, the established usage is for a group.

Key Terms
عام بمعناه دون صيغته (ʿāmm bi-maʿnāhu dūna ṣīghatihi): A term whose form (ṣīghah) is singular but its meaning denotes a plurality, making it general.
طائفة (ṭāʾifah): A word meaning 'party' or 'group'; according to Ibn Abbas, it applies to one or more, but stronger narrations define it as 'العصبة' (a band).
فرد (fard): A singular word, i.e., not in plural form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on the word 'طائفة' (party/group) regarding the minimum number it applies to?
A) It applies to three or more
B) It applies to one or more
C) It applies only to a group of ten
D) It is not a general term

Key Takeaway
Terms that are singular in form but plural in meaning are generally for three or more; the exception of طائفة is based on a weak narration, and the stronger view aligns with the rule.

Words General in Meaning but Not in Form: Raht, Qawm, Ta'ifa
Defines and exemplifies 'amm by meaning only with raht, qawm, ta'ifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify words that are 'amm bi ma'nāhu dūna sīghatih and classify their scope of generality according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A word can be general in meaning despite being singular in form; Hanafi usul recognizes this as a distinct type of 'amm.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the category of 'amm (general) includes words that are singular in form but denote a class of individuals. This lesson examines the definition and examples provided in the source text, focusing on words like raht, qawm, and ta'ifa. You will learn the linguistic reasoning behind their classification and the special case of ta'ifa based on a Quranic verse and Ibn Abbas's interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that the passage defines 'amm bi ma'nāhu dūna sīghatih: words that are general by meaning but not by form.
This is a subset of 'amm distinct from words that are general by form (e.g., al-jam' al-muḥallā bi al-lām).
2
Distinguish form and meaning
Analyze the examples: raht and qawm are singular in form (like Zayd) but plural in meaning (three or more). Note that the singular form does not limit the meaning to one.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
Verify that raht and qawm apply to three or more persons because of their root usage.
The passage states they are 'name for three or more'.
4
Analyze the exception of ta'ifa
Understand that ta'ifa is treated differently: it can mean one or more. Identify the reason given: it is a singular adjective that became a generic noun with the feminine ta, and Ibn Abbas's tafsir of Quran 9:122 supports this.
This distinguishes ta'ifa from raht and qawm.
5
Evaluate the evidence
Examine the Quranic verse (9:122) and Ibn Abbas's interpretation. Note that the Hanafi school adopts this broader scope based on this evidence.
This is a dalil naqli that supports the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العام بمعناه دون صيغته فأنواع: منها: ما هو فرد وضع للجمع ، مثلُ: الرَّهْط، والقوم، ونحو ذلك، ومثل : الطائفة، والجماعة. فصيغة رَهْطِ وقوم مثل: زيد وعمرو، ومعناهما: الجمع، ولما كان فرداً بصيغته جمعاً بمعناه : كان اسماً للثلاثة فصاعداً، إلا الطائفة : فإنها اسم للواحد، فصاعداً. كذلك قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما في قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَلَوْلَا نَفَرَ مِن كُلِّ فرْقَةِ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ﴾ : إنه يقع على الواحد، فصاعداً؛ لأنه نعت فرد صار جنساً بعلامة الجماعة.
As for the general (al-‘amm) by its meaning without its form, it has types: among them is what is a singular word placed for the plural, such as 'raht' (group), 'qawm' (people), and the like, and such as 'ta'ifa' (party/group) and 'jama'ah' (assembly). So the form of 'raht' and 'qawm' is like Zayd and 'Amr (singular), and their meaning is plural; and since it is singular in form and plural in meaning, it is a name for three or more, except 'ta'ifa': it is a name for one or more. Thus said Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) regarding the saying of Allah Most High: 'If only a party from every group among them had gone forth' (Quran 9:122): it applies to one or more, because it is a singular attribute that became a generic term with the sign of the collective (the feminine ta).
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines a category of 'amm (general): words that are general by meaning but not by their singular form. The author lists examples: raht (group), qawm (people), ta'ifa (party), jama'ah (assembly). The first two are singular words that denote three or more, like a collective noun. The exception is ta'ifa, which according to Ibn Abbas can apply to one person or more, citing the Quranic verse (9:122). Ibn Abbas argues that the word is a singular adjective that became a noun indicating the genus, and the feminine 'ta' is a sign of the group, but it does not necessitate plurality. Thus ta'ifa can be one person. This is a Hanafi position derived from Ibn Abbas's tafsir.

Key Terms
العام بمعناه دون صيغته (al-‘amm bi ma‘nāhu dūna sīghatihi): A word that is singular in form but denotes a general/plural meaning.
الطائفة (al-ṭā'ifa): A group; in this context, can refer to one or more persons based on Ibn Abbas's interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) raht
B) qawm
C) ta'ifa
D) jamā'ah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, words that are general in meaning but singular in form are considered 'amm, and ta'ifa can indicate one or more persons based on Ibn Abbas's interpretation.

The General Word 'Man' and Hanafi Dispute in Manumission
Applies the general word 'man' to a manumission scenario with Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the usul principle that generates the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the combination of 'man' and 'min', and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf for manumission.

Core Idea
When 'man' meets 'min', generality meets partitivity — and the true meaning of tab'id limits the scope.

The word 'man' (who) is a general word in Arabic, but its scope can be affected by accompanying particles. In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute: when a master says to his agent, 'Man shi'ta min 'abidi u'tiqhu' (whomever you wish from my slaves, free him), can the agent free all slaves or must he leave one? Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, and their reasoning reveals a deeper usul principle about the interplay of generality and partitivity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position: Abu Hanifa's ruling
Identify what Abu Hanifa says: the agent may free all except one slave. Understand his reasoning that the combination of 'man' (general) and 'min' (partitive) limits the scope.
This is the more restrictive view.
2
Identify the opposing view: Sahibayn's position
State the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the agent may free all slaves. Understand their reasoning that 'min' is merely for specification (tamyeez), not partitivity.
This is the expansive view.
3
Analyze the proof of Sahibayn
Examine their dalil: 'man' is general by default, and the Quranic verse 'fajtanibu al-rijsa mina al-awthani' uses 'min' to specify the type, not to limit quantity. Therefore, 'min' here only distinguishes the group.
They analogize to classical Quranic usage.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine his dalil: the master combined 'man' (general) with 'min' (partitive). If 'min' were mere specification, it would be superfluous. To give effect to both, the command applies to a part generally, so the agent must leave out one.
He prioritizes the literal partitive sense.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, one slave remains unfreed; according to Sahibayn, all are freed. This shows how a small nuance in language can change a legal outcome.
This is the real-world impact of the usul difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : كلمة: مَن، وهي تحتمل العموم والخصوص، قال الله تعالى: وَمِنْهُم مَّن يَسْتَمِعُونَ إِلَيْكَ ..... وَمِنْهُم مَّن يَنظُرُ إِلَيْكَ (يونس). وأصلها العموم، قال النبيُّ صلى الله عليه و سلم: «من دخل دار أبي سفيان: فهو آمن». وقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله فيمن قال لعبيده مَن شاء من عبيدي العتق : فهو حر، فشاؤوا جميعاً : عتقوا. فأما إذا قال: مَن شئت من عبيدي عتقه : فأعتقه: فقد قال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : للمأمور أن يُعتقهم جميعاً ؛ لأن كلمة من عامة وكلمة من التمييز عبيده من غيرهم، مثل قوله :تعالى ﴿فَاجْتَنِبُوا الرِّجْسَ مِنَ الْأَوْثَنِ ﴾ (الحج / 30). وقال أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه : له أن يُعتقهم إلا واحداً منهم؛ لأن المولى جَمَعَ بين كلمة العموم والتبعيض، فصار الأمر متناولاً بعضاً عاماً، وإذا قصر عن الكل بواحد : كان عملاً بهما ، وهذا حقيقة التبعيض.
And among that is the word 'man' (who), which admits generality and specificity. Allah Most High said: 'And among them are those who listen to you... and among them are those who look at you' (Quran 10:?). Its origin is generality. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan is safe.' And our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding one who said to his slaves: 'Whoever among my slaves wishes manumission, he is free,' and they all wished it: they all become free. But if he said: 'Whomever you wish from my slaves to manumit, manumit him,' then Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: The agent may manumit all of them, because the word 'man' is general and the word 'min' is for distinguishing his slaves from others, like His saying: 'So avoid the filth of idols' (Quran 22:30). And Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) said: He may manumit all except one of them, because the master combined the word of generality with partitivity, so the command applies to a part generally, and if it falls short of all by one, that is acting upon both, and this is the reality of partitivity.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage discusses the word 'man' as a general word. First, it establishes that 'man' can be general or specific, but its default is generality, supported by Quran and hadith. Then it presents a scenario: if a master says 'whoever among my slaves wishes manumission, he is free' and all wish it, all are freed by consensus. The dispute arises when the master says to an agent: 'Whomever you wish from my slaves to manumit, manumit him.' Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue the agent can free all because 'man' is general and the preposition 'min' here is merely for specification (tamyeez) distinguishing the slaves from others, just as in the Quranic verse 'avoid the filth of idols' where 'min' specifies the type of filth. Abu Hanifa, however, says the agent can free all except one. His reasoning is that the master combined a general word ('man') with a partitive ('min' indicating 'from among'), so the command is directed to a part in a general sense. Since the partitive inherently limits the scope, the agent cannot apply it to all; he must leave out one to give effect to both the generality (the word 'man' implying any) and the partitivity (the 'min' implying some). This, he argues, is the true meaning of partitivity. The ikhtilaf illustrates how usul principles (like the scope of 'amm combined with tab'id) produce fiqh differences even within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
من (man): A relative pronoun meaning 'who' or 'whoever'; used as a general conditional particle here.
تبعيض (tab'id): Partitivity; indicating a part of the whole, as in 'min' meaning 'some of'.
تمييز (tamyeez): Specification; distinguishing a class or type without implying partitivity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a master says to his agent 'man shi'ta min 'abidi u'tiqhu' (whomever you wish from my slaves, free him), how many can the agent manumit?
A) All of them
B) All except one
C) Only one
D) None

Key Takeaway
The word 'man' is inherently general, but its combination with the partitive 'min' creates an ikhtilaf in Hanafi usul: Abu Hanifa holds it restricts to all except one, while the Sahibayn maintain full generality.

The Word 'Mīn' and the Scope of 'Ta'ifa' in Hanafi Usul
Parsing the linguistic scope of 'طَائِفَة'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Hanafi usul principle that 'طَائِفَة' (party) can refer to one or more persons by parsing the Quranic verse 9:122 and applying the concept of signs of plurality to determine the scope of a word.

Core Idea
The word 'طَائِفَة' includes one person because a singular description becomes a genus by context.

In this lesson, we examine a classical Hanafi usul passage that analyzes the word 'مِن' in Quran 9:122. The focus is on the word 'طَائِفَة' (a party) and how it can apply to one or more individuals. This analysis reveals an important principle about how context and signs of plurality affect the scope of a word, which is fundamental to deriving rulings from texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse
Locate Quran 9:122 and note the phrase 'مِن كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ'. This verse is the basis of the analysis.
2
Analyze the word 'طَائِفَة'
Observe that 'طَائِفَة' is a singular noun describing an individual or a group. The author argues it applies to one and more.
3
Understand the contextual sign
The phrase 'مِن كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ' (from every group) provides a context of plurality. This sign of plurality transforms the singular description into a genus covering one or more.
The phrase 'نعت فرد صار جنساً بعلامة الجماعة' captures this.
4
Derive the usul principle
Conclude that a singular description can become general (a genus) when accompanied by a contextual sign of plurality. This principle applies to other texts for determining the scope of words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مِن كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ (التوبة / ١٢٢): إنه يقع على الواحد، فصاعداً؛ لأنه نعت فرد صار جنساً بعلامة الجماعة.
‘From every group of them, a party’ (Quran 9:122): it applies to one and more, because it is a description of an individual that became a genus by the sign of plurality.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
The author begins by analyzing the word ‘مِن’ (min) as used in the Quranic verse ‘مِن كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ’ (from every group of them, a party). He explains that the word ‘طَائِفَةٌ’ (party) applies to a single person or more. This is because the description of ‘طَائِفَة’ is given in the singular but is made into a genus (i.e., includes multiple individuals) by the sign of plurality in the context, meaning it can refer to one or many. This illustrates how the word ‘مِن’ in this construction indicates a partitive sense but also implies generality depending on context.

Key Terms
مِن (min): A preposition that can indicate the beginning of a part, origin, or cause; here used for partiality (تبعيض) with potential generality.
طَائِفَةٌ (ta'ifa): A group or party; can refer to one or more individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It refers only to a single person
B) It refers to two or more but not one
C) It can apply to one or more persons
D) It always means a large crowd

Key Takeaway
The word 'طَائِفَة' can refer to one person, because a singular description becomes a genus by a contextual sign of plurality.
